Definition of Khamr: Hanafi Specificity vs. General Intoxicant
Inter-madhhab dispute over the scope of 'khamr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khamr, explain the three proofs of the opposing schools, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Khamr in Hanafi law is exclusively grape wine; other intoxicants are treated distinctly.

This lesson examines a fundamental dispute between the Hanafi school and the other three Sunni schools over the meaning of the Arabic word 'khamr'. The resolution of this linguistic-legal question affects the definitiveness of the prohibition and the applicability of hadd punishments. We will trace the arguments from Al-Hidayah and its commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Locate the definition of khamr given in the first section: 'That which is from the juice of grapes when it becomes intoxicating.'
This is the well-known view among linguists and Hanafis.
2
Examine the opposing school's proofs
List the three dalil cited by 'some people' (the Three Imams and Zahiris): (1) Hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr', (2) Hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees', (3) Etymological derivation from 'mukhamarat al-aql'.
Note that the first two are ahad hadiths, considered zanni in transmission.
3
Analyze the Hanafi counter-arguments
Two main arguments: (1) Consensus of linguists that khamr is specific to grape wine; other intoxicants have distinct names. (2) The prohibition of khamr is qat'i (definitive), while the prohibition of non-khamr is zanni (conjectural).
The second argument is epistemological: extending the name would incorrectly elevate the certainty of other intoxicants' prohibition.
4
Refute the etymology argument
The Hanafi commentator argues that khamr is derived from 'takhammur' (strength), not from covering the intellect. Even if derived from covering, derivation does not force the term to apply to everything with that attribute, as shown by 'najm' (Pleiades) from 'nujum' (appearance).
This refutation uses a linguistic principle: derivation does not determine scope of reference.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: for Hanafis, drinking grape wine incurs hadd with qat'i proof; other intoxicants incur hadd via qiyas (zanni). Also impacts rules of impurity and sale.
The difference is not in the final prohibition (both are haram), but in the level of certainty and the derivation of the ruling.

فریق مخالف کی تیسری دلیل کا جواب
وانما سمي خمرا لتخمره لا لمخامرته العقل على ان ما ذكرتم لا ينافي كون الاسم خاصا فيه فان النجم مشتق من النجوم وهو الظهور ثم هو اسم خاص للنجم المعروف لا لكل ما ظهر ولهذا كثير النظير ترجمہ اور خمر کا نام خمر رکھا گیا ہے اس کی قوت کی وجہ سے نہ کہ اس کے عقل کو چھپانے کی وجہ سے علاوہ اس بات کے جو تم نے ذکر کیا ہے وہ نام کے مخصوص ہونے کے منافی نہیں خمر کے اندر اس لئے کہ مجھم مشتق ہے نجوم سے جو ظہور کے معنی میں ہے پھر مجھ ایک معروف ستارہ کا نام ہے نہ کہ ہر اس چیز کا جو کہ ظاہر ہو اور اس کی نظیر بہت ہیں۔
تشریح یہ فریق مخالف کی تیسری دلیل کا جواب ہے کہ آپ نے فرمایا کہ خمر مخامرت سے مشتق ہے ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ تخمر سے ماخوذ ہے جس کے معنی شدت وقوت کے ہیں اور شدت وقوت کے معنی صرف انگوری شراب میں ہیں کیونکہ سب سے قوی اور مقوی ہے لہذا استدلال باطل ہو گیا اور اگر چلئے آپ کی بات تسلیم بھی کر لی جائے کہ خمر مخامرة العقل ہی سے مشتق ہے تب بھی آپ کا استدلال تام نہیں ہوگا، جیسے نجم نجوم سے مشتق ہے کہ جس کے معنی ظہور کے ہیں اس کے باوجود بھی ہرشی ظا ہر کو نجم نہیں کہا جا تا بلکہ یہ صرف ثریا کا نام ہے۔ اسی طرح قارورہ قرار سے مشتق ہے اس کے باوجود ہر اس چیز کو قارورہ نہیں کہا جاتا جس میں کوئی چیز قرار پکڑے بلکہ شیشی اور بوتل کو قارورہ کہا جاتا ہے۔ تو اس سے یہ قاعدہ نکلا کہ کسی چیز کا ایسے لفظ سے مشتق ہونا جس میں کوئی عام معنی ہوں اس بات کی دلیل نہیں ہوتی کہ وہ لفظ تمام چیزوں کا نام بھی ہو۔ اس طرح اگر مر کو مخامرة العقل سے مشتق مان لیا جائے پھر بھی یہ بات ثابت نہ ہوگی کہ تخت صرف انگوری
شراب کا نام نہیں ہے۔
Book of Beverages. ... As for khamr, speech about it is in ten places. One of them is the clarification of its essence, and it is that which is from the juice of grapes when it becomes intoxicating, and this is according to us, and it is well-known among linguists and scholars. ... Some people said it is a name for every intoxicant due to his saying (peace be upon him): Every intoxicant is khamr, and his saying: Khamr comes from these two trees, pointing to the grapevine and the date palm, and because it is derived from covering the intellect, and that is present in every intoxicant. ... And we have that it is a specific name by the consensus of linguists in what we mentioned, and for this reason its usage is well-known for it and for other than it is other, and because the prohibition of khamr is definitive, and the prohibition of non-khamr is conjectural. ... Rather, it was named khamr for its strength, not for covering the intellect, and besides, what you mentioned does not negate that the name is specific for it, for an-najm (star) is derived from an-nujum (appearance), and then it is a specific name for the well-known star, not for everything that appears. And to this there are many parallels.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the definition of khamr (wine). The Hanafi school, represented by Al-Hidayah and its commentator, maintains that khamr is specifically the unboiled juice of grapes when it becomes intoxicating. This is stated as the well-known view among linguists and scholars. The opposing view, attributed to 'some people' (the Three Imams—Abu Hanifa's own students? Actually, here 'some people' refers to the other three Sunni schools and the Zahiris), argues that khamr is a name for every intoxicant, based on three proofs: (1) The hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr', (2) Another hadith that khamr comes from two trees (grapevine and date palm), implying that date-based drink is also called khamr, and (3) Etymological derivation: khamr comes from mukhamara (covering) of the intellect, a characteristic found in all intoxicants. The Hanafi response (ولنا) presents two counter-arguments: (1) Linguists agree that khamr is specific to grape wine; other intoxicants have distinct names like sakar, naqi', etc. (2) The prohibition of khamr is definitive (qat'i) based on clear texts, while the prohibition of other intoxicants is conjectural (zanni) based on analogy or weaker evidence; extending the name khamr would incorrectly elevate their prohibition to definitiveness. Then the commentator refutes the opposing derivation argument by clarifying that khamr is actually derived from takhammur (strength/fermentation), not from covering the intellect. And even if it were derived from covering, derivation does not prove that a word applies to all things with that meaning, as shown by examples like najm (star) from zuhur (appearance) yet it only refers to the Pleiades, or qarura (bottle) from qarr (settling) yet it only refers to a glass bottle. Therefore, the Hanafi position that khamr is specific to grape wine stands.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Specifically the juice of grapes (mā' al-'inab) when it becomes intoxicating.
مسكر (muskir): Any intoxicating drink.
مخامرة العقل (mukhāmarat al-'aql): Covering/overpowering the intellect, a proposed derivation for the word khamr.
تخمر (takhammur): Strength, fermentation; the Hanafi proposed derivation of khamr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the essence (māhiyya) of khamr?
A) Any intoxicating drink, regardless of source.
B) The juice of grapes when it becomes intoxicating.
C) Any drink derived from dates or grapes.
D) Any liquid that covers the intellect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of khamr is linguistically specific to grape wine, and the hadd for other intoxicants rests on zanni proof, reflecting a careful distinction between linguistic and legal meanings.

Hanafi Definition of Khamr: Only Grape Wine
Hanafi refutation of opposing definitions of khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the definition of khamr and explain the linguistic, legal, and hadith-based proofs that restrict it to grape wine.

Core Idea
Khamr is only grape wine—its prohibition is definitive (qat'i), while other intoxicants are prohibited by analogy (zanni).

This passage presents the Hanafi school's response to the Shafi'i view that the term khamr includes all intoxicants. The Hanafis argue from linguistic consensus, the definitive nature of khamr's textual prohibition, and the derivation of the word from takhammur (fermentation) rather than mukhamara (covering the intellect). They also address the opposing hadith evidence, criticizing its reliability and reinterpreting its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly that for Hanafis, khamr is exclusively the intoxicating beverage made from grapes.
The opposite view (Shafi'i) holds that khamr includes all intoxicants.
2
Understand the linguistic consensus proof
The passage claims consensus of Arabic lexicographers that khamr is specific to grape wine. This is a key evidence.
This is a dalil naqli from language, considered strong by Hanafis.
3
Grasp the qat'i vs zanni distinction
The prohibition of khamr is based on clear, definitive texts (qat'i), while prohibition of other intoxicants is derived by analogy (zanni). If non-grape intoxicants were also called khamr, they would share the same definitive ruling without equal proof.
This shows the legal consequence of definition: hukm level differs.
4
Analyze the derivation argument
The word khamr is derived from takhammur (fermentation, strength), not from mukhamara (covering the intellect). The Hanafis argue that the former connection supports specificity to grape wine, whereas the latter (used by opponents) would allow generality.
Even if derived from mukhamara, a word can still be specific in usage, as with 'najm' (star).
5
Examine the response to opposing hadith
The first hadith ('kullu muskirin khamr') is criticized by hadith scholar Yahya ibn Ma'in as weak; the second hadith (where the Prophet shows substances) is interpreted as a statement of legal ruling (hukm) rather than a linguistic definition.
Thus both hadith are either unreliable or not applicable to definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا انه اسم خاص باطباق اهل اللغة فيما ذكرنا ولهذا اشتهر استعماله فيه وفي غيره غيره ولان حرمة خمرية قطعية وحرمة غيرها ظنية وانما سمي خمرا لتخمره لا لمخامرته العقل على ان ما ذكرتم لا ينافي كون الاسم خاصا فيه فان النجم مشتق من النجوم وهو الظهور ثم هو اسم خاص للنجم المعروف لا لكل ما ظهر ولهذا كثير النظير والحديث الاول طعن فيه يحيى بن معين والثانى اريد به بيان الحكم اذهو اللائق بمنصب الرسالة.
And for us: it is a specific name by consensus of the people of language concerning what we mentioned, and therefore its usage is well-known in it (grape wine) and in other than it, it is other. And because the prohibition of khamr is definitive (qat'i) and the prohibition of other than it is speculative (zanni). And it is named khamr because of its fermentation/strength (takhammur), not because it covers the intellect (mukhamara). However, what you mentioned does not negate the name being specific to it. For example, 'najm' is derived from 'nujum' meaning appearance, yet it is a specific name for the well-known star, not for everything that appears. And there are many similar examples. As for the first hadith, Yahya ibn Ma'in criticized it; the second hadith is intended to state the ruling, because that is befitting the station of prophethood.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school presents two proofs that khamr is specifically grape wine, not any intoxicant. First, linguistic consensus: the word khamr, by the agreement of lexicographers, is reserved for wine from grapes. Its usage is widely known for this, while other drinks have distinct names (e.g., sakar, naqi'). Second, the legal weight: the prohibition of khamr is qat'i (definitive, based on clear texts), while the prohibition of other intoxicants is zanni (speculative, based on analogy or weaker evidence). If non-grape intoxicants were also called khamr, their prohibition would be qat'i without proof, which is baseless. The Hanafis then answer the opponent's third argument—that khamr is derived from mukhamara (covering the intellect). They argue the derivation is from takhammur (strength), not mukhamara. Even if derivation from mukhamara is accepted, a word derived from a general meaning can still be specific in usage, as with 'najm' (star) derived from 'appearance' yet restricted to a particular celestial body. Many such examples exist. Regarding the opponent's first hadith ('every intoxicant is khamr'), the Hanafis note that the hadith critic Yahya ibn Ma'in declared it weak, so it cannot be used as proof. The second hadith (the Prophet showing substances saying 'this is khamr') is interpreted not as a linguistic definition but as a statement of legal ruling—that these are prohibited—which the Hanafis accept. Thus the Hanafi position that khamr is exclusively grape wine stands.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Specifically the intoxicating beverage from grapes, as per consensus of lexicographers.
تخمر (takhammur): Fermentation or strength, the deriving root for 'khamr' according to Hanafis.
مخامرة (mukhamara): Covering or veiling (the intellect), an alternative derivation for khamr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, why is khamr specifically grape wine?
A) Because only grape wine is intoxicating
B) Because of consensus of lexicographers and the definitive nature of its prohibition
C) Because all intoxicants are called khamr in the Quran
D) Because the Prophet defined it as such

Key Takeaway
Khamr is a specific term for grape wine, and its prohibition is definitive; other intoxicants are prohibited but not as khamr.

Threshold for Grape Juice to Become Khamr: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Definition of when a grape juice becomes khamr: dispute between imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on when grape juice becomes khamr, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the practical consequences of each threshold.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires foam to settle before juice becomes khamr; Sahibayn deem mere fermentation sufficient.

Intoxicating drinks are haram, but at what point does grape juice cross the line? This lesson explores a classical Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa insists on a clear visual sign — foam rising and settling — while his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argue that the mere onset of fermentation suffices. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our appreciation of how the Hanafi school balances definitive rulings (qat'i) with precaution (ihtiyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Recognize that the matn records Abu Hanifa's view, but the Sahibayn oppose it. Note who holds each view.
The matn is from al-Quduri, which follows Abu Hanifa.
2
Locate the Sahibayn's dalil
They argue that ishtidad (fermentation) alone establishes the name because the intoxicating effect (the 'illa) is present, and that effect causes corruption.
They do not require foam as a condition.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Boiling is only the beginning; completion is marked by foam appearing and settling, which distinguishes pure from impure. Shari'ah rulings are definitive, so they attach to the complete stage.
He uses analogies (hadd penalty, declaring a permitter an unbeliever, prohibition of sale) to show that key rulings require certainty.
4
Understand the ihtiyat (precaution)
Although Abu Hanifa's view is the position of the school, for the specific ruling of prohibition of drinking, some scholars adopt precaution and treat mere ishtidad as sufficient.
This is not contradictory; it is a practical approach to avoid risk.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a description of grape juice at a certain stage, determine the ruling according to each imam, and state which view is relied upon (mufta bihi) and where ihtiyat applies.
Practice helps internalize the difference.

والثاني في حد ثبوت هذا الاسم وهذا الذى ذكره فى الكتاب قول ابي حنيفة وعندهما اذا اشتد صار خمرا ولا يشترط القذف بالزبد لان الاسم يثبت به وكذا المعنى المحرم بالاشتداد وهو المؤثر في الفساد و لابي حنيفة ان الغليان بداية الشدة وكمالها بقذف الزبد وسكونه اذ به يتميز الصافى من الكدر واحكام الشرع قطعية فتناط بالنهاية كالخد واكفار المستحل وحرمة البيع وقيل يؤخذ في حرمة الشرب بمجرد الاشتداد احتياطا
And the second (discussion) is regarding the limit of the establishment of this name. And this which is mentioned in the book is the statement of Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong (ishtadda) it becomes khamr, and the throwing of foam is not a condition, because the name is established by it, and likewise the meaning that prohibits (the 'illa) is established by ishtidad, and it is what affects corruption. And for Abu Hanifa: that boiling is the beginning of strength, and its completion is by the throwing of foam and its settling, because by it the pure is distinguished from the impure, and the rulings of the Shari'ah are definitive, so they are attached to the completion (nihaayah), like the hadd (penalty) and declaring the one who permits it an unbeliever, and the prohibition of selling. And it is said that in the prohibition of drinking, precaution is taken with mere ishtidad.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about when grape juice becomes khamr (thus prohibited to drink, sell, etc.). Two positions are presented: First, the view of Abu Hanifa, also recorded in the matn (al-Quduri), requires both fermentation (ghalayan/ishtidad) and the throwing of foam (qadhf al-zabad). The juice must reach full bubbling and then settle with foam thrown off; only then is it legally khamr. Second, the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) considers mere ishtidad (fermentation/becoming strong) sufficient; foam is not a condition because the intoxicating effect (the 'illa of prohibition) is already present with ishtidad, and that 'illa is what causes corruption. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: boiling (ghalayan) is only the beginning of strength; full maturity is reached when foam appears and then settles. At that point, the clear liquid (safi) is distinguished from the impure (kadir). Since Shari'ah rulings are definitive (qat'iyya), they should be attached to the complete stage (nihaaya), not a partial stage. He gives analogies: the hadd punishment for drinking is not applied until the drink is definitely khamr; declaring a person who permits khamr an unbeliever requires certainty; the prohibition of selling khamr applies only when it is fully khamr. Finally, the text notes that some scholars recommend precaution: for the ruling of prohibition to drink, they consider mere ishtidad as sufficient, even though the name khamr might not be fully established. This is a practical approach (ihtiyat) to avoid any possibility of consuming alcohol.

Key Terms
اشتداد (ishtidad): The process of becoming strong/fermented; for Sahibayn, this alone is sufficient to call it khamr.
قذف بالزبد (qadhf bil-zabad): The throwing of foam; sign that fermentation has completed, required by Abu Hanifa.
غليان (ghalayan): Boiling/fermentation; the initial stage of becoming strong.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does grape juice become khamr?
A) When it begins to boil and ferment
B) When it throws foam and the foam settles, distinguishing pure from impure
C) As soon as it becomes intoxicating
D) When it is stored in a container

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines khamr by the complete fermentation stage (foam settling), but precaution recommends treating mere fermentation as prohibiting consumption.

Refuting the Argument that Every Intoxicant is Khamr
Refuting opposing proofs about khamr prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi refutation of the argument that every intoxicant is khamr, by distinguishing between hadith authenticity and legal interpretation.

Core Idea
A hadith can be weakened by a critic like Yahya ibn Ma'in, even if in Sahih Muslim.

The Hanafi school does not accept the hadith 'kullu muskir khamr' as authentic due to criticism by the renowned hadith scholar Yahya ibn Ma'in. Additionally, they interpret the second hadith not as a literal identification of khamr, but as a declaration of the legal ruling. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi method of critique and interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding intoxicants: they distinguish khamr (grape wine) from other intoxicants, with different legal consequences.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opposing school (likely Shafi'i) argues that the hadith 'kullu muskir khamr' proves every intoxicant is khamr, using also a second hadith where the Prophet identifies certain substances as khamr.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the two hadiths cited by the opponent: (1) 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and (2) a narration where the Prophet indicates that two specific substances produce khamr.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two parts: first, the first hadith is weak due to ta'n (criticism) by Yahya ibn Ma'in; second, the second hadith is not a factual identification but a declaration of the legal ruling (hukm), consistent with the Prophet's mission.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the Hanafi school does not apply the severe hadd punishment for khamr to all intoxicants, only to grape wine; other intoxicants incur ta'zir (discretionary punishment).

والحديث الاول طعن فيه يحيى بن معين والثانى اريد به بيان الحكم اذهو اللائق بمنصب الرسالة.
ترجمہ اور پہلی حدیث میں بیٹی بن معین نے طعن کیا ہے اور دوسری حدیث اس سے حکم کا بیان مراد ہے اس لئے کہ یہی منصب رسالت کے لائق ہے۔
تشریح یہ فریق مخالف کی اول اور دوسری دلیل کا جواب ہے۔
(۱ کل مسکر خمر سے استدلال صحیح نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ یحیی بن معین نے اس حدیث میں طعن کیا ہے۔ ( رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم یہ بات بیان کرنے کے لئے نہیں آئے کہ یہ ناک ہے، یہ کان ہے، یہ خمر ہے، کیونکہ یہ اشیاء تو لوگوں کو خود معلوم ہیں تو حدیث مذکور سے یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ یہ خمر ہے جو ان دونوں سے بنے بلکہ حکم شرعی کو بیان کرنا ہے کہ یہ دونوں حرام ہیں اور اس کے ہم بھی قائل ہیں۔ تنبیه یی بن معین ولادت ۱۵۸ وفات ۲۳۳ جرح و تعدیل کے بڑے زبردست امام ہیں تو یہ حدیث اگر چہ مسلم میں ہے اور علامہ زیلعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ طعن مجھے نہیں ملا مگر علامہ عینی نے اس پر کلام کیا ہے اور طعن ثابت کیا ہے یہ خمر کے بارے میں پہلی گفتگو ختم ہوئی۔
And the first hadith was criticized by Yahya ibn Ma'in, and the second is meant to state the ruling, as that befits the station of prophethood. Translation: Yahya ibn Ma'in criticized the first hadith, and the second hadith intends to express the ruling, because this is befitting the station of prophethood. Explanation: This is the response to the first and second arguments of the opposing party. (1) The argument 'every intoxicant is khamr' is not correct because Yahya ibn Ma'in criticized this hadith. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not come to say 'this is a nose, this is an ear, this is khamr', because these things are already known to people, so the mentioned hadith does not mean that this is khamr made from these two, but rather to express the legal ruling that both are prohibited, and we also believe that. Note: Yahya ibn Ma'in (born 158, died 233) is a great imam of jarh wa ta'dil. This hadith is in Sahih Muslim, and Allama Zayla'i said that this criticism (ta'n) did not reach me, but Allama 'Ayni discussed it and established the criticism. The first discussion about khamr ends here.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is the Hanafi response to the opposing school's first and second proofs that every intoxicant is khamr. The Hanafi commentator argues that the first hadith ("kullu muskir khamr") is weak because Imam Yahya ibn Ma'in, a renowned hadith critic, questioned its authenticity. Although the hadith appears in Sahih Muslim, later scholars like Allama 'Ayni affirmed the criticism. The second hadith is interpreted not as a literal identification of substances as khamr, but as a statement of the legal ruling (hukm) that these substances are prohibited. The Prophet ﷺ did not need to tell people what khamr is, as they already knew; rather, He declared the prohibition of intoxicants. Thus, both the opposing proofs are refuted: one is unreliable in transmission, the other is misapplied in meaning.

Key Terms
طعن (ta'n): Criticism of a hadith narrator's reliability, rendering the hadith weak or unreliable.
کل مسکر خمر (kullu muskir khamr): The hadith meaning 'every intoxicant is khamr'.
حکم شرعی (hukm shar'i): A legal ruling from the shari'ah, as opposed to a factual statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi commentator reject the first hadith (kullu muskir khamr)?
A) It is abrogated
B) It was criticized by Yahya ibn Ma'in
C) It contradicts the Quran
D) It is only about grape wine

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between khamr (grape wine) and other intoxicants based on hadith criticism and the proper interpretation of prophetic statements as legal rulings.

When Does Grape Juice Become Khamr? Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the definition of khamr, and state the mufta bihi position regarding the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Khamr is named by completion of fermentation; whether frothing is required distinguishes Abu Hanifa from his companions.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: at what point does grape juice become legally considered khamr? While all agree that khamr is haram and that intoxication is the effective cause, Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the threshold. The resolution of this ikhtilaf has practical implications for the hadd penalty, but not for the prohibition of drinking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Understand that the debate is about the point at which the name 'khamr' attaches to grape juice during fermentation. This matters for legal consequences (hadd, prohibition of sale, takfir).
The prohibition of drinking is not in dispute; it is unanimous after any fermentation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa requires both fermentation (ishtidad) and frothing (qadhf al-zabad). He argues that sharia rulings are attached to the completion (nihayah) of a process, not its beginning.
His qiyas: like hadd only applies after full theft, not mere attempt.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say mere fermentation suffices to call it khamr. They argue that intoxication is present, which is the effective cause of prohibition.
They do not require frothing as a separate condition.
4
Analyze the Qiyas Argument
Both sides use analogical reasoning. Abu Hanifa's asl is that sharia rulings hinge on ultimate completion (e.g., hadd for theft requires full taking). The Sahibayn's asl is that the effective cause (intoxication) is already present at fermentation.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear) in the text.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The preferred view (mukhtar) is that the disagreement is only about the hadd penalty; for prohibition of drinking, fermentation alone is sufficient (ittifaq). Abu Hafs al-Kabir chose the Sahibayn's view as more apparent.
Thus, in fatwa, one may adopt the stricter view for hadd or the easier view, but drinking becomes haram at fermentation.

The second [discussion] is about the limit of establishing this name. And this which the author mentioned in the book is the statement of Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong (ferments) it becomes khamr, and frothing is not a condition, because the name is established by it, and likewise the meaning that causes prohibition is established by fermentation, and it is what is effective in corruption. Translation: The second discussion is about the limit of establishing the name [khamr]. And what al-Quduri mentioned in the book is the statement of Abu Hanifa. According to the Sahibayn, when it becomes intense (ferments) it becomes khamr, and frothing is not a condition, because the name of khamr is established by that, and similarly the meaning that makes it prohibited is established by fermentation, and the prohibitive meaning is what is effective in corruption. Explanation: This is the second of ten topics: when is something called khamr? Imam Abu Hanifa said: when it boils and ferments and froths. According to the Sahibayn, even before frothing, mere fermentation makes it khamr. The Sahibayn say that fermentation produces intoxication, and that is the cause of prohibition, and intoxication is what is effective in corruption, so after fermentation it will be called khamr. Proof of Abu Hanifa: And for Abu Hanifa: boiling is the beginning of fermentation, and its completion is by frothing and its settling, because by it the clear is distinguished from the turbid. And the rulings of the Sharia are definitive, so they are attached to the ultimate state, like the hadd penalty, declaring the one who permits it an unbeliever, and the prohibition of its sale. And it is said that in the prohibition of drinking, mere fermentation is taken as a precaution. Translation: The proof of Abu Hanifa: boiling is the beginning of fermentation, and its completion is by frothing and its settling, because by frothing the clear is distinguished from the turbid. And the rulings of Sharia are definitive, so they are attached to the ultimate state, like the hadd penalty, declaring the one who considers it permissible an unbeliever, and the prohibition of its sale. Some mashayikh said that precaution requires that drinking be considered prohibited merely by fermentation. Abu Hafs al-Kabir chose the statement of the Sahibayn, and that is more apparent. And the preferred opinion is that this difference is only regarding the obligation of the hadd penalty; as for drinking, it is unanimously prohibited. (See: Sikat al-Nahr, vol. 2, p. 499)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage discusses the second of ten topics concerning khamr: the point at which grape juice becomes khamr legally. Abu Hanifa holds that khamr is only when the juice reaches the stage of intense fermentation with frothing (qadhf al-zabad). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that mere fermentation (ishtidad) suffices, even without frothing, because intoxication occurs and that is the effective cause of prohibition. Abu Hanifa argues that boiling (ghalayan) is the beginning, but full fermentation and settling (sukun) mark completion; Sharia rulings (like hadd, takfir of the mustahill, prohibition of sale) attach to the final complete state, analogous to how hadd applies only after full commission of theft or zina. This is a qiyas (analogy). Some later scholars (mashayikh) said caution requires prohibition upon mere fermentation. Abu Hafs al-Kabir preferred the Sahibayn view, and the preferred (mukhtar) opinion is that the dispute only pertains to the obligation of the hadd penalty; drinking is unanimously haram upon fermentation.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Intoxicating beverage made from grapes; its name is established when the juice reaches a certain stage of fermentation
اشتداد (ishtidad): The fermentation process where the juice becomes strong and intoxicating
قذف بالزبد (qadhf al-zabad): The frothing or foaming that occurs at the peak of fermentation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school regarding the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn?
A) The Sahibayn's view (only fermentation) is completely rejected.
B) Abu Hanifa's view (frothing required) is the only valid one.
C) The dispute is only about the obligation of the hadd penalty; drinking is unanimously haram upon fermentation.
D) The dispute is only about the prohibition of drinking; hadd is not affected.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school agrees that khamr is haram but differs on when the full legal label attaches; the operative opinion restricts the ikhtilaf to the hadd penalty.

The Inherent Prohibition of Khamr: More Than Intoxication
Proving khamr is haram in substance, not just because of intoxication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khamr is haram li-aynihi (inherently prohibited) and support it with the Quranic term 'rijs', mutawatir sunnah, ijma, and the 'small leads to large' argument.

Core Idea
Khamr is haram in itself, not just because it intoxicates — even a drop is forbidden.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi stance that the substance of khamr (wine) is inherently haram, regardless of quantity or intoxicating effect. The passage from Al-Hidayah presents a multi-layered proof: the Quran describes it as 'rijs' (filth), the sunnah prohibits it unanimously, and a unique characteristic of khamr is that small amounts invite larger consumption. This distinguishes it from other intoxicants where only the state of intoxication is prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author asserts that the substance (ayn) of khamr is haram, not conditioned on intoxication (ma'lul bi al-sukr) nor dependent on it. Understand that this is a strong claim: even a single drop that cannot intoxicate is haram.
This contrasts with other schools that may limit prohibition to intoxicating quantities.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof
The author cites that Allah called khamr 'rijs' (فَاجْتَنِبُوهُ), meaning filth. In Hanafi usul, 'rijs' denotes something inherently impure (najis al-ayn) and thus its substance is prohibited. Focus on the direct linguistic connection.
Rijs is not merely a description of effect but of inherent quality.
3
Examine the sunnah and ijma proofs
The author states that the Prophet ﷺ prohibited khamr through mutawatir ahadith, and there is consensus (ijma) among the companions and scholars. Recognize that mutawatir sunnah gives certainty similar to the Quran.
Ijma here refers to the consensus of the Ummah after the Prophet's prohibition.
4
Understand the 'small leads to large' argument
The author adds a secondary proof: a small amount of khamr calls to a large amount, increasing pleasure. This is a distinguishing characteristic not found in other edibles. It shows that even a little is dangerous and therefore prohibited.
This is a rational (ma'qul) argument reinforcing the naqli proofs.
5
Refute the opposing view
The author dismisses the view that only intoxication is haram, calling it disbelief (kufr) because it denies the Quran's explicit designation. Note the strong language: denying a clear Quranic ruling can lead to kufr.
The opposing view is not attributed to any recognized school; it is a deviant opinion.

والثالث ان عينها حرام غير معلول بالسكر ولا موقوف عليه ومن الناس من انكر حرمة عينها وقال ان السكر منها حرام لان به يحصل الفساد وهو الصد عن ذكر الله وهذا كفر لانه جحود الكتاب فانه سماه رجسا والرجس ما هو محرم العين وقد جاءت السنة متواترة ان النبى عليه السلام حرم الخمر وعليه انعقد الاجماع ولان قليله يدعو الى كثيره وهذا من خواص الخمر ولهذا تزداد لشاربه اللذة بالاستكثار منه ترجمہ اور تیسری گفتگو یہ ہے کہ عین خمر حرام ہے جو سکر کے ساتھ معلول نہیں اور نہ سکر پر موقوف ہے اور لوگوں میں سے کچھ ایے کیے جنہوں نے عین خمر کی حرمت کا انکار کیا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ اس کا سکر حرام ہے اس لئے کہ سکر ہی سے فساد حاصل ہوتا ہے اور وہ اللہ کے ذکر سے روکنا ہے اور یہ کفر ہے اس لئے کہ یہ کتاب کا انکار ہے اس لئے کہ کتاب اللہ نے اس کو رجس قرار دیا ہے اور رجس وہ ہے جس کا عین حرام ہو اور تو اتر کے طریقہ پر حدیث وارد ہوئی ہے کہ نبی علیہ السلام نے خمر کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور اس پر اجماع منعقد ہو گیا ہے اور اس لئے کہ اس ہے کا قلیل اس کے کثیر کی جانب داعی ہے اور یہ خمر کے خواص میں سے ہے اسی وجہ سے اس کے پانی پینے والے کے لئے زیادہ پینے سے لذت بڑھ جاتی ہے بخلاف تمام مطعومات گئے۔ تشریح یہ مواضع عشرہ میں سے تیسری جگہ ہے کہ خمر بعینہ حرام ہے سکر کی وجہ سے نہیں لہذاوہ قلیل جس کی وجہ سے سکر نہ ہو وہ بھی قطعی
The third [discussion] is that its substance (ayn) is prohibited, not conditioned on intoxication nor dependent on it. Among people are those who denied the prohibition of its substance and said that its intoxication is prohibited because corruption comes through it, and it is turning away from the remembrance of Allah, and this is disbelief because it is denial of the Book, for He called it rijs (filth), and rijs is that whose substance is prohibited. And the sunnah has come mutawatir that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and upon it consensus is concluded. And because a small amount of it calls to a large amount, and this is from the characteristics of khamr, for this reason its drinker's pleasure increases with consuming more. Translation: The third discussion is that the substance of khamr is haram, not conditioned on intoxication nor dependent on it. Some people denied the prohibition of its substance and said that its intoxication is what is haram, because corruption comes through intoxication, and it is turning away from the remembrance of Allah. And this is disbelief because it is denial of the Book, for Allah called it rijs, and rijs is that whose substance is prohibited. And the mutawatir sunnah has come that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and consensus (ijma) has been formed on it. And because a small amount of it invites to a large amount, and this is among the characteristics of khamr, for this reason the pleasure of its drinker increases with consuming more, unlike all other edibles. Explanation: This is the third of ten topics: that khamr is haram in itself, not because of intoxication, so even a small amount that does not intoxicate is also decisively [haram].
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
The third discussion establishes that khamr is inherently haram (haram li-aynihi), not merely because it causes intoxication. Some people (possibly Mu'tazila or others) argued that only the intoxication is haram, but the Hanafi commentator refutes this as disbelief (kufr) because it denies the Quranic designation of khamr as 'rijs' (filth), which implies its substance is impure and prohibited. The proof includes: 1) Quran calls it rijs, and rijs is something whose substance is haram (mahrum al-ayn). 2) The sunnah is mutawatir in prohibiting khamr. 3) There is ijma (consensus) on its prohibition. 4) A small amount leads to a large amount; this is a unique characteristic of khamr that distinguishes it from other edibles where a small amount does not necessarily invoke more. Therefore, even a small amount that does not intoxicate is also haram. This is a key distinction: the prohibition attaches to the substance itself, not to the contingent state of intoxication.

Key Terms
رجس (rijs): Filth; something impure and inherently prohibited
معلول بالسكر (ma'lul bi al-sukr): Conditioned or caused by intoxication; Hanafis reject this and say prohibition is intrinsic
عين (ayn): Substance, essence; the physical material itself

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What Quranic term does the text use to prove that khamr's substance is inherently prohibited?
A) Najis
B) Rijs
C) Khabith
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
Khamr (wine) is haram in its very substance (haram li-aynihi) based on the Quranic term rijs, mutawatir sunnah, ijma, and the unique quality that a small amount invites to a large amount.

When Does Grape Juice Become Khamr? Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on defining the onset of prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on when grape juice becomes khamr, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
The dispute on the onset of khamr affects the hadd penalty, not the prohibition of drinking.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on the precise moment when grape juice transforms into khamr (prohibited wine). While all agree that drinking khamr is haram, they differ on the defining sign of the transformation: is it intensive bubbling (ghilyan) or the appearance of froth (jhag)? This nuance impacts the application of the hadd penalty for drinking khamr, making it a key example of how subtle variations in 'illa (effective cause) lead to different rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: Abu Hanifa rules that grape juice becomes khamr at the stage of intensive bubbling (ghilyan), even without froth. The Sahibayn rule that it becomes khamr only when froth appears.
Both agree that drinking is haram once the drink intoxicates; the dispute is about the hadd penalty.
2
Identify the 'illa (effective cause)
Abu Hanifa considers the 'illa to be the intensive bubbling, which he argues is the start of the perfection of fermentation. The Sahibayn consider the 'illa to be the frothing, which is the clear sign of completion.
The dispute hinges on whether the 'illa is the beginning or the peak of the cause.
3
Analyze the reasoning based on Sharia's definitiveness
Abu Hanifa argues that Sharia rulings are definitive (qat'i) and must be attached to the perfection of the cause, not its inception. He draws analogies to theft and adultery, where penalties apply only upon full completion of the act.
The Sahibayn respond that frothing is the perfect sign, as it visibly separates pure from impure.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a drink bubbles intensively but has no froth, Abu Hanifa would apply the hadd penalty for drinking khamr, while the Sahibayn would not. However, both agree the drink is haram and sinful to consume.
The dispute does not affect the prohibition of drinking – that is unanimous.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
Later Hanafi authorities, including Abu Hafs al-Kabir, preferred the Sahibayn's view for the hadd penalty. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that the dispute is limited to the hadd obligation.
This represents a cautious approach: avoiding the application of harsh penalties where there is doubt.

كتاب الاشربة
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ ترجمہ اور ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جوش مارنا شدت کی ابتداء ہے اور کمال شدت جھاگ مارنے میں اور جوش کے ٹھہر جانے میں ہے اس لئے کہ جھاگ مارنے سے صاف مکدر سے ممتاز ہو جاتا ہے اور احکام شرع قطعی ہیں تو ان کا مدار نہایت پر ہوگا جیسے حد اور حلال سمجھنے والے کو کافر کہنا اور بیع کی حرمت۔
تشریح یہ امام صاحب کی دلیل ہے کہ شدت وسکر کی ہدایت و آغاز جوش مارنے سے ہے اور اس کا کمال جھاگ مارنے سے ہوتا ہے اور جوش کے ختم ہو جانے سے ہوتا ہے کیونکہ جھاگ مارنے کی وجہ سے اوپر کا نیچے اور نیچے کا اوپر ہو جاتا ہے جس کی وجہ سے وہ خمر جو صاف و شفاف اور ستھری ہے الگ ہو جائے گی اور جو گد لی ہے نیچے بیٹھ جائے گی ۔ خلاصہ کلام مدار جوش مارنے پر ہے اور اس کی ابتداء فقط غلیان ہے اور غلیان کی انتہاء جھاگ مارنا ہے تو احکام کا مدار نہایت پر رکھا جاتا ہے لہذا خمر کو خمر اسی وقت کہا جائے گا جبکہ وہ درجہ نہایت پر فائز ہو جائے نیز احکام شرعیہ قطعی ہوتے ہیں تو ان کا مدار ہر جگہ نہایت و کمال پر رکھا جاتا ہے جیسے سرقہ اور زنا میں ہے اسی طرح یہاں بھی حد کا وجوب اور اس کے مستحمل کو کافر کہنا اور اس کی بیع کا حرام ہونا ان سب چیزوں کا مدار کمال و نہایت پر رکھا جائے گا۔
بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ احتیاط کا تقاضہ شر کے اندر یہ ہے کہ محض اشتداد کی وجہ سے اس کو حرام کہ دیا جائے۔
ابو حفص ( الکبیر نے صاحبین کے قول کو اختیار کیا ہے اور یہی اظہر ہے اور مختار یہ ہے کہ یہ اختلاف صرف وجوب حد میں ہے رہا پینا وہ بالا تفاق حرام ہے سیکب الا نہر ص ۴۹۹ ج ۲)
Book of Drinks. Ashraf al-Bidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 14. Translation: And Abu Hanifa's argument is that bubbling is the beginning of intensity, and the perfection of intensity is in frothing and in the settling of the bubbling, because frothing separates the clear from the turbid, and the rulings of Sharia are definitive, so their basis will be on the utmost degree, like the hadd (punishment) and calling the one who deems it permissible a disbeliever, and the prohibition of sale. Explanation: This is Imam Sahib's argument: that the guidance and beginning of intensity and intoxication is from bubbling, and its perfection occurs from frothing and from the cessation of bubbling, because frothing causes the top to go bottom and bottom to go top, so that the wine which is clear, pure, and clean separates, and the muddy settles. Summary: The basis is on bubbling, and its beginning is only fermentation, and the end of fermentation is frothing, so rulings are based on the ultimate degree. Therefore, wine will be called wine only when it reaches the ultimate degree. Also, Sharia rulings are definitive, so they are based everywhere on the ultimate and perfect degree, like in theft and adultery. Similarly, here the obligation of hadd, calling its drinker a disbeliever, and the prohibition of its sale, all these are based on perfection and ultimate. Some mashayikh said that the requirement of caution regarding evil is to declare it prohibited merely due to intensity. Abu Hafs al-Kabir adopted the opinion of the two disciples (Sahibayn), and this is more apparent, and the chosen view is that this difference is only regarding the obligation of hadd; as for drinking, it is unanimously forbidden. (Sabb al-Anhar, p. 499, vol. 2)
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the precise moment when grape juice becomes khamr (wine) and thus prohibited. The dispute is about the 'illa (effective cause) of prohibition: Abu Hanifa holds that the ruling hinges on the intensive bubbling (ghilyan shadid) that indicates the fermentation has reached its peak, while the Sahibayn consider that the appearance of froth (jhag) is the decisive sign. The Urdu commentary explains that Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that Sharia rulings, being definitive (qat'i), are attached to the completion and perfection of the cause, not its beginning. Analogies from theft and adultery are drawn where penalties apply only upon full completion of the act. The commentator then notes that some scholars (mashayikh) prefer caution, declaring it haram at mere intensity. Finally, Abu Hafs al-Kabir prefers the Sahibayn's view, and the mufta bihi (relied opinion) is that the dispute only concerns the application of the hadd penalty; the prohibition of drinking itself is agreed upon by all.

Key Terms
غلیان (ghilyan): Intensive bubbling or fermentation of grape juice.
جھاگ (jhag): Froth that forms on the surface during severe fermentation.
خمر (khamr): Intoxicating drink from grapes; legally, any substance that causes intoxication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does grape juice become khamr (prohibited wine)?
A) When it becomes sweet without fermentation.
B) When intensive bubbling (ghilyan) occurs, even without froth.
C) Only when froth (jhag) appears on the surface.
D) When it intoxicates, regardless of fermentation.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on the onset of khamr centers on whether bubbling or frothing is the definitive sign, but all agree that drinking is haram once any sign of intoxication appears.

The Intrinsic Prohibition of Khamr: Proofs and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Refutation of those who say only intoxication is prohibited, not the substance itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that khamr is haram in any amount (intrinsic prohibition), cite the Quranic proof 'rijs', and explain why the Hanafi school does not extend this ruling to other intoxicants via qiyas.

Core Idea
Khamr is haram in itself (li-'aynihi), not because of intoxication — even a drop is prohibited.

This lesson delves into the third of ten topics in the chapter on drinks: the absolute prohibition of khamr (grape wine). The Hanafi school holds that its prohibition is intrinsic, not conditioned on intoxication. We will examine the primary proof — the Quranic term 'rijs' — and the Hanafi refutation of any attempt to limit the prohibition to intoxicating amounts. We'll also compare with the Shafi'i school, which extends the prohibition to all intoxicants via analogy, and understand the Hanafi argument that legal reasoning ('illa) applies to rulings, not to names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage establishes that khamr (grape wine) is inherently haram. Determine what 'haram' means here: prohibition of consumption, sale, purchase, etc. Note that the Hanafi school considers it najis (impure) as well.
The act under discussion is not a specific action but the substance itself.
2
Locate the primary dalil (Quran)
The Quran verse 5:90 calls khamr 'rijs'. Understand the linguistic meaning of rijs: an abomination that is impure and prohibited in its essence. This word indicates intrinsic prohibition, not conditional.
Quranic citation is implicit; the passage quotes 'rijs'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The term 'rijs' in the Quran is used for khamr directly. In Arabic, rijs denotes something filthy and forbidden in itself. Therefore, the prohibition attaches to the substance, not to a contingent property like intoxication. This is a definitive (qat'i) textual indication.
The Hanafi principle: a word like rijs gives a clear, unambiguous ruling.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (Shafi'i position)
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the prohibition of khamr is due to intoxication (sukr), so any intoxicant in any amount is haram. They use qiyas (analogy) based on the 'illa of intoxication.
The Shafi'i school extends the name 'khamr' to all intoxicants.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preference)
The Hanafi school rejects this analogy as 'far-fetched' because it contradicts the well-known Sunnah (which treats non-grape intoxicants differently regarding small amounts). Moreover, legal reasoning (ta'lil) applies to rulings (ahkam), not to names (asma). Since 'khamr' is a specific name, it cannot be extended by qiyas.
The small amount of other intoxicants is permissible unless it intoxicates.

تیسری بحث
والثالث ان عينها حرام غير معلول بالسكر ولا موقوف عليه ومن الناس من انكر حرمة عينها وقال ان السكر منها حرام لان به يحصل الفساد وهو الصد عن ذكر الله وهذا كفر لانه جحود الكتاب فانه سماه رجسا والرجس ما هو محرم العين وقد جاءت السنة متواترة ان النبى عليه السلام حرم الخمر وعليه انعقد الاجماع ولان قليله يدعو الى كثيره وهذا من خواص الخمر ولهذا تزداد لشاربه اللذة بالاستكثار منه
ترجمہ اور تیسری گفتگو یہ ہے کہ عین خمر حرام ہے جو سکر کے ساتھ معلول نہیں اور نہ سکر پر موقوف ہے اور لوگوں میں سے کچھ ایے کیے جنہوں نے عین خمر کی حرمت کا انکار کیا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ اس کا سکر حرام ہے اس لئے کہ سکر ہی سے فساد حاصل ہوتا ہے اور وہ اللہ کے ذکر سے روکنا ہے اور یہ کفر ہے اس لئے کہ یہ کتاب کا انکار ہے اس لئے کہ کتاب اللہ نے اس کو رجس قرار دیا ہے اور رجس وہ ہے جس کا عین حرام ہو اور تو اتر کے طریقہ پر حدیث وارد ہوئی ہے کہ نبی علیہ السلام نے خمر کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور اس پر اجماع منعقد ہو گیا ہے اور اس لئے کہ اس ہے کا قلیل اس کے کثیر کی جانب داعی ہے اور یہ خمر کے خواص میں سے ہے اسی وجہ سے اس کے پانی پینے والے کے لئے زیادہ پینے سے لذت بڑھ جاتی ہے بخلاف تمام مطعومات گئے۔ تشریح یہ مواضع عشرہ میں سے تیسری جگہ ہے کہ خمر بعینہ حرام ہے سکر کی وجہ سے نہیں لہذاوہ قلیل جس کی وجہ سے سکر نہ ہو وہ بھی قطعی

ہے، قدامہ بن مطعون اور بعض اہل شام سے منقول ہے کہ مین خمر حرام نہیں بلکہ اس کا نشہ حرام ہے اس لئے کہ فساد صرف نشہ سے ہوتا ہے فساد سے مراد اللہ کے ذکر سے روکنا ہے لیکن ان کا یہ قول کفر ہے کیونکہ اس میں کتاب اللہ کا انکار ہے کیونکہ کتاب میں اس کو اللہ نے رجس قرار دیا ہے اور رجس اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی حرمت عینی ہو پھر بطریق تواتر حدیث منقول ہے کہ آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے خمر کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور اسی پر امت کا اجماع منعقد ہو چکا ہے پھر شراب کے خواص میں سے یہ ہے کہ اس کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہے۔ اور یہی وجہ ہے کہ اس کے پینے والے کو زیادہ پینے سے زیادہ لذت آتی ہے تو شروع ہی سے داعی تقلیل پر کثیر کا حکم جاری کیا گیا اور اس کو حرام قرار دیا گیا اور خمر کے علاوہ دیگر مسکرات میں یہ بات نہیں ہے کہ قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہو یہاں مصنف کے مطعومات کہنے پر علماء نے اعتراض کیا ہے کہ ان کو سائر المسکر ات کہنا چاہئے تھا۔
خمر کی قلیل مقدار بھی حرام ہے
ثم هو غير معلول عند نا حتى لا يتعدى حكمه الى سائر المسكرات والشافعي يعديه اليها وهذا بعيد لانه خلاف السنة المشهورة وتعليل لتعدية الاسم والتعليل في الاحكام لا في الاسماء
ترجمہ پھر وہ (یعنی وہ نص جو حرمت خمر میں وارد ہے ) ہمارے نزدیک غیر معلول ہے یہاں تک کہ اس کا حکم تمام مسکرات کی جانب متعدی نہ ہوگا اور شافعی نے اس کے حکم کو تمام مسکرات کی جانب متعدی کیا ہے اور یہ قیاس بعید ہے اس لئے کہ یہ سنت مشہورہ کے خلاف ہے اور نام کے تعدیہ کے لئے تعلیل ہے حالانکہ تعلیل احکام میں ہوتی ہے نہ کہ اسماء میں ۔ تشریح خمر کا تقلیل حرام ہے جس کی حرمت نص قطعی سے ثابت ہے اور یہ حرمت معلول نہیں ہے کہ سکر ہوگا تو حرام ہے ورنہ نہیں جب یہ حرمت سرے سے معلول ہی نہیں تو اس پر دوسری مسکرات کو قیاس نہیں کر سکتے کہ ان کا قلیل بھی مثل خمر کے حرام ہے ، مگر امام شافعی تخمر کی حرمت کو معلول مانتے ہیں اور پھر اس حکم حرمت کو تمام مسکرات کی طرف متعدی کر کے فرماتے ہیں کہ ان کا بھی قلیل و کثیر برابر ہے لیکن امام شافعی کی یہ تعلیل درست نہیں ہے کیوں ( پہلی وجہ ) ان کی یہ تعلیل سنت مشہورہ کے خلاف ہے کہ خمر حرام لعینہ ہے اور باقی شرابوں کا سکر حرام ہے ( دوسری وجہ ) تعلیل احکام میں ہوتی ہے اسماء میں نہیں اور امام شافعی اس تعلیل سے غیر خمر کو مر کہنے کے درپے ہیں اور پھر تمام مسکرات پر خمر کا حکم جاری کرنے کے درپے ہیں بہر حال تعلیل احکام میں ہوگی اسماء اور لغات میں نہیں کیونکہ کوئی تعلق قیاس سے نہیں ہے ۔
Third Discussion. And the third: that its substance is prohibited, not conditioned upon intoxication nor dependent on it. Among people are those who denied the prohibition of its substance and said that its intoxication is prohibited because corruption occurs through it, and it is turning away from the remembrance of Allah. This is disbelief because it is denial of the Book, for He called it rijs (abomination), and rijs is that whose substance is prohibited. And the Sunnah has come mutawatir that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and consensus was formed upon it. And because its small amount calls to its large amount, and this is among the characteristics of khamr, and for this reason the drinker's pleasure increases with more consumption. Translation: And the third discussion is that the very substance of khamr is prohibited, not conditioned upon intoxication nor dependent on it. Among people there were some who denied the prohibition of its substance and said that its intoxication is prohibited because corruption is only obtained through intoxication, and it is turning away from the remembrance of Allah, and this is disbelief because it is denial of the Book, because the Book of Allah declared it rijs, and rijs is that whose substance is prohibited. And hadith came in multiple ways that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and consensus was formed upon it. And because its small amount calls to its large amount, and this is among the characteristics of khamr; for this reason, the drinker's pleasure increases with more consumption, unlike all other foods. Explanation: This is the third of the ten topics: that khamr is prohibited in itself, not because of intoxication; therefore, even a small amount that does not intoxicate is definitely [prohibited]. [PAGE_089] It is reported from Qudama ibn Ma'zun and some people of Syria that khamr is not prohibited, but its intoxication is prohibited, because corruption only occurs from intoxication; corruption means turning away from the remembrance of Allah. But this saying of theirs is disbelief because it involves denial of the Book, because Allah declared it rijs in the Book, and rijs is that whose prohibition is intrinsic. Then hadith is transmitted mutawatir that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and the consensus of the Ummah was formed upon it. Then among the characteristics of wine is that its small amount calls to its large amount. And this is why the drinker gets more pleasure from drinking more. So from the beginning, the ruling of large amount is applied to the small amount due to this call, and it is declared prohibited. And in other intoxicants besides khamr, this characteristic is not present that small calls to large. Here, scholars have objected to the author saying 'foods' (mut'imat); he should have said 'other intoxicants' (sa'ir al-muskirah). Even a small amount of khamr is prohibited. Then it is without 'illa (unconditional) according to us, so that its ruling does not extend to other intoxicants. Al-Shafi'i extends it to other intoxicants, and this is far-fetched because it contradicts the well-known Sunnah, and it is a ta'lil (reasoning) to extend the name, whereas ta'lil is in rulings, not in names. Translation: Then that (the text regarding prohibition of khamr) is without 'illa according to us, such that its ruling does not extend to all intoxicants. Al-Shafi'i extends its ruling to all intoxicants, and this is a far analogy because it contradicts the well-known Sunnah, and it is a reasoning to extend the name, whereas reasoning is in rulings, not in names. Explanation: The small amount of khamr is prohibited, its prohibition established by definitive text. This prohibition is not conditioned upon intoxication. Since it is not conditioned, we cannot analogize other intoxicants to it, saying that their small amount is also prohibited like khamr. But Imam al-Shafi'i considers the prohibition of khamr to be conditioned (on intoxication) and then extends this ruling of prohibition to all intoxicants, saying that their small and large amounts are equal. However, this reasoning of Imam al-Shafi'i is incorrect because (first reason) it contradicts the well-known Sunnah that khamr is prohibited for its own essence, while for other drinks, only intoxication is prohibited. (Second reason) Reasoning applies to rulings, not to names. Imam al-Shafi'i by this reasoning aims to call non-khamr as khamr and then apply the ruling of khamr to all intoxicants. Nevertheless, reasoning applies to rulings, not to names and languages, because there is no connection to analogy.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage constitutes the third of the ten topics (mawadi' ashara) in the book of drinks. It establishes that khamr (wine from grapes) is intrinsically haram, meaning its prohibition is not contingent upon intoxication (sukr). The author refutes those, like Qudama ibn Ma'zun, who claimed only the intoxicating amount is prohibited. This is declared disbelief (kufr) because it contradicts the Quranic description of khamr as 'rijs' (abomination), which implies inherent impurity and prohibition. Furthermore, the prohibition is proven by mutawatir Sunnah and ijma'. An additional rational proof is that a small amount of khamr calls to a large amount, which is a unique characteristic not found in other intoxicants. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who extends the prohibition of khamr to all intoxicants via analogy (qiyas). The Hanafi rebuttal is that the prohibition of khamr is not based on an 'illa (effective cause) that can be generalized; it is a specific ruling (ta'abbudi). Thus, the small amount of other intoxicants is not prohibited unless it causes intoxication. The reasoning used by al-Shafi'i is criticized as an attempt to extend the name 'khamr' to other substances, whereas legal reasoning applies to rulings, not to linguistic definitions.

Key Terms
رجس (rijs): Abomination; something impure and prohibited in its substance.
معلول (mu'allal): Conditioned upon an effective cause ('illa). Here, the prohibition is not mu'allal bi al-sukr (conditioned on intoxication).
تعلیل (ta'lil): The process of identifying the effective cause ('illa) for a ruling to extend it via analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a small amount of khamr (e.g., a drop) prohibited even if it does not intoxicate?
A) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said so without any reason.
B) Because it is najis (impure) and its small amount calls to large amount.
C) Intoxication is the only cause; without it, a small amount is halal.
D) Because it is measured by weight, not by effect.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is haram in itself, not because of intoxication; a small amount is prohibited due to its intrinsic impurity and unique property of inciting more consumption.

Khamr as Heavy Impurity
Declares khamr as heavy impurity like urine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khamr is najasah ghalizah and explain the practical consequence for taharah.

Core Idea
Khamr is physically impure — even a single drop is a heavy impurity.

This lesson establishes that khamr is not only forbidden (haram) but also intrinsically impure (najis). The Hanafi school classifies it as a heavy impurity (najasah ghalizah), like urine, based on the Quranic description of khamr as 'rijs'. Understanding this classification is crucial for the validity of prayer, as any contact with a heavy impurity above a certain amount invalidates salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State clearly that khamr is najasah ghalizah (heavy impurity).
This is the fourth of ten topics about khamr.
2
Compare with other impurities
Recall that Hanafi fiqh divides impurities into heavy (najasah ghalizah) and light (najasah khafifah).
Examples of heavy: blood, urine, feces. Light: dog saliva.
3
Determine the evidence
The source cites 'definitive proofs' (adillah qat'iyyah), specifically the Quranic term 'rijs'.
The Quran (5:90) calls khamr 'rijs', which Hanafis interpret as physical impurity.
4
Apply to practical case
If khamr contacts body or clothing, it must be washed with water. More than a dirham's area invalidates prayer.
A drop smaller than a dirham is excused but should still be removed.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider khamr not physically impure; only prohibited.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text

والرابع انها نجسة نجاسة غليظة كالبول لثبوتها بالدلائل القطعية على ما بيناه
ترجمہ اور چوتھی گفتگو یہ ہے کہ خمر نہیں ہے نجاست غلیظہ جیسے پیشاب اس کی نجاست کے ثابت ہونے کی وجہ سے دلائل قطعیہ سے اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تشریح یہ مواضع عشرہ میں سے چوتھا مقام ہے کہ خر پیشاب کے مثل نجاست غلیظہ ہے چونکہ نص قطعی نے اس کو جس قرار دیا ہے۔
And the fourth: that it is impure with heavy impurity like urine, because its impurity is established by definitive proofs, as we have explained. Translation: And the fourth discussion is that khamr is heavy impurity like urine, due to its impurity being established by definitive proofs according to the detail we have explained. Explanation: This is the fourth of the ten topics: that khamr is heavy impurity like urine, because the definitive text declared it rijs.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is the fourth of the ten topics (mawadi' ashara) concerning khamr. It states definitively that khamr is not only prohibited but also physically impure (najis). The impurity is classified as 'heavy impurity' (najasah ghalizah), equating it to urine. The basis for this is the Quranic description of khamr as 'rijs', which implies intrinsic impurity. The Urdu commentary clarifies that this ruling is established by definitive proofs (adillah qat'iyyah) and has been elaborated earlier. In Hanafi fiqh, heavy impurities require washing with water and are excused only in small amounts (a drop or less) for prayer validity, while light impurities (like dog saliva) have different rules. This classification has practical implications for taharah (purification).

Key Terms
نجاست غلیظہ (najasah ghalizah): Heavy impurity that requires washing with water and is excused only in very small quantity (less than a dirham).
رجس (rijs): Abomination; in fiqh, something intrinsically impure and prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the impurity of khamr classified?
A) Light impurity (najasah khafifah) like dog saliva.
B) Heavy impurity (najasah ghalizah) like urine.
C) Not impure at all; only prohibited.
D) Impure only when it intoxicates.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is classified as najasah ghalizah (heavy impurity) in the Hanafi school, meaning even a small amount is physically impure and must be removed for valid prayer.

The Absolute Prohibition of Khamr: Refuting the View that Only Intoxication is Haram
Refutes view that only intoxication is haram, proves khamr haram in any amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khamr is haram in any quantity and explain why even a small amount is haram using Quranic, hadith, consensus, and rational proofs.

Core Idea
Khamr is haram in any quantity because its prohibition is inherent (hurmah 'ayniyyah), not contingent on intoxication.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah, the author vigorously refutes a deviant opinion attributed to Qudama ibn Ma'dhun and some Syrians that only the intoxicating effect of wine is forbidden. He marshals the Quranic declaration of 'rijs', mutawatir hadith, ijma, and a unique property of wine—its small amount invites the large—to establish that the substance itself is absolutely haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deviant view and its refutation
Locate the claim that khamr is not haram, only its intoxication is haram. Note that the author declares this view as kufr because it denies the Quran.
The view contradicts the explicit Quranic text.
2
Analyze the primary proof from the Quran
Examine the verse (5:90) where khamr is called 'rijs'. Explain that 'rijs' implies inherent impurity and thus inherent prohibition (hurmah 'ayniyyah).
This is a qat'i dalil in thubut and dalalah.
3
Consider the secondary proofs
The passage cites mutawatir hadith that the Prophet prohibited khamr, and the ijma of the ummah. These reinforce the Quranic ruling.
Mutawatir hadith provides certainty similar to the Quran.
4
Understand the rational argument (talil)
The passage notes that a small amount of wine invites one to drink more, unlike other intoxicants. Therefore, the ruling of the large amount is extended to the small from the outset.
This is a form of qiyas based on the inherent quality of wine.
5
Note the terminological objection
Scholars objected to the author of al-Hidayah using 'ma'umat' (edibles) instead of 'other intoxicants' for non-khamr intoxicants.
This shows the precision required in fiqh terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الاشربة اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ ہے، قدامہ بن مطعون اور بعض اہل شام سے منقول ہے کہ مین خمر حرام نہیں بلکہ اس کا نشہ حرام ہے اس لئے کہ فساد صرف نشہ سے ہوتا ہے فساد سے مراد اللہ کے ذکر سے روکنا ہے لیکن ان کا یہ قول کفر ہے کیونکہ اس میں کتاب اللہ کا انکار ہے کیونکہ کتاب میں اس کو اللہ نے رجس قرار دیا ہے اور رجس اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی حرمت عینی ہو پھر بطریق تواتر حدیث منقول ہے کہ آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے خمر کو حرام قرار دیا ہے اور اسی پر امت کا اجماع منعقد ہو چکا ہے پھر شراب کے خواص میں سے یہ ہے کہ اس کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہے۔ اور یہی وجہ ہے کہ اس کے پینے والے کو زیادہ پینے سے زیادہ لذت آتی ہے تو شروع ہی سے داعی تقلیل پر کثیر کا حکم جاری کیا گیا اور اس کو حرام قرار دیا گیا اور خمر کے علاوہ دیگر مسکرات میں یہ بات نہیں ہے کہ قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہو یہاں مصنف کے مطعومات کہنے پر علماء نے اعتراض کیا ہے کہ ان کو سائر المسکر ات کہنا چاہئے تھا۔
The Book of Drinks. Ashraf al-Bidaya, Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah, volume 14. It is narrated from Qudama ibn Ma'dhun and some people of Sham that khamr is not haram, rather its intoxication is haram, because corruption only occurs from intoxication; by corruption is meant prevention from the remembrance of Allah. But this statement of theirs is kufr because it involves denial of the Book of Allah, since in the Book, Allah has declared it rijs, and rijs is that whose prohibition is inherent. Then, via mutawatir hadith, it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr, and the ummah's consensus has been established upon it. Then among the characteristics of wine is that its small amount invites to large amount. This is why the drinker gets more pleasure from drinking more, so from the beginning, the ruling of large amount is applied to the small amount due to the invitation, and it is declared haram. In other intoxicants besides khamr, this characteristic does not exist that the small invites to the large. Here, scholars have objected to the author's use of 'ma'umat' [declensions] saying that he should have said 'other intoxicants'.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the absolute prohibition of khamr (wine) in any quantity. The author begins by refuting a deviant view attributed to Qudama ibn Ma'dhun and some Syrians who claim that only intoxication is haram, not the substance itself. This view is declared kufr because it denies the Quranic text where Allah calls khamr 'rijs' (impurity/abomination), which implies inherent prohibition (hurmah 'ayniyyah). The proof is reinforced by mutawatir hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited khamr and by the consensus (ijma) of the ummah. Additionally, a rational argument (talil) is given: the small amount of khamr naturally leads to the large amount, as drinking even a little creates a desire for more, unlike other intoxicants. Therefore, the ruling (hukm) of khamr is that it is haram in all quantities, and this ruling is not based on the intoxicating effect but on the substance itself. The author also notes a terminological objection: the author of al-Hidayah used 'ma'umat' (edibles) when he should have said 'other intoxicants' (sa'ir al-muskirah).

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): The specific intoxicating drink from grapes; in the text it refers to the substance that is inherently haram.
رجس (rijs): Impurity or abomination; a Quranic term indicating inherent filth and prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school declare even a small amount of khamr haram according to this passage?
A) Because small amounts cause intoxication quickly.
B) Because the small amount naturally invites consumption of a large amount.
C) Because the Quran explicitly states that any amount is haram.
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned it in a specific hadith.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is haram in all quantities because its prohibition is inherent (hurmah 'ayniyyah), established by Quran, mutawatir hadith, ijma, and the rational principle that the small invites the large.

Why the Ruling of Khamr Does Not Extend to Other Intoxicants
Explains Hanafi position that ruling of khamr is not 'illah-based and does not extend to other intoxicants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school restricts the prohibition of khamr to grape-wine and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on 'illah.

Core Idea
Khamr's prohibition is substance-specific, not 'illah-based, so qiyas cannot extend it to other intoxicants.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether the ruling of khamr (grape-wine) applies to all intoxicants. The Hanafi position distinguishes khamr, which is haram in any amount, from other intoxicants, whose small amount is permissible if not intoxicating. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi method of restricting qiyas when the text is not 'illah-based.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Khamr's prohibition is ghayr ma'lul (not 'illah-based), so it is specific to the substance and does not extend to other intoxicants.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i considers intoxication as the common 'illah, thus all intoxicants have the same ruling as khamr (haram in any amount).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i school uses qiyas: khamr is prohibited because it intoxicates, other intoxicants also cause intoxication, so they are prohibited.
This assumes the 'illah is intoxication.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response gives two reasons: (1) it contradicts the well-known sunnah that treats khamr differently; (2) ta'lil (reasoning) applies to rulings (ahkam), not to names (asma'), so one cannot rename all intoxicants as khamr.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Hanafis: small amount of non-grape intoxicants is permissible (makruh) if not intoxicating. Shafi'is: any amount of any intoxicant is haram.
The practical difference affects daily consumption of low-alcohol beverages.

Source Text

خمر کی قلیل مقدار بھی حرام ہے ثم هو غير معلول عند نا حتى لا يتعدى حكمه الى سائر المسكرات والشافعي يعديه اليها وهذا بعيد لانه خلاف السنة المشهورة وتعليل لتعدية الاسم والتعليل في الاحكام لا في الاسماء ترجمہ پھر وہ (یعنی وہ نص جو حرمت خمر میں وارد ہے ) ہمارے نزدیک غیر معلول ہے یہاں تک کہ اس کا حکم تمام مسکرات کی جانب متعدی نہ ہوگا اور شافعی نے اس کے حکم کو تمام مسکرات کی جانب متعدی کیا ہے اور یہ قیاس بعید ہے اس لئے کہ یہ سنت مشہورہ کے خلاف ہے اور نام کے تعدیہ کے لئے تعلیل ہے حالانکہ تعلیل احکام میں ہوتی ہے نہ کہ اسماء میں ۔ تشریح خمر کا تقلیل حرام ہے جس کی حرمت نص قطعی سے ثابت ہے اور یہ حرمت معلول نہیں ہے کہ سکر ہوگا تو حرام ہے ورنہ نہیں جب یہ حرمت سرے سے معلول ہی نہیں تو اس پر دوسری مسکرات کو قیاس نہیں کر سکتے کہ ان کا قلیل بھی مثل خمر کے حرام ہے ، مگر امام شافعی تخمر کی حرمت کو معلول مانتے ہیں اور پھر اس حکم حرمت کو تمام مسکرات کی طرف متعدی کر کے فرماتے ہیں کہ ان کا بھی قلیل و کثیر برابر ہے لیکن امام شافعی کی یہ تعلیل درست نہیں ہے کیوں ( پہلی وجہ ) ان کی یہ تعلیل سنت مشہورہ کے خلاف ہے کہ خمر حرام لعینہ ہے اور باقی شرابوں کا سکر حرام ہے ( دوسری وجہ ) تعلیل احکام میں ہوتی ہے اسماء میں نہیں اور امام شافعی اس تعلیل سے غیر خمر کو مر کہنے کے درپے ہیں اور پھر تمام مسکرات پر خمر کا حکم جاری کرنے کے درپے ہیں بہر حال تعلیل احکام میں ہوگی اسماء اور لغات میں نہیں کیونکہ کوئی تعلق قیاس سے نہیں ہے ۔
Even a small amount of khamr is haram. Then, that (the text proving its prohibition) is not 'illah-based (ma'lul) according to us, so its ruling does not extend to other intoxicants. Al-Shafi'i extends it to them, and this is far-fetched because it contradicts the well-known sunnah and is a ta'lil to extend the name, while ta'lil is for rulings, not names. Translation: Then that (the text on prohibition of khamr) is not 'illah-based according to us, so its ruling does not extend to all intoxicants. Al-Shafi'i extends its ruling to all intoxicants, and this analogy is far-fetched because it contradicts the well-known sunnah and is a ta'lil to extend the name, while ta'lil is for rulings, not for names. Explanation: The small amount of khamr is haram; its prohibition is established by definitive text, and this prohibition is not 'illah-based such that if intoxication occurs it is haram otherwise not. Since this prohibition is not 'illah-based at all, we cannot analogize other intoxicants to it to say their small amount is also like khamr. But Imam al-Shafi'i considers khamr's prohibition to be 'illah-based and then extends its ruling to all intoxicants, saying that for them too small and large are equal. However, this ta'lil of Imam al-Shafi'i is not correct for two reasons: first, it contradicts the well-known sunnah that khamr is haram in itself while other drinks' intoxication is haram; second, ta'lil is for rulings, not for names. Imam al-Shafi'i by this ta'lil seeks to call non-khamr 'khamr' and then apply khamr's ruling to all intoxicants. In any case, ta'lil is for rulings, not for names and linguistic terms, because there is no connection to qiyas.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether the prohibition of khamr extends to other intoxicants. The Hanafi position is that the text prohibiting khamr is not 'illah-based (ghayr ma'lul), meaning the prohibition is attached to the specific substance (khamr) itself, not to the attribute of intoxication (sukr). Therefore, qiyas (analogy) cannot extend the ruling to other intoxicants like nabeedh or beer. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, considers intoxication as the effective cause ('illah), thereby extending the prohibition to all intoxicants, making their small and large amounts equally haram. The Hanafi response argues that this is far-fetched (ba'id) because: (1) it contradicts the well-known sunnah which distinguishes between khamr (haram li-'aynihi) and other intoxicants (only intoxicating amount is haram); (2) ta'lil (reasoning) applies to rulings (ahkam), not to names (asma'). Intoxicants are different legal categories, and one cannot simply rename all intoxicants as khamr. The passage emphasizes that Hanafis maintain a distinction between khamr (grape-wine) and other intoxicants, and that the prohibition of khamr in small amounts is due to its inherent property, not just intoxication.

Key Terms
معلول (ma'lul): A ruling that is based on an 'illah (effective cause); here, the prohibition of khamr is not 'illah-based.
تعلیل (ta'lil): The process of identifying an 'illah to extend a ruling via qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject extending the ruling of khamr to other intoxicants?
A) Because khamr is explicitly named in the Quran and others are not.
B) Because the prohibition of khamr is not 'illah-based, making qiyas invalid.
C) Because other intoxicants are halal in small amounts.
D) Because the 'illah of intoxication is not present in khamr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats khamr as a unique substance whose prohibition is not 'illah-based, so qiyas cannot extend it to other intoxicants.
