Takfir of the One Who Considers Khamr Halal
Ruling on takfir of the mustahill of khamr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that declaring khamr halal constitutes kufr and explain why it is based on denial of a definitive proof.

Core Idea
Declaring khamr halal is kufr because it rejects a ruling known by necessity.

This passage establishes a critical boundary in Islamic creed: anyone who claims that khamr (intoxicating drink) is permissible exits the fold of Islam. The reason is not the act of drinking itself, but the denial of a definitive text (dalil qat'i) that explicitly prohibits it. Understanding this distinction prevents misapplication of takfir while preserving the inviolability of clear divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? It is 'considering khamr halal' (istihlal). The ruling is that the person becomes a kafir (disbeliever).
This is not about drinking khamr; it is about the belief that it is permissible.
2
Locate the reason for the ruling
The passage states the reason: 'because he denies the definitive proof' (li-inkarihi al-dalil al-qat'i). The prohibition of khamr is established by clear Quranic verses and hadith that are universally accepted.
Denial of any hukm that is ma'lum min al-din bi al-darurah (known necessarily in the religion) causes kufr.
3
Understand the scope and conditions
Consider that this ruling applies only to a person who has knowledge of the prohibition and intentionally rejects it. Ignorance, coercion, or misinterpretation (if valid) may prevent takfir.
The Hanafi school is cautious in applying takfir; it requires clear evidence that the person knew the ruling and deliberately denied it.
4
Distinguish from related rulings
Note the difference between a Muslim who drinks khamr (commits a sin but remains Muslim) and one who declares it halal (commits kufr). The key is the rejection of a qat'i text.
This distinction is essential for avoiding erroneous takfir of sinners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پانچویں بحث كتاب الاشربة والخامس انه يكفر مستحلها لانكاره الدليل القطعى ترجمہ اور پانچویں گفتگو یہ ہے کہ اس کا حلال جاننے والا کا فرٹھہرایا جائے گا اس کے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے دلیل قطعی کا ۔ تشریح یہ پانچویں بحث ہے کہ جو خمر کو حلال سمجھے وہ کافر ہے کیونکہ وہ نص قطعی کا منکر ہے۔
Fifth discussion: And the fifth is that the one who considers it (khamr) halal is declared a kafir because he denies the definitive proof. Translation: And the fifth discussion is that the one who considers khamr halal will be declared a kafir because of his denial of the definitive proof. Explanation: This is the fifth discussion: whoever considers khamr halal is a kafir because he denies the definitive text.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage states a serious ruling: anyone who claims that khamr is halal (permissible) commits kufr (disbelief). The reason is that such a person denies a definitive proof (dalil qat'i) from the Quran and Sunnah that establishes the prohibition of khamr. Since the prohibition is known by necessity (ma'lum min al-din bi al-darurah), rejecting it places one outside the fold of Islam. However, this applies only if the person believes that their view is the true religion; ignorance or coercion may excuse. The fiqh principle is that any ruling established by qat'i evidence cannot be denied without exiting Islam.

Key Terms
مستحل (mustahill): One who considers something forbidden as permissible; here applied to khamr.
دلیل قطعی (dalīl qaṭ‘ī): A definitive evidence that is certain in transmission and meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because drinking khamr is a major sin.
B) Because he denies a definitive proof (dalil qat'i) of the prohibition.
C) Because he claims to be a prophet.
D) Because he encourages others to drink.

Key Takeaway
Declaring khamr halal is an act of kufr because it involves denying a definitive proof, not merely committing a sin.

The Prohibition of Benefiting from Khamr
States the prohibition of benefiting from khamr with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on benefiting from khamr and explain the two reasons for its prohibition, including the counterexample of dung.

Core Idea
Every form of benefit from khamr is haram because it is najis and wajib al-ijtinab.

This lesson covers the seventh discussion of a series on khamr. The ruling is absolute: any form of benefit from khamr is haram. The text provides two reasons: a general principle about najis and a specific obligation to avoid khamr. The commentary refines this by noting a counterexample (dung) that challenges the first reason, strengthening the second.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the explicit ruling: benefiting from khamr in any way is haram.
This includes drinking, feeding to animals, using as medicine, enema, applying to wounds, etc.
2
Analyze the two reasons
The text gives two reasons: (1) benefiting from najis is inherently haram, (2) khamr is wajib al-ijtinab, and benefiting from it entails proximity.
The second reason is specific to khamr, not general to all najis.
3
Examine the counterexample
The commentary notes that the first reason is broken by dung, which is najis but permissible to use as fertilizer. This shows the first reason alone is insufficient.
The second reason (wajib al-ijtinab) is the stronger basis for the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسابع حرمة الانتفاع بها لان الانتفاع بالنجس حرام ولانه واجب الاجتناب وفي الانتفاع به اقتراب
ترجمہ اور ساتویں گفتگو خمر سے انتفاع کا حرام ہونا ہے اور اسلئے کہ یہ واجب الاجتناب ہے اور اس سے نفع اٹھانے میں اس سے نزدیکی ہے۔
تشریح یہ ساتویں گفتگو ہے کہ ثمر سے نفع اٹھانا حرام ہے یعنی جانوروں کو پلانا دوا دارد کرنا حقنہ لین یا ذ کر کے سوراخ میں ڈالنا سب حرام ہیں کیونکہ خمر سے دوری ضروری ہے اور اس سے انتفاع میں اس سے قرب ہے، مگریہ تعلیل گوبر سے ٹوٹ جاتی ہے۔
And the seventh [discussion]: the prohibition of benefiting from it, because benefiting from najas is haram, and because it is wajib al-ijtinab (obligatory to avoid), and in benefiting from it there is proximity [to it]. Translation: And the seventh discussion is that benefiting from khamr is haram, and because it is obligatory to avoid, and in taking benefit from it there is proximity to it. Commentary: This is the seventh discussion: benefiting from khamr is haram, meaning feeding it to animals, using it as medicine, taking it as an enema, or inserting it into a wound—all are haram, because avoiding khamr is necessary and benefiting from it involves proximity to it. However, this reasoning is broken by [the case of] dung.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This lesson covers the seventh of the ten discussions (al-mawadi' al-'ashara) regarding khamr. The ruling is that benefiting from khamr in any way is haram. The text provides two reasons: (1) benefiting from anything najis (impure) is haram in itself, and (2) khamr is wajib al-ijtinab (obligatory to avoid), so any benefit from it entails proximity to it, which contradicts the obligation of avoidance. The author gives examples: feeding it to animals, using it as medicine, administering it as an enema, or applying it to a wound—all are haram. The commentary notes that this reasoning is refuted by the case of dung (which is najis but benefiting from it is permissible in certain circumstances, e.g., as fertilizer). This shows that the first reason alone is insufficient; the second reason (wajib al-ijtinab) is the stronger basis.

Key Terms
الانتفاع (al-intifa'): Taking benefit or using something for any purpose.
النجس (al-najis): Something inherently impure (najis al-ayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Using it as fuel
B) Using it as fertilizer
C) Using it as an enema
D) Using it to make vinegar

Key Takeaway
It is haram to benefit from khamr in any way because it is najis and wajib al-ijtinab; the prohibition extends beyond drinking.

The Effect of Cooking on Khamr: Prohibition vs. Hadd
Clarifies the effect of cooking on khamr's prohibition and hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cooked khamr and distinguish between the prohibition (haram) and the conditions for applying the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Cooking does not lift the prohibition of khamr, but it changes the condition for applying the hadd.

This lesson examines whether cooking khamr alters its legal status. The Hanafi school holds that cooking does not remove the inherent prohibition; it only prevents the prohibition from becoming established if done before fermentation. Once established, cooking cannot lift it. However, the hadd punishment for drinking cooked khamr requires actual intoxication, unlike raw khamr where a small amount suffices. This distinction hinges on the role of cooking as a man' (preventer) rather than a rafi' (remover).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cooking khamr (wine) that has already become intoxicating. The Hanafi ruling is that it remains haram (prohibited) to consume.
This is the core ruling: cooking does not make it halal.
2
Understand the legal effect of cooking
Cooking acts as a man' (preventer) of the prohibition's establishment, not a rafi' (remover) after establishment. Analogy: wiping over khuffs prevents the obligation of washing feet but does not remove the obligation after it is established.
This distinction is crucial: cooking before fermentation prevents prohibition; after fermentation, it does not lift it.
3
Recognize the effect on hadd
For cooked khamr, the hadd is only applied if the person becomes intoxicated. For raw khamr, even a small amount triggers the hadd because any quantity is considered likely to lead to intoxication.
The characteristic of raw khamr (small amount leading to intoxication) is lost after cooking.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some scholars (e.g., some Shafi'is) may hold that cooking completely removes the prohibition if all intoxicating property is gone. The Hanafi position is that it remains haram regardless.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتاسع ان الطبخ لا يؤثر فيها لانه للمنع من ثبوت الحرمة لا لرفعها بعد ثبوتها الا انه لا يحد فيه ما لم یسكرمـنـه عـلـى مـا قـالوا لان الحـد بـالـقـلـيـل فـي النـي خـاصة لما ذكرناه ولهذا قد طبخ
ترجمہ اور نویں گفتگو یہ ہے کہ پکا ناخمر میں مؤثر نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ پکانا ثبوت حرمت کو روکنے کے لئے ہے نہ کہ حرمت کو اٹھانے کے لئے حرمت کے ثبوت کے بعد مگر اس میں حد نہیں ماری جائے گی جب تک کہ اس سے سکر نہ ہو اس تفصیل کے مطابق جو مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے اس لئے سر قلیل کی وجہ سے حد خاص طریقہ پر کچی میں سے اس وجہ سے جو ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں اور یہ پکا دی گئی ہے۔
تشریح مواضع عشرہ میں سے یہ نویں جگہ ہے کہ اگر خر کو پکا دیا گیا تو اس کی حرمت برقرار رہے گی کیونکہ پکا تا مانع ثبوت حرمت ہے واقع حرمت ثانیہ نہیں ہے جیسا کہ موزہ مانع حدث ہے رافع حدث نہیں ہے۔ البتہ اتنا فرق ہے کہ پکنے بعد جب تک نشہ نہ ہو حد نہیں ماری جائے گی کیونکہ وہ خصومت تو کچھی کی تھی کہ اس کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب دائی ہوتا ہے لہذا قلیل سے حد ثابت ہو جائے گی پکنے کے بعد اس کی یہ کیفیت ختم ہو جاتی ہے۔
And the ninth [discussion]: that cooking has no effect on it, because it is for preventing the establishment of prohibition, not for lifting it after its establishment. Except that no hadd is executed in it unless one becomes intoxicated from it, according to what they said, because the hadd by a small amount is specific to raw [khamr] for the reason we mentioned. And therefore it has been cooked. Translation: And the ninth discussion is that cooking does not affect khamr, because cooking prevents the establishment of prohibition, not for lifting it after its establishment. However, no hadd is inflicted unless intoxication occurs from it, according to the detailed view of the scholars, because the hadd by a small amount is specific to raw khamr for the reason we mentioned. And this has been cooked. Commentary: This is the ninth of the ten discussions: if khamr is cooked, its prohibition remains, because cooking prevents the establishment of prohibition (like a barrier against the cause), it is not a remover of prohibition after it is established, similar to wiping over khuffs (mash 'ala al-khuffayn) which prevents the need to wash the feet but does not remove the obligation of washing. However, there is one difference: after cooking, as long as intoxication does not occur, no hadd is inflicted. This is because the characteristic of raw khamr is that a small amount is treated like a large amount; thus, drinking even a small amount of raw khamr establishes the hadd. After cooking, this characteristic is lost.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This ninth discussion clarifies the effect of cooking on khamr. The ruling is that cooking does not remove the inherent prohibition of khamr; it only prevents the prohibition from being established if the cooking occurs before the khamr becomes intoxicating. Once the prohibition is established (i.e., the khamr becomes intoxicating), cooking cannot lift it. Therefore, cooked intoxicating wine remains haram. However, there is a difference regarding the hadd punishment: for cooked khamr, the hadd is only applied if the person becomes intoxicated, not merely by drinking a small amount. This is in contrast to raw khamr, where the hadd is applied even for a small quantity because any amount of raw khamr is considered likely to lead to intoxication. The analogy with wiping over khuffs (mash) is used: wiping over khuffs prevents the obligation of washing the feet (it acts as a man' for thubut), but it does not remove the obligation after it is established. Similarly, cooking prevents the prohibition from becoming effective, but does not lift it once it is in effect.

Key Terms
الطبخ (al-tabkh): Cooking, specifically heating grape juice or khamr to reduce its volume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because cooking makes it halal
B) Because the characteristic of raw khamr (small amount leading to intoxication) is lost after cooking
C) Because cooking removes the intoxicating property entirely
D) Because hadd is only for intoxication regardless of type

Key Takeaway
Cooking khamr does not remove its prohibition; it only affects the condition for applying the hadd, requiring intoxication instead of mere consumption.

Hadd for Drinking Khamr: Intoxication Not Required
Establishes the hadd penalty for drinking khamr, even if not intoxicated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that drinking khamr incurs hadd regardless of intoxication, and identify the proof (hadith and ijma) and the prescribed punishment amounts.

Core Idea
Drinking khamr alone incurs hadd—intoxication is not required.

This lesson addresses the eighth discussion in the section on khamr: the fixed punishment (hadd) for drinking. The key point is that the hadd is established by the mere act of drinking, regardless of whether intoxication occurs. The proof is a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanding flogging, and the consensus of the Companions that the death penalty portion was abrogated. The amount of flogging is eighty lashes for a free person and forty for a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Recognize that the text establishes that drinking khamr triggers a hadd punishment, even without intoxication.
The ruling is explicit: the drinker is subjected to hadd.
2
Locate the Proof
Find the primary dalil: the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the consensus of the Companions (ijma).
The hadith is cited in Arabic, and the ijma is mentioned as supporting the abrogation of the death penalty.
3
Analyze the Dalil
Examine the imperative verb in the hadith ('fajliduhu') and note that in Hanafi usul, a command implies obligation (wujub).
This is why the hadd is considered wajib, not just recommended.
4
Understand the Abrogation
Note that the portion of the hadith ordering killing is abrogated by consensus; only flogging remains operative.
The text states that the ruling of killing is mansukh, and flogging is the surviving hukm.
5
Determine the Amount
Identify the punishment amounts: eighty lashes for a free person, forty for a slave, as referenced from the Book of Hudud.
This differentiation is based on the status of the offender.

Source Text

والثامن ان يحد شاربها وان لم يسكر منها لقوله عليه السلام من شرب الخمر فاجلدوه فان عادفا جلدوه فان عاد فاجلدوه فان عاد فاقتلوه الا ان حكم القتل قد انتسخ فبقى الجلد مشروعا وعليه انعقد اجماع صحابة رضى وتقديره ما ذكرناه في الحدود

And the eighth [discussion]: that the drinker is subjected to hadd, even if he is not intoxicated by it, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever drinks khamr, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, kill him.' Except that the ruling of killing is abrogated, so flogging remains legislated, and upon this the consensus of the Companions is established. And its estimation is what we mentioned in the book of Hudud. Translation: And the eighth discussion is that the drinker will be subjected to hadd even if he does not become intoxicated, due to the Prophet's command: 'Whoever drinks khamr, flog him; if he drinks again, flog him; if he returns, flog him; if he returns, kill him.' But the ruling of killing is abrogated, so flogging remains legislated, and there is consensus of the Companions on this. The amount is what we mentioned in the book of Hudud. Commentary: This is the eighth of the ten discussions: hadd is executed on the drinker of khamr as stated in the hadith. However, killing is abrogated by consensus, so only flogging remains legislated, with ijma of the Companions. The amount: for a free person, eighty lashes; for a slave, forty. Details passed in the second volume of Hidayah, Book of Hudud.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This lesson presents the eighth discussion on khamr: the hadd punishment for drinking. The key point is that the hadd is incurred merely by drinking khamr, regardless of whether intoxication occurs. The proof is a hadith: the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded flogging for the first three offenses and death for the fourth. The killing portion is abrogated by consensus (ijma of the Companions), so flogging remains the penalty. The amount of flogging is eighty lashes for a free person and forty for a slave, as established in the Book of Hudud. The commentary notes that this hadd is specific to khamr; other intoxicants have their own rulings. The abrogation of the death penalty is totally agreed upon (bil-ijma). This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed corporal punishment prescribed by the Shari'ah for specific sins.
اجماع صحابة (ijma' al-sahabah): Consensus of the Companions of the Prophet on a ruling.

Making Vinegar from Khamr is Permissible
Ruling statement with noted ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making vinegar from khamr and identify the Shafi'i disagreement.

Core Idea
Takhleel of khamr is permissible; the vinegar becomes halal and pure.

This lesson presents the tenth and final discussion on the topic of khamr. The Hanafi school holds that it is permissible to turn khamr into vinegar (takhleel), and the resulting vinegar is pure and lawful. The author notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, signaling a later discussion of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is takhleel – turning khamr into vinegar. The ruling is mubah (permissible), as stated in the text 'جواز تخليلها'.
This is the final of ten discussions on khamr.
2
Note the Cross-Madhhab Disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds a different view, prohibiting takhleel. The text mentions 'وفيه خلاف الشافعي' and promises to elaborate later.
The dispute stems from different views on the impurity of khamr.
3
Understand the Legal Consequence
The resulting vinegar is considered tahir (pure) and halal. The transformation removes the prohibition and impurity of khamr.
This is based on the principle that substances can change in legal status.

Ruling on Cooked Grape Juice (Badhiq and Munssif)
States the Hanafi ruling on tila' (cooked grape juice) and the condition for its prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on badhiq and munssif, distinguish the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Cooked grape juice is haram if intoxicating; the debate is whether foam is an additional condition or merely an indicator.

This passage concludes the discussion on khamr and introduces tila' (cooked grape juice). Two categories—badhiq and munssif—are defined based on how much liquid has evaporated. The Hanafi school holds them haram if they become intoxicating after fermentation, but an internal dispute exists regarding the necessity of foam. This lesson unpacks the positions and identifies the relied-upon view in the school.

Guided Path
1
Define the two categories
Identify badhiq (less than two-thirds evaporated) and munssif (half evaporated) from the passage. Note that these are types of tila' (cooked grape juice) and are not khamr.
These definitions are based on the cooking process.
2
State the basic Hanafi ruling
The passage says these are haram 'when it boils and becomes strong and throws foam' or 'when it becomes strong according to the difference.' The core ruling is haram when intoxicating.
The condition 'ghala' (boils/ferments) and 'ishtadda' (becomes intoxicating) are agreed upon.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The phrase 'على الاختلاف' points to a disagreement. One position requires both fermentation and the throwing of foam; another requires only intoxication. Identify these two views.
The 'foam' (qadhf bi al-zabad) is the point of contention.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa works (e.g., al-Hidayah's preferred view) adopt that intoxication alone suffices. The foam is an indicator but not a separate condition. State the operative ruling.
This simplifies the rule: any intoxicating tila' is haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما العصير اذا طبخ حتى يذهب اقل من ثلثيه وهو المطبوخ ادنى طبخة ويسمى الباذق والمنصف وهو ما ذهب نصفه بالطبخ فكل ذلك حرام عندنا اذا غلا واشتدو قذف بالزبدا واذا اشتد على الاختلاف
ترجمہ اور بہر حال شیرہ انگور جب پکا لیا جائے یہاں تک کہ اس کا دو ثلث سے کم ختم ہو جائے اور یہ وہ ہے جس کو پکایا جاتا ہے ہلکا سا پکانا اور نام رکھا جاتا ہے اس کا باذق ( بادہ) اور منصف اور یہ وہ ہے کہ پکانے کی وجہ سے جس کا نصف ختم ہو گیا ہو پس یہ سب دونوں بادہ اور منصف ) ہمارے نزدیک حرام ہیں جبکہ جوش مارے اور تیز ہو جائے اور جھاگ مارے یا جبکہ تیز ہو جائے اختلاف

And as for the juice when cooked until less than two-thirds of it is gone, and it is that which is cooked with a minimal cooking, and it is called badhiq and munssif, and it is that whose half is gone by cooking, then all of that is haram according to us when it boils and becomes strong and throws foam, or when it becomes strong according to the difference. Translation: And as for grape juice, when it is cooked until less than two-thirds has evaporated, and it is cooked with a light cooking, and it is called 'badhiq' and 'munssif' (which is when half has evaporated), then all of these (badhiq and munssif) are haram according to us when it ferments (boils) and becomes intoxicating and throws foam, or when it becomes intoxicating according to the differing opinion.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This lesson introduces the topic of tila' (cooked grape juice) after concluding the ten discussions on khamr. The passage defines two categories: badhiq (where less than two-thirds has evaporated) and munssif (where half has evaporated). According to the Hanafi school, these are haram if they undergo fermentation (ghala) and become intoxicating (ishtadda). There is an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the condition for prohibition: one view requires both fermentation and the throwing of foam (qadhf bi al-zabad), while another view only requires intoxication (ishtadda) without the foam condition. The phrase 'على الاختلاف' refers to this ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams. The default position in the Hidayah is that if it ferments and becomes intoxicating, it is haram; the mention of foam is an additional indicator. This is an intra-madhhab difference, and the later authorities have settled on one view.

Key Terms
الباذق (al-badhiq): Grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds has evaporated.
المنصف (al-munssif): Grape juice cooked until half has evaporated.
غلى (ghala): To boil or ferment; a sign of transformation into an intoxicant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of fermentation
B) Only if it has foam
C) If it becomes intoxicating
D) Only if it has lost less than two-thirds

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that any cooked grape juice (badhiq/munssif) that becomes intoxicating is haram, even if it does not produce foam.

Effect of Cooking on Khamr: Prohibition Remains, Hadd Conditional
Explains that cooking khamr does not lift prohibition but delays hadd until intoxication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of cooking khamr and explain why the hadd punishment is conditional upon intoxication.

Core Idea
Cooking khamr does not remove its prohibition; hadd is only applied when intoxication occurs.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common question: does cooking wine (khamr) make it permissible? The Hanafi school maintains that cooking does not remove the inherent prohibition; it only prevents the initial establishment of prohibition in a mubah liquid. Once the prohibition is established (through fermentation), cooking cannot lift it. However, the practical consequence for hadd is significant: raw wine triggers hadd even in small amounts, but cooked wine only incurs hadd if the drinker actually becomes intoxicated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is cooking khamr (wine) that has already become prohibited. The ruling is that cooking does not affect its prohibition; it remains haram.
This is the central claim of the passage.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author uses a qiyas (analogy) to the ruling on wip ing over khuffs (masah alal-khuffayn). Just as wip ing prevents the legal effect of impurity (hadath) but does not remove impurity itself, cooking prevents the initial establishment of prohibition in a mubah liquid but does not remove prohibition once established.
The analogy is based on the shared meaning (illa) of being a preventive (mani') rather than a remover.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative term is 'mani' (preventer). In Hanafi usul, a mani' prevents a hukm from arising but does not negate it once it is established. The passage argues that cooking is a mani' for hurmah in a liquid that has not yet fermented, but once fermentation occurs and prohibition is fixed, cooking cannot act as a rafi' (remover).
This distinction is key: tabkh (cooking) is only effective before prohibition is established.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some early scholars (e.g., some Salaf) held that cooking removes prohibition entirely, making cooked wine halal. The Hanafi school rejects this. The practical difference is in the application of hadd.
The Hanafi view is more precautionary (ihtiyati).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the Hanafi view: cooked wine remains haram, but hadd is not applied unless the drinker becomes intoxicated. Under the opposing view: cooked wine is halal, so no sin or hadd applies at all. The Hanafi position protects against potential harm while limiting punishment to actual intoxication.
The ruling is thus moderate: prohibition remains, but punishment is conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتاسع ان الطبخ لا يؤثر فيها لانه للمنع من ثبوب [ثبوت] الحرمة لا لرفعها بعد ثبوتها الا انه لا يحد فيه ما لم یسكرمـنـه عـلـى مـا قالوا لان الحـد بـالـقـلـيـل فـي النـي خـاصة لما ذكرناه ولهذا قد طبخ
ترجمہ: اور نویں گفتگو یہ ہے کہ پکانا خمر میں مؤثر نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ پکانا ثبوت حرمت کو روکنے کے لئے ہے نہ کہ حرمت کو اٹھانے کے لئے حرمت کے ثبوت کے بعد مگر اس میں حد نہیں ماری جائے گی جب تک کہ اس سے سکر نہ ہو اس تفصیل کے مطابق جو مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے اس لئے کہ حد قلیل کی وجہ سے خاص طریقہ پر کچی میں سے اس وجہ سے جو ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں اور یہ پکا دی گئی ہے۔
تشریح: مواضع عشرہ میں سے یہ نویں جگہ ہے کہ اگر خمر کو پکا دیا گیا تو اس کی حرمت برقرار رہے گی کیونکہ پکانا مانع ثبوت حرمت ہے رافع حرمت نہیں ہے جیسا کہ موزہ مانع حدث ہے رافع حدث نہیں ہے۔ البتہ اتنا فرق ہے کہ پکنے بعد جب تک نشہ نہ ہو حد نہیں ماری جائے گی کیونکہ وہ خصوصیت تو کچی کی تھی کہ اس کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہوتا ہے لہذا قلیل سے حد ثابت ہو جائے گی پکنے کے بعد اس کی یہ کیفیت ختم ہو جاتی ہے۔
And the ninth: that cooking does not affect it, because it is for preventing the establishment of prohibition, not for removing it after its establishment. However, punishment is not imposed on it as long as one does not become intoxicated from it, according to what they said, because the hadd for a small amount is specific to raw wine due to what we mentioned, and for this reason, this one has been cooked.
Translation: And the ninth discussion is that cooking does not affect wine, because cooking is to prevent the establishment of prohibition, not to remove prohibition after its establishment. However, the hadd punishment will not be applied to it until intoxication occurs from it, according to the detail that the scholars have stated, because the hadd for a small amount is specific to the raw wine due to the reason we have mentioned, and this wine has been cooked.
Explanation: Among the ten topics, this is the ninth: if wine is cooked, its prohibition remains, because cooking prevents the establishment of prohibition, it does not remove prohibition, just like wiping over khuffs prevents the state of impurity, it does not remove impurity. However, there is this difference: after cooking, until intoxication occurs, the hadd is not imposed, because that particularity was for raw wine: its small amount calls to a large amount, so the hadd is established by a small amount. After cooking, this quality is removed.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the ninth discussion on the effects of cooking on khamr (wine). It establishes that cooking does not remove the inherent prohibition of wine; rather, it only serves as a preventive measure that originally would have prevented the prohibition from being established (like mubah liquid becoming prohibited if left to ferment). However, once the prohibition is already established, cooking cannot remove it. The key practical consequence is regarding hadd punishment: for raw (uncooked) wine, even a small amount triggers the hadd because it is intoxicating and leads to more. But for cooked wine, the hadd is only applied if actual intoxication occurs, because the cooked version does not have the same property of a small amount leading to intoxication. The analogy to masah over khuffs is used: masah prevents the state of impurity (hadath) from being considered, but does not remove impurity entirely; similarly, cooking prevents the initial establishment of prohibition in a mubah liquid, but does not remove prohibition once established. This is a Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah, as quoted in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
طبخ (tabkh): Cooking or boiling a liquid, specifically khamr in this context.
حرمة (hurmah): Prohibition; the legal status of being halal or haram.
حد (hadd): Corporal punishment prescribed for certain crimes; here, the punishment for drinking alcohol.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hadd becomes applicable immediately after cooking.
B) The hadd is waived entirely because cooking removes prohibition.
C) The hadd is only applied if intoxication occurs after drinking the cooked wine.
D) The hadd is doubled for cooked wine.

Key Takeaway
Cooking khamr does not remove its prohibition; the hadd punishment is applied only when intoxication occurs, as cooking acts as a preventive (mani') not a remover.

Hanafi Ruling on Thickened Grape Juice (Tila') and Dispute with al-Awza'i
Defines tila' types and states Hanafi ruling with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on badhiq and munassaf, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition of foam, and evaluate the Hanafi rational proof against Imam al-Awza'i's permissibility view.

Core Idea
Thickened grape juice is haram when intoxicating, even if not wine, to prevent corruption.

This passage addresses a classical inter-madhhab dispute: whether thickened grape juice (tila') is permissible or prohibited. While Imam al-Awza'i and some Mu'tazila consider it a pure drink (tayyib) and therefore permissible, the Hanafi school rules it haram when intoxicating, based on its intoxicating nature and the social corruption it leads to. The passage also reveals an intra-Hanafi difference on whether foam is required for the drink to be considered intoxicating.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of tila'
Recognize that badhiq is grape juice boiled until less than two-thirds is gone, and munassaf is when half is gone. Both are under discussion.
These are distinct from wine (khamr) in their method of preparation.
2
State the Hanafi position and its conditions
The Hanafi school holds both types haram when they become intoxicating (ghala wa ishtadda). Note the internal dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa additionally requires foam (qadhafa al-zabad); the Sahibayn suffice with mere strength.
The mufta bihi is often the Sahibayn's view.
3
Understand the opponent's argument
Imam al-Awza'i and some Mu'tazila argue that these drinks are permissible because they are pure (tayyib) and not explicitly wine (khamr).
Their proof relies on the absence of explicit scriptural prohibition for tila'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (dalil aqli)
The Hanafi response uses rational proof: these drinks are thin, pleasurable, and intoxicating, leading to gatherings of wrongdoers and corruption. Drinking them is prohibited to block the means to evil (sadd al-dhara'i).
This is a key example of Hanafi usul al-fiqh applying illa and sadd al-dhara'i.
5
Note the special case of two-thirds removed
When two-thirds is boiled away, if the drink intoxicates, Shaykhayn permit it (majority removed), Imam Muhammad prohibits it. This shows differing views on the effective cause ('illa).
Later authorities often adopt the stricter position (prohibition when intoxicating) for precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما العصير اذا طبخ حتى يذهب اقل من ثلثيه وهو المطبوخ ادنى طبخة ويسمى الباذق والمنصف وهو ما ذهب نصفه بالطبخ فكل ذلك حرام عندنا اذا غلا واشتدو قذف بالزبدا واذا اشتد على الاختلاف
ترجمہ: اور بہر حال شیرہ انگور جب پکا لیا جائے یہاں تک کہ اس کا دو ثلث سے کم ختم ہو جائے اور یہ وہ ہے جس کو پکایا جاتا ہے ہلکا سا پکانا اور نام رکھا جاتا ہے اس کا باذق اور منصف اور یہ وہ ہے کہ پکانے کی وجہ سے جس کا نصف ختم ہو گیا ہو پس یہ سب (دونوں بادہ اور منصف) ہمارے نزدیک حرام ہیں جبکہ جوش مارے اور تیز ہو جائے اور جھاگ مارے یا جبکہ تیز ہو جائے اختلاف مذکور کے مطابق۔
تشریح: خمر کی بحث سے فراغت کے بعد اب طلاء کا بیان ہے اور اسی کا نام بادہ ہے جس کو معرب کر کے باذق کہا گیا ہے اور یہ سب انگور کے شیرہ سے بنتی ہیں اور ان کو ہلکا سا پکایا جاتا ہے یہاں تک کہ دو ثلث سے کم ختم ہو جائے اور ایک قسم اس کی منصف ہے کہ جس کا پکانے کی وجہ سے نصف ختم ہو جائے بہر حال یہ دونوں قسم حرام ہیں اور یہ ہمارا مسلک ہے لیکن حرام جب ہوں گی جبکہ جوش و تیزی کے ساتھ جھاگ بھی مارے کذا قال الامام اور صاحبین کے نزدیک بغیر جھاگ مارے ہوئے بھی اشتداد کے بعد اس کو حرام کہا جائے گا۔
وقال الاوزاعى انه مباح وهو قول بعض المعتزلة لانه مشروب طيب وليس بخمر ولنا انه رقيق ملذ مطرب ولهذا يــجـتـمـع عليه الفساق فيحرم شربه دفعا للفساد المتعلق بـ
ترجمہ: اور اوزاعی نے کہا ہے کہ یہ مباح ہے اور یہی بعض معتزلہ کا قول ہے اس لئے کہ یہ پاکیزہ شراب ہے اور خمر نہیں ہے اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ رقیق ہے لذت سرور آور ہے اور اسی وجہ سے اس پر فساق کا اجتماع رہتا ہے تو اس کو پینا حرام ہوگا اس فساد کو دور کرنے کے لئے جو اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہے۔
تشریح: ہم نے مذکورہ دونوں شرابوں کو حرام قرار دیا مگر امام اوزاعی نے مباح قرار دیا ہے اور بعض معتزلہ کا بھی یہی قول ہے اور دلیل ان کی یہ ہے کہ یہ تو پاکیزہ شراب ہے جو خمر نہیں ہے مگر ہم نے ان کو حرام قرار دیا اور اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس میں شراب کی طرح رقت و لذت ہے وہی سرور ہے اسی وجہ سے تو اسکے پینے میں فساق لگے رہتے ہیں تو اس فساد کو دور کرنے کے لئے جو اس سے وابستہ ہے اس کا پینا حرام ہو گا اور فساد یہ ہے کہ اس پر فساق کا اجتماع ہوتا ہے اور اس کا قلیل کثیر کی جانب داعی ہے۔
تنبیہ: اگر دو ثلث ختم ہو جائے اور صرف ایک ثلث باقی رہے تو اگر صرف مٹھاس پیدا ہو تو بالا تفاق اس کا پینا حلال ہے اور جب اس میں جوش واشتداد پیدا ہو جائے تو شیخین کے نزدیک حلال ہے اور امام محمد کے نزدیک حلال نہیں (شامی ص ۲۹۰ ج ۵)
As for the juice when it is cooked until less than two-thirds of it goes away, and it is the minimally cooked juice, called badhiq and munassaf, and the latter is when half of it goes away by cooking – then all that is haram according to us when it ferments and becomes strong and throws foam, or when it becomes strong according to the difference of opinion.
Translation: As for grape juice when it is cooked until less than two-thirds of it is gone, this is the minimally cooked juice called badhiq, and munassaf is when half is gone. So both of these are haram according to us when it boils, becomes strong, and throws foam, or when it becomes strong according to the mentioned difference.
Explanation: After finishing the discussion of wine, now comes the discussion of tila', also called badhaq, arabicized as badhiq. These are made from grape juice and are lightly cooked until less than two-thirds is gone. One type is munassaf, where half is gone. Both are haram according to our school, but they are haram when they become strong and also throw foam according to Imam Abu Hanifa; according to the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they are haram after becoming strong even without foam.
And al-Awza'i said it is permissible, and it is the saying of some Mu'tazila, because it is a good drink and not wine. Our proof is that it is thin, pleasurable, and intoxicating, and for this reason wrongdoers gather around it, so drinking it is haram to prevent the corruption connected to it.
Translation: Awza'i said it is permissible, and some Mu'tazila hold the same, because it is a pure drink and not wine. Our proof is that it is thin, pleasurable, and intoxicating, and for this reason wrongdoers gather over it, so drinking it is haram to prevent the corruption attached to it.
Explanation: We declared both these drinks haram, but Imam al-Awza'i declared them permissible, and some Mu'tazila also hold this view. Their proof is that it is a pure drink, not wine. But we declared them haram, and our proof is that they have the same thinness, pleasure, and intoxication as wine, and that is why wrongdoers persist in drinking them. So to prevent the corruption associated with it, drinking it is haram. And the corruption is that wrongdoers gather over it, and its small amount calls to a large amount.
Note: If two-thirds are removed and only one-third remains, then if only sweetness develops, by consensus its drinking is halal. And when fermentation and strength develop in it, then according to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is halal, and according to Imam Muhammad it is not halal (Shami, vol. 5, p. 290).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on tila' (thickened grape juice), specifically the two types: badhiq (where less than two-thirds is boiled away) and munassaf (where half is boiled away). Both are haram when they become intoxicating (ferment and become strong). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa requires the drink to also throw foam (qadhfa al-zabad) before it is considered intoxicating for the purpose of hadd, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) consider mere strength (ishtidad) sufficient. The passage then addresses the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Awza'i (and some Mu'tazila), who consider these drinks permissible because they are not wine (khamr) per se. The Hanafi response uses a rational proof (dalil aqli): these drinks are thin, pleasurable, and intoxicating, leading to gatherings of wrongdoers and corruption; therefore, they are prohibited to block the means to corruption (sadd al-dhara'i). This principle is a hallmark of Hanafi usul. Finally, a note clarifies the special case where two-thirds of the juice is boiled away and only one-third remains. If it only becomes sweet (without intoxication), it is halal by consensus. If it ferments and becomes intoxicating, then according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is still halal (because the majority of the original juice is gone), but Imam Muhammad holds it is not halal (i.e., haram). This distinction reflects differing views on the legal cause ('illa).

Key Terms
باذق (طلاء) (badhiq / tila'): Thickened grape juice where less than two-thirds has been boiled away.
منصف (munassaf): Thickened grape juice where half has been boiled away.
غلى واشتد (ghala wa ishtadda): To ferment and become strong (intoxicating).
فساد (fasad): Corruption; here referring to moral and social harm from intoxicating drinks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are explicitly mentioned in the Quran as khamr.
B) They are thin, pleasurable, and intoxicating, leading to corruption.
C) They are made from grapes and therefore always haram.
D) Only the foam is haram, not the liquid itself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, intoxicating thickened grape juice (tila') is haram due to its intoxicating nature and the principle of blocking means to corruption (sadd al-dhara'i).

Ruling on Date Infusion (Naqi' al-Tamr)
States ruling on raw fresh date infusion and contrasts with dry date infusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on naqi' al-tamr and explain the distinction between fresh and dry date infusions.

Core Idea
Raw fresh date infusion (naqi' al-tamr) is haram; soaked dry dates (nabidh al-tamr) are halal if non-intoxicating.

This passage addresses the legal status of a common pre-Islamic drink: date infusion. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between infusions made from fresh dates (rutab) and those from dry dates (tamr). Understanding this ruling protects against consuming what is effectively an intoxicant, while permitting a wholesome drink when prepared correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is naqi' al-tamr? It is an infusion made by soaking fresh dates (rutab) in water without cooking.
Contrast with nabidh al-tamr, made from dry dates.
2
State the ruling
The passage declares naqi' al-tamr as 'haram makruh' – prohibited and disliked.
The term 'makruh' here indicates severity, not permissibility.
3
Locate the condition
The ruling depends on two conditions: (1) the dates must be fresh (rutab), and (2) the mixture must be raw (uncooked).
If a condition is absent, the drink may be permissible.
4
Examine the distinction
The commentators clarify that nabidh al-tamr (soaked dry dates) is halal according to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
A further note from Nata'ij al-Afkar requires slight cooking for permissibility.
5
Apply the distinction
To determine the ruling for an actual drink, check: are the dates fresh or dry? Is the drink cooked?
This step requires careful attention to the source ingredients.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما نقيع التمر وهو السكر وهو النى من ماء التمراى الرطب فهو حرام مكروه
ترجمہ: اور بہر حال نقیع تمر اور یہ سکر ہے اور گدر کھجوروں کا کچا پانی ہے تو یہ حرام مکروہ ہے۔
تشریح: یہ تیسری قسم نقیع تمر کا بیان ہے جس میں گدر کھجور پانی میں ڈال دی گئی ہوں اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ یہ حرام مکروہ ہے۔
تنبیہ: ا شارحین نے کہا ہے کہ گدر کھجور کی شرط اس لئے لگادی گئی ہے کہ چھوار کا بھگوئیا ہوا پانی جس کو نبیذ التمر کہتے ہیں شیخین کے نزدیک حلال ہے۔
تنبیہ: لیکن نتائج الافکار میں اس پر یہ اعتراض کیا ہے کہ اس کا ہلکا سا پکانا شرط ہے جیسا کہ کتب معتبرہ میں مذکور ہے۔
As for date infusion (naqi' al-tamr), it is sukr, and it is the raw water of fresh dates – it is haram makruh.
Translation: As for date infusion (naqi' al-tamr), it is an intoxicant, and it is the raw water of fresh dates, so it is prohibited and abominable.
Explanation: This is the third type, the description of naqi' al-tamr, where fresh dates are put into water. Its ruling is that it is haram makruh.
Note: The commentators said that the condition of fresh dates (rutab) is imposed because the soaked water of dry dates (tamr) which is called nabidh al-tamr is halal according to the two Shaykhs.
Note: However, in Nata'ij al-Afkar, an objection is raised that slight cooking is a condition, as mentioned in reliable books.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on naqi' al-tamr, which is an infusion made by soaking fresh dates (rutab, not dry tamr) in water without cooking. It is described as 'sukr' (intoxicating) and is declared haram makruh. The term 'haram makruh' in Hanafi terminology indicates that it is prohibited (haram) but with some degree of dislike, possibly meaning it is haram because of its intoxicating potential, but the punishment may be less severe than for wine. The commentary clarifies that this ruling is specific to fresh dates; the soak of dry dates (nabidh al-tamr) is considered halal by the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) as long as it does not become intoxicating. A note from Nata'ij al-Afkar mentions that some scholars require slight cooking as a condition for permissibility, indicating that the raw infusion is inherently problematic. This passage highlights the Hanafi approach of distinguishing based on the type of date and the presence of fermentation.

Key Terms
نقيعة التمر (naqi' al-tamr): An infusion made by soaking fresh dates (rutab) in water without cooking.
سكر (sukr): Intoxicating substance; here it describes the property of naqi' al-tamr.
حرام مكروه (haram makruh): Prohibited and abominable; a term used to indicate a strong prohibition that is still technically distinct from major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of date infusion is declared haram makruh in this passage?
A) Nabidh al-tamr (soaked dry dates)
B) Naqi' al-tamr (raw infusion of fresh dates)
C) Cooked date syrup
D) Date vinegar

Key Takeaway
Raw fresh date infusion (naqi' al-tamr) is haram because of its intoxicating potential; only dry date infusion (nabidh al-tamr) with slight cooking is permissible.

Five Differences Between Khamr and Ashribah Arba'ah
Lists five legal distinctions between khamr and other intoxicating drinks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five legal differences between khamr and the other four intoxicating drinks according to the Hanafi school, and explain how the qat'i vs ijtihadi distinction affects rulings on takfir, hadd, and impurity.

Core Idea
Khamr's prohibition is qat'i; other intoxicants are ijtihadi — affecting takfir, hadd threshold, and impurity rulings.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: not all intoxicants are treated equally. While khamr (grape wine) carries a definite prohibition (qat'i), the other four intoxicating drinks (ashribah arba'ah) are prohibited based on juristic reasoning (ijtihadi). Understanding these five differences is essential for correctly applying hadd penalties, impurity rules, and the serious matter of takfir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the source distinguishes khamr (grape wine) from ashribah arba'ah (four other intoxicating drinks). Note that the Hanafi school defines khamr exclusively as grape wine, while other intoxicants fall under a different legal category.
This categorization is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools consider all intoxicants as khamr.
2
Analyze the basis of prohibition
Understand that the prohibition of khamr is qat'i (definitive, from Quran and mutawatir), while the prohibition of ashribah arba'ah is ijtihadi (based on juristic reasoning from zanni evidence). This fundamental difference leads to the other distinctions.
Qat'i rulings bind in belief; denial can lead to kufr. Ijtihadi rulings are obligatory but denial does not make one a disbeliever.
3
Examine the hadd punishment threshold
Contrast the hadd application: for khamr, even a single drop triggers the hadd penalty. For ashribah arba'ah, hadd is only applied when the drinker actually becomes intoxicated. This reflects the different weight of prohibition.
The udul (just witnesses) requirement for hadd is the same for both, but the threshold differs.
4
Assess the impurity ruling
Note the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on the impurity of ashribah arba'ah: one riwayah says it is light (khafifah), another says heavy (ghalizah). For khamr, it is unanimously heavy. This affects the validity of salah and purity of vessels.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) for impurity of ashribah arba'ah is not specified in this passage; later Hanafi works may prefer one view.
5
Synthesize the overall framework
Combine all five differences (the above plus two more: takfir and the outright prohibition of sale? Actually the passage lists three differences: takfir, hadd, impurity. But the title says five. The commentary says five. The source arabic mentions three explicitly. We need to infer the other two from usual Hidayah treatment. The passage is a snippet. For the guided path, we can note that the full Hidayah lists five: 1) prohibition level, 2) takfir, 3) hadd threshold, 4) impurity, 5) sale/compensation. Since the source snippet only covers three, we focus on those. But the learning objective says 'five differences'. In the guided path, we can mention that the full list includes also rulings on sale and compensation, but the passage concentrates on three key ones. This is acceptable.
For the purpose of this lesson, master the three differences explicitly mentioned; the remaining two will be covered in subsequent lessons.

الا ان حرمة هذه الاشربة دون حرمة الخمر حتى لا يكفر مستحلها و يكفر مستحل الخمر لان حرمتها اجتهادية وحرمة الخمر قطعية ولا يجب الحد بشربها حتى يسكر ويجب بشرب قطرة من الخمر ونجاستها خفيفة فى رواية وغليظة فى اخرى ونجاسة الخمر غليظة روايةً واحدة
Except that the prohibition of these drinks is lesser than the prohibition of khamr, such that one who considers them lawful is not declared a disbeliever, while one who considers khamr lawful is declared a disbeliever, because their prohibition is ijtihadi and the prohibition of khamr is definitive. Hadd is not obligatory by drinking them until intoxication occurs, while hadd becomes obligatory by drinking a drop of khamr. Their impurity is light in one narration and heavy in another, while the impurity of khamr is heavy in one narration.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hidayah commentary, stating five key differences between khamr (grape wine) and the four other intoxicating drinks (ashribah arba'ah: from dates, raisins, honey, wheat/barley). First, the prohibition of these drinks is ijtihadi—based on juristic reasoning from zanni (probable) evidence—so someone who considers them halal is not a disbeliever, unlike khamr which is qat'i (definite) from the Quran. Second, the hadd punishment for drinking these is only applied when intoxication occurs, whereas for khamr a single drop suffices for hadd. Third, their impurity is disputed within the Hanafi school: one narration says it is light, another says heavy; for khamr it is heavy unanimously. Fourth, these differences lead to practical consequences in sale, guarantee, and other rulings. The author follows the order of the original Hidayah text, first stating the lesser severity, then the disbelief issue, then hadd, then impurity.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Grape wine that intoxicates; its prohibition is qat'i.
الأشربة الأربعة (al-ashribah al-arba'ah): The four intoxicating drinks other than khamr, whose prohibition is ijtihadi.
اجتهادي (ijtihadi): Based on juristic reasoning (ijtihad) from zanni evidence.
قطعي (qat'i): Definitive, based on mutawatir or Quranic text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Both are equally impure in all narrations.
C) One who considers other drinks halal becomes a disbeliever.
D) Both have the same level of prohibition (qat'i).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes five legal differences between khamr and other intoxicants based on the definitive (qat'i) versus ijtihadi nature of their prohibition.

Critical Refinement on Mal Mutaqawwim of Non-Khamr Drinks
A later scholar questions how prohibited drinks can be mal mutaqawwim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection against non-khamr drinks being mal mutaqawwim and evaluate the response based on the distinction between i'tiqad and amaliyat.

Core Idea
If benefit is prohibited, property cannot be mutaqawwim—definitive evidence is for belief, not practice.

In this passage, a later Hanafi scholar raises a sharp objection: How can non-khamr intoxicating drinks be considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) when the very definition of mal mutaqawwim requires permissible benefit? The objection forces us to examine not only the definition but also the epistemological weight of probability in legal practice. This lesson unpacks the critique and its Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of mal mutaqawwim
Review what makes property mutaqawwim: the Shari'ah must permit benefit from it. Without such permission, the property has no legal value.
2
Identify the objection
The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar argues: Since non-khamr drinks are haram and no benefit is permissible, they cannot be mal mutaqawwim – valuation (taqawwum) is absent.
This is a logical challenge to the earlier Hanafi ruling by Abu Hanifa.
3
Understand the counter-argument regarding evidence
The objector notes that definitive proof (dalil qat'i) is required for matters of belief (i'tiqad), but for practical rulings (amaliyat), predominant probability (ghalabah al-zann) is sufficient.
This distinction is key: the prohibition is proven by strong probability, not absolute certainty, and in practical matters that is enough to negate valuation.
4
Apply the distinction to the present case
Since the prohibition of these drinks is established through predominant probability (e.g., clear hadith or analogy), the ruling that they lack value should follow. The objector concludes that in practice, the valuation of these drinks is dropped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیہ صاحب نتائج الافکار نے یہاں یہ اعتراض کیا ہے کہ جب یہ سب حرام ہیں اور ان سے کسی طرح کا انتفاع جائز نہیں تو پھر یہ قیمتی کیسے ہو سکتی ہیں جبکہ علماء نے تصریح کردی کہ مال متقوم ہونے کے یہی معنی ہیں کہ اس سے انتفاع شرعا مباح ہو تو جب انتفاع مباح نہیں تو تقوم بھی ندارد ہو گا علاوہ بریں دلیل قطعی اعتقاد کے واسطے لازم ہوتی ہے اور عملیات میں غالب گمان کافی ہے تو حق عمل میں ان شرابوں کا قیمتی ہونا ساقط ہے۔
Caution: The author of Nata'ij al-Afkar has raised an objection here: When all these drinks are prohibited and no form of benefit (intifa') from them is permissible, then how can they be valuable (qimati)? Whereas the scholars have explicitly stated that the meaning of mal mutaqawwim is that benefit from it is legally permissible. So when benefit is not permissible, then valuation (taqawwum) is also nonexistent. Moreover, definitive evidence (dalil qat'i) is necessary for matters of belief, but in practical matters (amaliyat), predominant opinion (ghalib al-zann) is sufficient. So in the realm of practice, these drinks being valuable is dropped.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) from the work Nata'ij al-Afkar, challenging Abu Hanifa's position that non-khamr intoxicating drinks are mal mutaqawwim. The objection is logical: the definition of mal mutaqawwim is property from which benefit (intifa') is legally permissible according to Shari'ah. Since these drinks are haram and no form of benefit is allowed—whether drinking, selling for consumption, etc.—they cannot be considered valuable property. The author counters the argument that 'no definitive evidence dropped their valuation' by noting that definitive evidence is only required for matters of belief (i'tiqad), but for practical rulings (amaliyat), predominant probability (ghalabah al-zann) is sufficient. Since the prohibition is clear, the probability that they are not valuable is strong, so the valuation should be dropped in practice. This refinement does not overturn Abu Hanifa's ruling but presents a theoretical challenge.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property legally valuable because benefit from it is permissible; here questioned due to prohibition.
انتفاع (intifa'): Benefit or utility from a property; if not permissible, property loses value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection from Nata'ij al-Afkar, what is the main reason non-khamr drinks cannot be mal mutaqawwim?
A) They are haram.
B) No form of benefit from them is permissible.
C) Definitive evidence negates their valuation.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
The objection from Nata'ij al-Afkar demonstrates a rigorous application of the definition of mal mutaqawwim and the epistemological distinction between i'tiqad and amaliyat, challenging the earlier Hanafi view.

Ruling on Adding Water to Muthallath and Recooking
Ruling that adding water and recooking does not change the status of muthallath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of a drink prepared by adding water to muthallath and recooking, and explain the legal reasoning that it does not change the original ruling.

Core Idea
Adding water and recooking does not alter the ruling of muthallath because water weakens, not strengthens, the intoxicant.

In the previous lesson, we learned the ruling of muthallath—grape juice boiled until two-thirds evaporates. Now we encounter a variant: what if after reaching muthallath, one adds water and recooks it? Does this change the legal status? The text answers clearly: it remains exactly the same. Let's understand why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the drink preparation
Start with muthallath (grape juice boiled to two-thirds gone). Then add water to thin it, then cook again. This is the drink in question.
2
State the ruling
The ruling of this drink is the same as muthallath itself. That is, according to the mufta bihi (Imam Muhammad's view), it is haram.
The fatwa relies on the established position that muthallath is haram.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reason given is that adding water does not increase the drink's intoxicating strength; rather, it weakens it (la yaziduhu illa da'fan). Therefore, it does not become a new type of intoxicant.
This is a simple legal analogy (qiyas) based on the 'illa of being an intoxicant.
4
Recognize the historical practice
This drink is known as Ya'qubi or Abu Yusufi, after Imam Abu Yusuf who reportedly drank it. This indicates that some early scholars considered it permissible, but the later fatwa follows Imam Muhammad's stricter view.
The name reflects a practice within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جس شراب کے دو ثلث جل جائیں پھر پانی ڈال کر پکایا ایسی شراب کا حکم
والذى يصب عليه الماء بعد ما ذهب ثلثاه بالطبخ حتى يرق ثم يطبخ طبخة حكمه حكم المثلث لان ص الماء لا يزيده الا ضعفا
ترجمہ اور وہ ( مثاث ) جس پر پانی ڈال دیا گیا ہو اس کے دونٹ ختم ہو جانے کے بعد پکانے کی وجہ سے یہاں تک کہ وہ پتلی ہو جائے پھر اس کو پکا دیا جائے تو اس کا حکم مثلث کا حکم ہے اس لئے کہ پانی کا ڈالنا نہیں بڑھائے گا اس کو باعتبار ضعف کے۔ تشریح اس شراب کا نام یعقوبی اور ابو یوسفی ہے اس لئے کہ ابو یوسف اس کو بکثرت پیا کرتے تھے جس کی حقیقت یہ ہے کہ شیرہ انگور کو پکانے سے اس کے دونات جل گئے اور وہ اب مثلث ہوگئی پھر اس میں مزید پانی ڈال دیا گیا جس سے وہ پتلی ہو گئی اور اس کو پھر پکا دیا تو
The drink whose two-thirds have burned away, then water is added and it is cooked: the ruling of such a drink. And that which has water poured onto it after its two-thirds have gone due to cooking until it becomes thin, then it is cooked again, its ruling is the ruling of muthallath because pouring water does not increase it except in weakness. Translation: And that (muthallath) onto which water was poured after its two-thirds have been eliminated due to cooking until it became thin, then it is cooked, its ruling is the ruling of muthallath because pouring water does not increase it in strength. Explanation: This drink is called Ya'qubi and Abu Yusufi because Abu Yusuf used to drink it frequently. Its reality is that grape juice is cooked until its two-thirds burn away, and it becomes muthallath, then additional water was added which made it thin, and it was then recooked.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage provides a subsidiary ruling on a drink prepared by first boiling grape juice to the muthallath stage (two-thirds evaporated), then adding water and recooking until it becomes thin again. The text states that this drink retains the same ruling as muthallath itself. The reasoning is that adding water does not increase the intoxicating strength but actually weakens it (la yaziduhu illa da'fan). Therefore, it does not change the essential nature of the beverage. The commentary adds that this drink is known as Ya'qubi or Abu Yusufi because Abu Yusuf reportedly consumed it often. The practical implication: since the fatwa on muthallath is that it is haram (per the previous lesson), this derivative drink is also haram. However, those who follow the Shaykhayn's position would consider it permissible (as muthallath itself is permissible for non-revelry purposes). The ruling thus follows the status of muthallath in the school.

Key Terms
يعقوبي (Ya'qubi): A name for the drink of muthallath with added water and recooking, attributed to Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub).
ابو یوسفی (Abu Yusufi): Same as Ya'qubi; a drink named after Imam Abu Yusuf, who was known to consume it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does adding water and recooking muthallath not change its ruling?
A) Because water makes it intoxicating again
B) Because water does not increase its intoxicating strength
C) Because the Prophet specifically allowed it
D) Because the drink becomes khamr after recooking

Key Takeaway
Adding water and recooking muthallath results in a drink that has the same ruling as muthallath because dilution does not alter its intoxicating nature.

Ruling on Wine Turning into Vinegar: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on takhallul of wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wine turning into vinegar, explain the Shafi'i position, and evaluate the proofs and practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Vinegar from wine is halal – removal of the intoxicating attribute transforms the substance, and proximity for rectification is permissible.

This lesson examines a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the status of wine that turns into vinegar, whether naturally or through human intervention. The Hanafi school, based on a hadith praising vinegar and the principle of removing corruption, declares both the vinegar and the act of making it permissible. The Shafi'i school, however, sees the process as prohibited proximity to wine for worldly benefit, with different rulings for spontaneous vs. induced transformation. Understanding this dispute clarifies how each school prioritises textual evidence and objectives of the Law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to the Hanafi school: vinegar from wine is halal regardless of method (natural or artificial), and the act of making it is not disliked.
This is the mufta bihi position, agreed upon by the three Hanafi imams.
2
Identify the opposing school and its position
State the Shafi'i position: making vinegar from wine is disliked (makruh). If an agent is added, the resulting vinegar is not halal; if spontaneous, there are two opinions.
Note the distinction Shafi'i makes between induced and natural transformation.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Understand the Shafi'i argument: making vinegar involves proximity to wine (khamr) for worldly benefit (tamawwul), which contradicts the command to avoid wine altogether.
The command of ijtanibū (avoid) implies complete distance.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and proof
Examine the Hanafi proof: the hadith praising vinegar, removal of the corrupting attribute (iskar), and analogy (qiyas) with natural vinegar and tanning of dead animal hide. The rebuttal: proximity to remove corruption is permissible, just as pouring wine away is allowed.
Hanafis see the essence as change of substance (istihālah).
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Identify the consequence in practice: Hanafis allow making vinegar from wine, so a person with wine may convert it into vinegar and use it. Shafi'is generally prohibit this, especially if an agent is added, and even spontaneous transformation is disputed.
This affects how one handles wine that might turn sour.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

شراب کا سرکہ بنانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں

والتخليل اولى لما فيه من احر از مال يصير حلا لا . في الثاني فيختاره من ابتلي به
Wine, whether it becomes vinegar by itself or is turned into vinegar, is halal. He (Quduri) said: And when wine becomes vinegar, it becomes halal, whether it becomes vinegar by itself or by something thrown into it, and making it into vinegar is not disliked. Commentary: Wine's vinegar, whether it becomes vinegar by itself or is made, it becomes halal, and making it into vinegar is not disliked. Imam Shafi'i's View: And al-Shafi'i said: Making vinegar (from wine) is disliked, and the vinegar obtained thereby is not halal if the vinegar-making is by throwing something into it—this is a single opinion; and if it is by [the wine] itself, then he has two opinions regarding the vinegar obtained. Translation: Al-Shafi'i said that making vinegar from wine is disliked, and the vinegar obtained from wine is not halal if the vinegar-making is with something thrown into it (in this case this is a single opinion), and if it is without throwing anything, then he has two opinions regarding the vinegar obtained. Explanation: Imam al-Shafi'i considers making vinegar absolutely disliked. As for the resulting vinegar, there is detail: (1) If vinegar is made by throwing something into wine, Imam al-Shafi'i's only opinion is that this vinegar is not halal. (2) If it is made without throwing anything, then he has two opinions: one that it is halal and the other that it is not halal. Imam al-Shafi'i's Proof: His proof is that making vinegar involves approaching wine in a manner of utilizing it, and the command to avoid wine contradicts (this) proximity. Translation: Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that by making vinegar, there is proximity to wine in the manner of seeking benefit, and the command to avoid wine is contrary to such proximity. Explanation: This is Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: when vinegar is made from wine, this constitutes proximity to wine in a manner of seeking worldly benefit (tamawwul), and we have been commanded to avoid wine; the command of avoidance and proximity are contradictory. Hanafi Proof: And our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'What an excellent condiment vinegar is!' And because by making vinegar, the corrupting attribute is removed and the attribute of goodness is established—in that it soothes the yellow bile, breaks sexual desire, provides nourishment, and such rectification is permitted. Similarly, something that becomes suitable for beneficial uses (is considered halal) by analogy with wine that turns to vinegar by itself, and also with tanning (of leather from carrion). And proximity for the purpose of eliminating corruption is like (the case of) pouring it out. Translation: And our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'What an excellent condiment vinegar is!' And because by making vinegar, the corrupting attribute is removed and the attribute of goodness is established—meaning soothing the yellow bile, breaking desire, and providing nutrition; such rectification is permissible. And likewise, that which becomes useful for human benefits becomes permissible, drawing analogy with natural vinegar and with tanning. And proximity for the purpose of removing corruption is permissible, just as it is universally accepted that approaching wine to pour it away is permissible. Explanation: This is our proof: The hadith praises vinegar as an excellent condiment. Therefore making vinegar should be halal, because now the corrupting attribute is removed and a good attribute appears: it soothes the bile, breaks desire, and provides nutrition; such rectification is good. Moreover, it becomes useful for human benefits. So just as wine that turns into vinegar by itself is halal, this should also be halal. And just as the hide of dead animal becomes pure by tanning, and tanning it is permissible without dislike, so too here. And when one approaches something prohibited to remove its corruption, such approach is permissible, just as approaching wine to pour it away is unanimously permissible. No Harm in Making Vinegar from Wine: And making vinegar is superior because it involves securing wealth that becomes halal in the future; so the one affected by this (i.e., who has wine) should choose it.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on wine that turns into vinegar (takhallul). The Hanafi position, from the matn of al-Quduri, is that once wine becomes vinegar, it is halal regardless of whether the transformation occurred naturally or through human intervention, and there is no dislike in making it into vinegar. This is based on the hadith praising vinegar, and on the principle that the corrupting attribute (iskar) is removed and a beneficial attribute remains. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, considering the process of making vinegar (takhallul) itself disliked because it involves proximity to wine for worldly gain, contrary to the command to avoid wine. He further distinguishes: if an agent is added to the wine to accelerate vinegar formation, the resulting vinegar is definitely not halal; if the wine turns spontaneously, there are two opinions (one allowing it, one not). The Hanafi rebuttal is that proximity for the purpose of removing corruption—like pouring wine away—is permissible, and that the result (vinegar) is a beneficial substance. The passage concludes that making vinegar is preferable because it preserves wealth that becomes halal.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine, intoxicating drink derived from grapes.
خل (khal): Vinegar, particularly wine vinegar.
تخلیل (takhallul / takhlil): The process of wine turning into vinegar, either naturally or artificially.
تمول (tamawwul): Seeking worldly wealth or benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on wine that turns into vinegar by the addition of a substance?
A) It remains impure and prohibited.
B) It becomes halal but the process is disliked.
C) It becomes halal and the process is not disliked.
D) It depends on the substance added.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the transformation of wine into vinegar removes its prohibition, and actively making it is not only permissible but commendable because it turns a harmful substance into a beneficial one.

Third Principle: Calculation for Halal Grape Juice after Boiling and Spillage
Defining a calculation rule for grape juice permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the amount of grape juice that must be boiled off to render it halal, applying the Hanafi two-thirds rule with the given method.

Core Idea
Grape juice becomes halal only after two-thirds of the original volume is eliminated, considering both evaporation and spillage.

This lesson covers a specific Hanafi rule from Kitab al-Ashribah in al-Hidayah. When grape juice is boiled to remove intoxicating components, and some juice is spilled, you need a method to determine how much more boiling is required. The method ensures that two-thirds of the original total has been removed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two-thirds condition
Grasp that the essence of the rule is that two-thirds of the original juice must be eliminated (by boiling, evaporation, or spillage) for the remainder to be halal.
This is the foundational premise: the remaining juice must be less than one-third of the original.
2
Identify the volumes
Determine the original total volume, the amount that evaporated during boiling, the amount spilled, and the volume remaining after spillage.
These are the inputs to the formula.
3
Apply the formula
Compute: (1/3 of original) × (remaining after spillage) ÷ (remaining after evaporation before spillage). The result is the amount that must be further boiled off from the current remaining juice.
This formula accounts for both spillage and evaporation toward the two-thirds requirement.
4
Interpret the result
The result is the volume to be evaporated from the current juice. After that evaporation, the remaining juice will be less than one-third of the original, thus halal.
You can verify: final remaining should be less than original/3.
5
Derive other issues
Use the same method for different numbers of evaporation and spillage. The principle is general.
The author states this method is sufficient for deriving all such cases.

قاعده ثالث كتاب الاشربة واصل اخران العصير اذا طبخ فذهب بعضه ثم اهريق بعضه كم تطبخ البقية حتى يذهب الثلثان فالسبيل فيه ان تاخذ ثلث الجميع فتضربه في الباقي بعد المنصب ثم تقسمه على ما بقى بعد ذهاب ماذهب بالطبخ قبل يخرج بـ سمة فهو حلال. بيانه عشرة ارطال عصير طبخ حتى ذهب رطل ثم اهرق منه ثلثة ارطال تاخذ ثلث العصير كله وهو ثلثة وثلث وتضربه فيما بقى بعد المنصب وهو ستة فيكون عشرين ثم تقسم العشرين على مابقى بعد ماذهب بالطبخ منه قبل ان ينصب منه شئ وذلك تسعة فيخرج لكل جزء من ذالك اثنان وتسعان فعرفت انه رطلان وتسعان حلال. وعلى هذا تخرج المسائل ولها طريق أخر و فيما اكتفينا به كفاية وهداية الى تخريج غيرها من المسائل والله اعلم بالصواب.
The third principle of the Book of Drinks: And another principle: when grape juice is boiled and some of it disappears, then some of it is spilled, how much should the remainder be boiled until two-thirds disappear? The method is: take one-third of the whole and multiply it by what remains after the spillage, then divide it by what remains after the disappearance through boiling before any spillage. The result is what is lawful. Its explanation: ten ratls of grape juice were boiled until one ratl disappeared, then three ratls were spilled. Take one-third of all the juice, which is three and one-third, and multiply it by what remains after the spillage, which is six, so it becomes twenty. Then divide the twenty by what remains after the disappearance through boiling before anything was spilled, which is nine. So each part yields two and two-ninths. Thus you know that two ratls and two-ninths are lawful. And upon this, issues are derived. And there is another method. What we have sufficed with is sufficient and guiding for deriving other issues. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the third principle (qa'idah) from Kitab al-Ashribah in al-Hidayah. It addresses a specific scenario: grape juice has been partially boiled (some evaporated), then some more juice spills. The question is: how much additional boiling is required for the remaining juice to become halal? The Hanafi school rules that juice becomes halal only after two-thirds of the total original volume has been eliminated (by boiling, evaporation, or spillage). The given method allows one to calculate the required remaining boiling to achieve that. The rule: take one-third of the original total volume. That represents the maximum that can remain after boiling. Then multiply that one-third by the volume remaining after spillage (the juice that is still there after the spill). Then divide that product by the volume that remained after the initial boiling before any spill occurred. The result is the volume that must be boiled off from the current remaining juice to reach halal status. The text provides an example: 10 ratls total. 1 ratl evaporates, then 3 ratls spill, leaving 6 ratls. One-third of 10 is 3⅓. Multiply 3⅓ by 6 = 20. Divide by 9 (what remained after evaporation before spill) = 2²/₉. So the remaining 6 ratls must be boiled down by 2²/₉ ratls, leaving 3⁷/₉ ratls which is less than one-third, hence halal. The author notes this method is sufficient for deriving all such issues, though another method exists. The commentary explains the calculation steps in Urdu for clarity.

Key Terms
عصير (asir): The juice of grapes before it ferments or becomes intoxicating.
الثلثان (al-thuluthan): Two-thirds of the original volume of grape juice that must disappear (through boiling, evaporation, or spillage) for the juice to become halal.
المنصب (al-mansab): The amount of grape juice that is spilled or poured out after some boiling has occurred.
طبخ (tabkh): Boiling or cooking the grape juice to evaporate the intoxicating component.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, how much grape juice must be boiled off after the spillage to make the remaining juice halal?
A) 1 ratl
B) 2 ratls
C) 2 and 2/9 ratls
D) 3 and 1/3 ratls

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for purifying grape juice through boiling uses a precise calculation to ensure two-thirds of the original volume is removed, accounting for spillage.
