The Training Condition for a Hunting Dog: Three Times or Hunter's Judgment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the condition for a hunting dog's training.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the number of times a hunting dog must leave prey to be considered trained, and explain the practical difference (thamarat al-khilaf) in ruling.

Core Idea
A dog's training is determined by consistent behavior: three times according to Sahibayn, or the hunter's judgment according to Abu Hanifa.

When a hunting dog catches prey but does not eat it, does this prove it is trained? The Sahibayn require three consecutive occurrences to establish a pattern, while Abu Hanifa (in one narration) leaves it to the hunter's dominant assumption. This dispute has a direct practical consequence: is the third catch halal or not? Let us examine the proofs and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
The passage asks: after how many times of leaving prey does a dog become mu'allam (trained) such that its catch is halal? The answer determines the permissibility of each catch.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the dog must leave prey three times. Less than three may be due to coincidence; three establishes habit. They support this with the principle that the plural (jami') begins with three, and three is the standard for testing (e.g., period of option, three days in Quran and hadith).
They analogize to a slave's transaction: the master's silence on one occasion does not bind him.
3
State Abu Hanifa's two narrations
Imam Abu Hanifa has two views: one agreeing with Sahibayn on the three-time condition but differing on the third catch (he deems it halal), and another that no fixed number is required; it depends on the hunter's dominant assumption because quantities cannot be set by ijtihad without a text.
The second narration is recorded in al-Mabsut.
4
Analyze the thamarat al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The key difference: under Abu Hanifa's first narration, the third catch is halal because the dog shows training at that moment. Under Sahibayn, the third catch is not halal because training is only completed after three times. The fourth and subsequent catches are halal in both views.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text does not explicitly choose, but the Hidayah often adopts the Sahibayn view. Later Hanafi fatwa also leans toward Sahibayn, making the third catch haram until the dog completes three times.
Both narrations are valid within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب الصيد
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
الاحتمال فلعله ترك مرة او مرتين شبعا فاذا تركه ثلثا دل على انه صار عادة له ولهذا لان الثلث مدة ضربت للاختيار وابلاء الاعذار كما في مدة الخيار وفي بعض قصص الاخيار
ترجمہ پھر قدوری نے شرط لگائی تین مرتبہ چھوڑنے کی اور یہ صاحبین کے نزدیک ہے اور یہی ایک روایت ہے ابو حنیفہ سے اس لئے کہ اس سے کم میں مزید احتمال ہے پس شاید اس نے کھانا چھوڑا ہو ایک یا دو مرتبہ شبع کی وجہ سے پس جب اس نے تین مرتبہ کھانا چھوڑ دیا تو یہ اس بات پر دال ہے کہ اس کی عادت بن گئی ہے اور یہ اس لئے کہ تین ایسی مدت ہے جس کو آزمائش کے لئے اور اعذار کی جانچ کے لئے مقرر کی گئی ہے جیسے خیار کی مدت میں اور بعض پسندیدہ حضرات کے واقعات میں۔ تشریح کتے کی تعلیم کی شرط یہ ہے کہ وہ تین مرتبہ شکار کو نہ کھائے اور یہ صاحبین کا مذہب ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے اس میں دو قول ہیں ایک تو یہی ہے جو صاحبین کا ہے اور دوسرا ادہہ ہے جو آئندہ آ رہا ہے کہ اس کی کوئی مقدار مقرر نہیں بلکہ یہ رائے مبتلی پر موقوف ہے۔ بہر حال مذہب اول کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر اس نے ایک یا دو مرتبہ کھانا چھوڑا ہو تو اس میں ابھی بہت سے احتمالات ہیں کہ شاید شبع کی وجہ سے نہ کھایا ہو البتہ جب اس نے تین مرتبہ تک نہیں کھایا تو یہ عادت ہو جانے کی دلیل ہے۔ اور تین کو عادت کے جانچنے میں اور دیگر اعذار میں کافی دخل ہے، حضرت موسیٰ اور حضر کے واقعہ میں بھی تین کا دخل ہے حضرت موسیٰ نے تیسری مرتبہ کے بارے میں کہ دیا تھا کہ ان سألتك عن شئ بعدها .
تنبیہ اور اس کے بہت سے نظائر ہیں، تمتعوافي داركم ثلاثة ايام، الا تكلم الناس ثلاثة ايام، اذا استأذن احدكم ثلاثا فلم يؤذن له فيرجع، من افجر فى شئ ثلاث مراتٍ فلم يربح فليشغل الى غيره، امهال مرند تین روز - اقل حیض، کثیر مقدار جو علم کے لئے علامت بن سکے
ولان الكثير هو الذى يقع امارة على العلم دون القليل والجمع هو الكثير وادناه الثلث فقد ربها وعندابي حنيفة على ماذكر في الأصل يثبت التعليم مالم يغلب على ظن الصائد انه معلم ولا يقدر بالثلث لان المقادير لا تعرف اجتهادا بل نصاوسما عاولا سمع فيفوض الى رأى المبتلى به كما هو اصله في جنسها
ترجمہ اور اس لئے کہ کثیر وہ مقدار ہے جو علم کی علامت بن سکتی ہے نہ کہ قلیل اور جمع ہی کثیر ہے اور جمع کا نمبر درجہ تین ہے تو علم کو تین کے ساتھ مقدر کیا جائے گا اور ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک مبسوط میں ذکر کردہ روایت کے مطابق تعلیم ثابت نہ ہوگی جب تک کہ شکاری کے گمان پر یہ بات غالب نہ آجائے کہ یہ معلم ہے اور تین مرتبہ کے ساتھ مقدر نہیں کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ مقادیر اجتہاد سے نہیں پہچانی جاتی بلکہ نص اور سماع سے اور کوئی سمع نہیں ہے تو اس کو رائے مبتلی پر چھوڑ دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ ابو حنیفہ کا یہی قاعدہ ہے اس جیسے مسائل ہیں۔
تشریح تین مرتبہ کھانا چھوڑنے کی اور دلیل فرماتے ہیں کہ کسی علم کی علامت قلیل نہ ہونا بلکہ کثیر ہونا ہے اور جمع کثیر ہے نہ کہ قلیل لہذا جمع کا اقل فرد تین کو لے لیا گیا اس کو علم کی علامت قرار دیا گیا ہے مبسوط میں مذکور ہے کہ امام صاحب کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ اس میں تین مرتبہ کی تحدید نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ تو رائے مبتلی پر چھوڑ دیا گیا کیونکہ مقادیر میں قیاسی بندیاں کام نہیں کر سکتیں بلکہ یہاں نص ضروری ہے اور نص موجود نہیں تو اس کو امام صاحب کے اصول کے مطابق رائے مبتلی پر چھوڑ دیا جائے گا۔
ثمرہ اختلاف اور صاحبین کی دلیل
وعلى الرواية الأولى عنده يحل ما اصطاده ثلثا وعندهما لا يحل لإنه انما يصير معلما بعد تمام الثلث وقبل التعليم غير معلم فكان الثالث صيد كلب جاهل وصار كالتصرف المباشر في سكوت المولى وله انه اية تعليمه عنده فكان هذاصيد جارحة معلمة بخلاف تلك المسالة لان الاذن اعلام ولا يتحقق دون علم العبد وذلك بعد المباشرة
ترجمہ اور ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک پہلی روایت کے مطابق وہ شکار حلال ہے جو کتے نے تیسری مرتبہ میں شکار کیا ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ معلم ہو گا تین مرتبہ کے بعد اور تعلیم سے پہلے وہ غیر معلم ہے تو تیسرا ہوگا جاہل کتے کا شکار اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے وہ تصرف جو کیا جائے مولیٰ کے سکوت کے وقت میں اور ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ (ترک اکل) کتے کی تعلیم کی علامت ہے ثالث کے وقت تو ہوگا یہ (ثالث) معلم کتے کا شکار بخلاف اس مسئلہ کے اس لئے اذن آگاہ کرتا ہے اور آگاہ کرنا متفق نہ ہو السلام کے جانے کے بغیر اور غلام کا جاننا مباشرت کے بعد ہے۔
تشریح مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کی اس پہلی روایت کے مطابق جو صاحبین کے ساتھ ہے اس میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک تیسری مرتبہ کا پکڑا ہوا شکار حلال ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک تیسری مرتبہ کا پکڑا ہوا شکار حلال نہیں ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کتا معلم ہوتا ہے تین مرتبہ مکمل کرنے کے بعد اور تعلیم سے پہلے کنا غیر معلم تھا تو تیسری مرتبہ میں ثلاث مکمل نہیں ہوئے لہذا ابھی اس شکار کو جاہل کتے کا شکار شمار کیا جائے گا اور اس کی مثال بعینہ یہ ہے کہ غلام کو مولیٰ نے دیکھا کہ وہ مال فروخت کرتا ہے اور مولیٰ نے اس پر سکوت اختیار کیا تو مولیٰ کا سکوت اجازت ہے اور اب غلام ماذون له في التجارة بن جائے گا لیکن یہ بیع جواب ہوتی ہوئی مولیٰ نے دیکھی وہ لازم نہ ہو گی ۔ یہاں تک کہ مولیٰ کو اس کے توڑنے کا حق ہے تو صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ جس طرح یہ ہے اسی طرح تیسری مرتبہ کا شکار ہے کہ وہ حلال نہ ہو گا البتہ ثالث کے بعد جو شکار کرے گا وہ حلال ہوگا۔ امام ابوحنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب کتے نے تیسری بار کھانا چھوڑ دیا تو اب یہ معلم ہو گیا اور یہ شکار کلب معلم کا شکار ہے اور رہا وہ مسئلہ جس کو صاحبین نے استدلال میں پیش کیا ہے تو اس سے استدلال اس لئے درست نہیں ہے کہ اذن مولی کی حقیقت اعلام ہے اور آگاہ کرنا
Kitab al-Sayd. Ashraf al-Hidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Vol. 14. (Arabic:) The possibility: perhaps he left it once or twice due to satiety; when he leaves it three times, it indicates it became a habit for him. And this is because three is a period set for trial and testing excuses, as in the period of option (al-khiyar) and in some stories of the chosen ones. (Urdu translation:) Then Quduri conditioned three times leaving, and this is according to the Sahibayn, and this is one narration from Abu Hanifa. Because less than that contains more possibility; perhaps he left food once or twice due to satiety. When he left it three times, it indicates that it became his habit. And this is because three is a period set for testing and checking excuses, as in the period of option and in some stories of the beloved figures. Commentary: The condition of the dog's training is that it does not eat the prey three times. This is the madhhab of the Sahibayn, and Imam Abu Hanifa has two sayings: one is that of the Sahibayn, and the other is coming later that no specific number is fixed; rather it is left to the opinion of the one who is tested. In any case, the proof of the first view is: if it left once or twice, there are still many possibilities that perhaps it did not eat due to satiety; but when it does not eat up to three times, that is evidence that it became a habit. And three has a decisive role in testing habit and other excuses; in the story of Musa and Khidr, three also had a role; Musa said about the third time: 'If I ask you about anything after this...' (Quran). Warning: And there are many similar instances: 'Enjoy in your house three days' (Quran), 'Do not speak to people for three days' (Quran), 'If one of you asks permission three times and is not given permission, let him return' (Hadith), 'Whoever fails in something three times and does not profit, let him turn to something else' (saying), 'Three days for the postponement of a decree', 'Minimum of menses' (qa'ida). A large amount that can be a sign for knowledge. (Arabic:) And because the large (kathir) is that which serves as an indication for knowledge, not the small (qalil), and the plural (jami') is the large, and its minimum is three; so it has been set. And according to Abu Hanifa, as mentioned in al-Asl, the training is established as long as the hunter's dominant assumption is that it is trained, and it is not quantified by three. Because quantities are not known by ijtihad but by text and tradition, and there is no text, so it is referred to the opinion of the one tested, as is his principle in similar matters. (Urdu:) And because the large amount is that which can be a sign for knowledge, not the small, and the plural is the large, and the smallest number for plural is three, so knowledge is measured by three. According to Abu Hanifa, as per the narration mentioned in al-Mabsut, training is not established until the hunter's dominant assumption is that it is trained, and it is not quantified by three. Because quantities are not recognized by ijtihad, only by text and hearing; and there is no hearing (text), so it is left to the opinion of the one tested, as is Abu Hanifa's principle in such matters. Commentary: The proof for three times leaving is that the sign of knowledge is not the small but the large, and the plural is large, not small; therefore the minimum of plural is taken as three and made a sign of knowledge. It is mentioned in al-Mabsut that Imam Sahib's madhhab is that there is no definite limit of three times; rather it is left to the opinion of the one tested because quantities cannot be determined by qiyas, rather a text is necessary, and there is no text, so according to Imam Sahib's principle it is left to the opinion of the one tested. (Arabic:) Fruit of disagreement and the proof of the Sahibayn: And according to the first narration from him (Abu Hanifa), what he catches on the third time is lawful, but according to them (Sahibayn) it is not lawful. Because he only becomes trained after completion of three, and before training he is untrained; so the third (catch) is the catch of an ignorant dog, and it becomes like direct action in the silence of the master. And for him (Abu Hanifa), it (leaving prey) is a sign of his training at the time of the third, so this is a catch of a trained bird of prey. Unlike that case (of the slave) because permission is an announcement, and it is not realized without the knowledge of the slave, and that is after the direct action. (Urdu translation:) According to Abu Hanifa's first narration, the prey caught on the third time is halal. According to the Sahibayn, it is not halal because he becomes trained after three times, and before training he is untrained, so the third is the catch of an ignorant dog, like that transaction done in the silence of the master. Abu Hanifa's proof is that this (leaving food) is a sign of training at the time of the third, so this (third) is the catch of a trained dog. Unlike that case because permission is an announcement, and it is not realized without the slave's knowledge, and that is after the direct action. Commentary: The author says that according to Imam Abu Hanifa's first narration, which is with the Sahibayn, the prey caught on the third time is halal for him. According to the Sahibayn, the prey caught on the third time is not halal. Their proof is that the dog becomes trained after completing three times; before training it was untrained, so on the third time three are not completed, so this prey is considered the catch of an ignorant dog. The example is that of a slave whom the master saw selling goods and the master remained silent: the master's silence is permission, and the slave becomes authorized to trade, but that particular sale seen by the master is not binding; the master has the right to annul it. Similarly, the Sahibayn say the third prey is not halal; after the third, what he catches will be halal. Imam Abu Hanifa says that when the dog left food for the third time, it became trained, and this prey is the catch of a trained dog. As for the case presented by the Sahibayn, it is not analogous because permission is an announcement, and announcement does not occur without the knowledge of the slave, and that is after the direct action.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, a commentary on al-Hidayah, discusses the condition for a hunting dog to be considered trained (mu'allam). The central issue is how many times the dog must leave the prey uneaten before it is deemed trained. The text presents a dispute between the two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the Sahibayn and one narration from Abu Hanifa, the dog must leave the prey untouched three consecutive times. The reasoning is that a single or double occurrence may be due to chance (e.g., fullness), but three times establishes a pattern. This principle is supported by various textual precedents where the number three marks a threshold: the period of option (khiyar), the three days of warning in the Quranic story of Moses and Khidr, the three-day period for guests (Quran 11:65), the three-day silence of Zechariah (Quran 19:10), and the hadith about seeking permission three times. These indicate that three is the minimal number to establish a reliable indication. The second narration from Abu Hanifa (mentioned in al-Mabsut) holds that no fixed number is required; rather, the training is determined by the hunter's dominant assumption. This is because quantities cannot be known through ijtihad but require a clear text, and since no such text exists, the matter is left to the hunter's assessment. The practical consequence (thamarat al-khilaf) is highlighted: according to Abu Hanifa's first narration, the third catch is halal, while according to the Sahibayn, it is not halal because the dog is only considered fully trained after completing three times. Abu Hanifa argues that the third act of leaving itself is a sign of training, so the third catch is from a trained dog. The Sahibayn analogize this to a slave's transaction witnessed by the master: the master's silence implies permission, but that specific transaction is not binding until after the act. Abu Hanifa responds that permission is an announcement that requires the slave's knowledge, which occurs only after the act, unlike the dog's training which is established by the consistent behavior.

Key Terms
تعلیم (ta'līm): The training of a hunting dog to the point where it obeys commands and does not eat the prey, making its catch halal.
ثلاث (thalāth): The number three, considered the minimum for establishing a pattern or habit in legal proofs.
کثیر (kathīr): A large quantity that can serve as a reliable sign for knowledge, contrasted with qalil (small).
رائے مبتلیٰ (rā’i al-mubtalā): The opinion of the hunter who is directly involved in testing the dog's training.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn in this passage, when is a hunting dog considered trained (mu'allam)?
A) After leaving prey once
B) After leaving prey twice
C) After leaving prey three consecutive times
D) Whenever the hunter believes it is trained

Key Takeaway
The number of times a dog must leave prey to be considered trained is disputed among Hanafi imams: three times (Sahibayn) or left to the hunter's assumption (Abu Hanifa), affecting the permissibility of the third catch.

Dispute on the Number of Refusals for Training a Hunting Dog
Intra-Hanafi dispute on threshold for trained dog status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on when a hunting dog becomes mu'allim and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The third refusal marks training according to Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn require a fourth catch.

In the Hanafi school, the threshold for a hunting dog's training (ta'leem) is a matter of debate. This passage from Al-Hidayah examines whether the dog becomes mu'allim at the third refusal or only after three refusals have been completed. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the school balances qiyas with textual limits (maqadir).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine the key question: At what point does a hunting dog become mu'allim such that its catch is halal? Specifically, is the third catch (where the dog refrains from eating for the third time) halal or not?
The dispute is between Abu Hanifa and his two students.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa (first riwaya) holds that the dog becomes mu'allim upon the third refusal, so the third catch is halal. His proof: refraining three times is a sign of knowledge; three is the minimum plural.
He argues that the dog already shows training at the third time itself.
3
State Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the dog becomes mu'allim only after completing three refusals, so the third catch is haram. Their proof: training is complete only after three full refusals; the third catch is like an action done without the owner's explicit permission (analogy to a slave selling goods in the owner's silence).
They require the fourth catch to be halal.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's response to Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa responds that the dog's refraining at the third time is itself the sign of training; the analogy to the silent owner is invalid because the owner's silence is notification (i'lam), which requires the slave's knowledge, but here the dog's action is known only after the catch.
Thus the third catch is halal because the dog's action indicates it understands the command.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the first riwaya of Abu Hanifa, as stated in the text: 'وعلى الرواية الأولى عنده.' Hence, in practice, the third catch is considered halal.
Note: Abu Hanifa also has a second riwaya that leaves the determination to the evaluator, but it is not relied upon.

Source Text

Book of Hunting. ... (faithful literal translation of the entire Arabic/Urdu passage for this lesson)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when a hunting dog becomes trained (mu'allim). ... (teacher-style commentary explaining the positions and reasoning)

Key Terms
معلم (mu'allim): A trained hunting animal (dog or bird) that refrains from eating when it catches game, indicating it is under control.
مقادير (maqadir): Fixed quantities or measurements in Shariah, such as the number of lashes or the amount of zakah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only after three complete refusals, so the third catch is haram
D) There is no fixed number; it depends on the trainer's opinion

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a hunting dog becomes mu'allim upon the third refusal, making that catch halal.

Ruling on Hunting with a Trained Animal and the Obligation of Tasmiyah
Ruling with proof: halal if tasmiyah recited at release

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers game caught by a trained dog or falcon as halal when tasmiyah is recited at release, and apply the exception for forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Sending the animal is like using a knife – tasmiyah must be at release, but forgetfulness is excused.

Hunting with trained animals is a permissible means of obtaining food, but it requires careful attention to the rules of slaughter. The Hanafi school draws a clear analogy: the act of sending the animal (irsal) is equivalent to moving a knife during slaughter, so tasmiyah must be recited at that moment. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the important exception that forgetfulness does not render the game unlawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on the permissibility of eating game caught by a trained dog or falcon. The act is sending the animal (irsal) to hunt. The ruling is halal when conditions are met.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim, which explicitly permits eating game caught by a trained dog when tasmiyah is recited at release.
This is a zanni al-thubut hadith but qati al-dalala according to Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The passage provides a rational argument (qiyas): the animal is a tool, like a knife. Slaughter is only achieved by using the tool. For a knife, usage is moving it (imrar); for an animal, usage is sending it (irsal). Therefore, tasmiyah must be at the moment of sending, analogous to reciting tasmiyah when moving the knife.
This analogy establishes that the operative moment is the release, not the catch.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require tasmiyah at the time the animal catches the game. The Hanafi position is that since the animal acts independently after release, tasmiyah at release suffices.
The cross-madhhab difference is practical: if the hunter says tasmiyah at release but forgets to repeat it later, Hanafis allow the catch; Shafi'is might not.
5
Evaluate the exception
If the hunter forgets to recite tasmiyah at release, the game remains halal. This is based on the principle that forgetfulness is excused in such matters, as established in other chapters (e.g., forgetfulness in slaughter).
Intentional omission renders the game haram, as stated in the Book of Slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا ارسل كلبه المعلم او بازيه وذكر اسم الله تعالى عند ارساله فاخذ الصيد وجرحه فمات حل اكله لما روينا من حديث عدى رضى الله عنه
ولان الكلب او البازى ألة والذبح لا يحصل بمجرد الألة الابالاستعمال وذلك فيهما بالارسال فنزل منزلة الرمى وامرار السكين فلابد من التسمية
ولوترکه ناسيــا حــل ايــضـــا عـلـى مـابيـنـاه وحرمة متروك التسمية عـامـدا فـي الـذبـائـح
And when one sends his trained dog or his trained falcon and mentions the name of Allah at the time of sending it, and it catches the game and wounds it, and it dies, then eating it is permissible, due to the hadith we narrated from 'Adi (may Allah be pleased with him). And because the dog or the falcon is a tool, and slaughter is not achieved by merely the tool except with usage, and usage in both of them is by sending, so sending is placed in the position of shooting an arrow and moving a knife, so tasmiyah is necessary. And if one leaves it forgetfully, it is also permissible according to what we have explained, and the prohibition of intentionally leaving tasmiyah is in the Book of Slaughter.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage provides the key ruling on hunting with trained animals. The author states that when a trained dog or falcon is sent with tasmiyah, the game it catches and kills is halal. The proof is the well-known hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim. The reasoning follows: the animal is a tool for slaughter, like a knife. Slaughter is only valid with actual use; for a knife, use means moving it; for a hunting animal, use means sending it (irsal). Therefore, tasmiyah must be recited at the moment of sending, analogous to shooting an arrow or cutting with a knife. The passage then adds an important exception: if one forgets to recite tasmiyah, the catch remains halal, based on the principle that forgetfulness is excused in such matters. However, intentionally omitting tasmiyah renders the meat haram, but that ruling is detailed in the Book of Slaughter (Kitab al-Dhaba'ih).

Key Terms
ارسال (irsal): Sending or releasing a hunting animal (dog or falcon) towards the game.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah' or 'Bismillah Allahu Akbar' at the time of sending the hunting animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a trained dog catches game but the hunter forgot to recite tasmiyah when releasing the dog?
A) The game is haram because tasmiyah is obligatory.
B) The game is halal because forgetfulness is excused.
C) The game is halal only if the hunter remembers to say tasmiyah after the catch.
D) The game is makruh but not haram.

Key Takeaway
A trained dog or falcon that catches and kills game while tasmiyah is recited at release yields halal meat; forgetfulness is excused, but intentional omission renders it haram.

Hunting with Trained Dog or Falcon Requires Tasmiyah
Ruling and proof for halal hunting with trained animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for halal hunting with a trained animal and explain why tasmiyah at release is wajib.

Core Idea
A trained dog or falcon is a slaughtering instrument; tasmiyah at release is wajib just like with a knife.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on hunting with trained animals. The key issue is whether the hunter must recite tasmiyah when releasing the animal. The text presents the ruling with its proof from hadith and a qiyas analogy, establishing that the trained animal is an instrument that requires active use with tasmiyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that hunting with a trained dog or falcon is halal when the hunter recites tasmiyah at the moment of release.
The ruling is explicit in the matn: 'And when one sends his trained dog or trained falcon and mentions the name of Allah at its release... its consumption is halal.'
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim (may Allah be pleased with him).
The text says 'due to what we narrated from the hadith of 'Adi'. This is a primary textual evidence (Sunnah ahad).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith directly permits consumption when tasmiyah is recited at release. The specific phrase 'mentions the name of Allah at its release' indicates the condition.
The proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala, but sufficient for the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the qiyas
The text analogizes the dog/falcon to a knife or arrow: all are instruments that require active use (istimal) for slaughter. The release (irsal) is the act of use, so tasmiyah must accompany it.
The qiyas is explicit: 'so sending takes the status of throwing an arrow and passing a knife, thus tasmiyah is necessary at the time of sending.'
5
Note the practical implication
The ruling distinguishes between a trained animal (mu'allam) and an untrained one; only trained animals qualify. The tasmiyah must be said at release, not earlier or later.
The text emphasizes 'trained dog' (kalb mu'allam) and 'trained falcon' (bazi mu'allam).

A trained dog or trained falcon is released with tasmiyah, then it goes and wounds the prey or it dies, then this prey is halal. He said: And when one sends his trained dog or his trained falcon and mentions the name of Allah at its release, then it catches the prey and wounds it, then it dies, its consumption is halal due to what we narrated from the hadith of 'Adi (may Allah be pleased with him). Translation: Al-Quduri said: And when the hunter releases his trained dog or trained falcon and mentions Allah at its release, then it catches the prey and wounds it, then the prey dies, its consumption is halal due to the proof which we have narrated, i.e., the hadith of 'Adi (may Allah be pleased with him). Explanation: When the hunter releases his trained dog or falcon with tasmiyah, and the dog or falcon catches the prey and wounds it, and the prey dies, then its consumption is halal, as indicated by the hadith of 'Adi. The dog and falcon are also instruments of slaughter like a knife. And because the dog or falcon is an instrument, and slaughter is not achieved by mere instrument except with use, and use in both (dog and falcon) is by sending, so sending takes the status of throwing an arrow and passing a knife, thus tasmiyah is necessary at the time of sending. Explanation: Just as a knife etc. is an instrument of slaughter, similarly dog and falcon are also instruments of slaughter, and it is obvious that a knife will not slaughter without use, even if a knife is lying and a goat falls on it and its throat is cut, it is not slaughtered because use is not found. Similarly, dog and falcon are instruments of slaughter for the hunter, and an instrument does not work without use. And the use in dog and falcon is to release them upon the prey, so releasing them upon the prey is like throwing an arrow and running a knife. So just as tasmiyah is necessary at the time of running the knife and throwing the arrow, likewise tasmiyah is necessary at the time of releasing the dog and falcon.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning hunting with trained animals. It opens with the ruling: when a hunter releases a trained dog or falcon while reciting tasmiyah (Bismillah), and the animal catches and wounds the prey, then the prey is halal even if it dies from the wound. The proof is the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim, which is cited. The commentator then explains that the dog or falcon is an instrument of slaughter (like a knife), and slaughter is only achieved through use. The use in this case is the act of releasing (irsal). This is analogized (qiyas) to throwing an arrow or running a knife, where tasmiyah at the moment of use is required. Thus, tasmiyah at release is necessary for the hunt to be halal. The explanation reinforces that mere presence of the instrument does not suffice; active release with intention is key.

Key Terms
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A dog trained for hunting that responds to command
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): Uttering 'Bismillah' (or the full tasmiyah) at the time of releasing the hunting animal
ارسال (irsal): The act of sending or releasing the hunting animal towards the prey

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the prey to be halal when hunting with a trained dog?
A) The dog must be trained and tasmiyah recited at release.
B) The dog must be trained but tasmiyah is optional.
C) Tasmiyah must be recited, but the dog need not be trained.
D) The hunter must catch the prey with his own hands.

Key Takeaway
Hunting with a trained animal requires both the animal's training and the recitation of tasmiyah at release; the animal is a slaughtering instrument.

Forgetting Tasmiyah in Hunting vs Intentional Omission
Ruling on forgetting vs intentionally omitting tasmiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetting tasmiyah when hunting and distinguish it from intentional omission.

Core Idea
Forgetting tasmiyah does not make prey haram; intentional omission does.

In hunting, the hunter must recite tasmiyah when releasing the animal. But what if he forgets? The Hanafi school distinguishes between forgetfulness and deliberate neglect. This passage gives the concise ruling, referring back to the detailed discussion in Kitab al-Dhaba'ih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is releasing a hunting animal (e.g., falcon, dog) and the omission of tasmiyah.
Tasmiyah is the recitation of 'Bismillah' at release.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
There are two cases: forgetfully omitting tasmiyah (nasiyan) and intentionally omitting it (aamidan).
The passage uses 'nasiyan' and 'aamidan'.
3
State the ruling for each
If forgetful, the prey is halal. If intentional, the prey is haram.
The exact wording: 'ولو تركه ناسيا حل أيضا' and 'وحرمة متروك التسمية عامدا'.
4
Recognize the cross-reference
The passage refers to Kitab al-Dhaba'ih for the full reasoning behind this ruling.
Kitab al-Dhaba'ih is the chapter on slaughtering.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'i scholars consider tasmiyah sunnah, so intentional omission may not render prey haram in their school.
This is a point of ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is that tasmiyah is wajib.

Source Text

عامد أمتروک التسمیہ کا حکم
ولوترکه ناسيــا حــل ايــضـــا عـلـى مـابيـنـاه وحرمة متروك التسمية عـامـدا فـي الـذبـائـح ترجمہ اور اگر تسمیہ کو بھول کر چھوڑ دیا تو بھی حلال ہے اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں اور عامد امتروک التسمیہ کی حرمت کو کتاب الذبائح میں ۔
تشریح اگر بوقت ارسال تسمیہ بھول گیا تو شکار حلال ہے اور اگر عمدا چھوڑا ہو تو شکار حرام ہے جس کی تفصیل کتاب الذبائح میں گذر چکی ہے۔
The ruling on intentionally omitted tasmiyah: And if he leaves it forgetfully, it is also halal, according to what we have explained. And the prohibition of intentionally omitted tasmiyah is in Kitab al-Dhaba'ih. Translation: And if he forgets tasmiyah, it is still halal according to the detail we have explained, and the prohibition of intentionally omitted tasmiyah is in Kitab al-Dhaba'ih. Explanation: If at the time of release he forgot tasmiyah, the prey is halal; and if he intentionally left it, the prey is haram, the detail of which has passed in Kitab al-Dhaba'ih.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is a concise ruling statement differentiating between forgetting and intentionally omitting tasmiyah at the time of releasing the hunting animal. The ruling refers back to the earlier discussion in Kitab al-Dhaba'ih for the full reasoning. The Hanafi position is clear: forgetfulness does not affect permissibility, but deliberate abandonment renders the prey haram. This is consistent with the general principle that tasmiyah is wajib, but it is excused when forgotten (nasiyan).

Key Terms
عامد ('aamid): Intentional, deliberate action
ناسی (naasi): Forgetful, unintentional
متروک التسمیہ (matrook al-tasmiyah): The one who has omitted the tasmiyah

Wounding (Jarh) is Required for Hunting in Zahir al-Riwayah
Ruling that wounding is a condition for halal hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of wounding for halal hunting and explain the concept of dhakat idtirari.

Core Idea
A hunted animal is only halal if wounded by the hunter's instrument—no wound means no proper slaughter.

In this lesson, we examine a key condition for the halal status of game captured by hunting. According to the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah, the hunting animal must inflict a wound on the prey. This wound is considered an emergency slaughter (dhakat idtirari) and must be attributable to the hunter's action. Understanding this rule helps avoid common mistakes in determining the permissibility of hunted meat.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on hunting according to Zahir al-Riwayah
Identify the condition: wounding (jarh) is necessary for the prey to be considered properly slaughtered.
This is a shart (condition) for halal status.
2
Define dhakat idtirari
Explain that emergency slaughter is realized by any wound anywhere on the prey's body, without need for throat-cutting.
Dhakat idtirari applies when normal slaughter is impossible.
3
Identify the additional requirement of attribution
The wound must be caused by the hunter's instrument through intentional use. For dogs and falcons, this means proper release (irsal).
If the instrument is not attributable, the wound is not valid.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that wounding is not required; any killing by the instrument suffices. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The relied-upon view is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Zahir al-Riwayah position (wounding required) is the authoritative position for fatwa.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کے لئے زخمی کرنا شرط ہے
ولابد من الجرح في ظاهر الرواية ليتحقق الذكاة الاضطرارى وهو الجرح فى اى موضع كان من البدن بانتساب ماوجد من الالة اليه بالاستعمال ترجمہ اور ضرور ہے بھی کر دینا ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق تا کہ ذکاۃ اضطراری تحقیق ہو جائے اور وہ زخمی کر تاہے بدن کے جس حصہ میں بھی ہو منسوب ہونے کی وجہ سے اس آلہ کے جو پایا گیا ہے شکاری کی جانب استعمال کے ساتھ ۔
تشریح یہ بات ضروری ہے کہ شکاری جانور شکار کو زخمی بھی کرے ورنہ اگر زخم نہ ہو اور جانور مر گیا ہو تو وہ حلال نہ ہوگا اور اگر زخمی کر دیا ہو اور شکار مر جائے تو حلال ہے اور اس زخم کو ذکاۃ اضطراری کا درجہ دیا جائے گا اور ذکاۃ اضطراری میں بدن کے جو نسے حصے میں بھی زخم لگے وہ کافی ہے لیکن یہ بات ضروری ہے کہ جو آلہ استعمال میں آئے وہ شکاری کی جانب منسوب ہو اور انتساب استعمال کے اعتبار سے ہو البتہ کتا اور باز بھی شکار کے لئے آلہ ہیں اس کی جانب سے استعمال یعنی ارسال ہونا چاہیے۔
Wounding is a condition for hunting: And it is necessary to wound according to Zahir al-Riwayah so that the emergency slaughter (dhakat idtirari) is realized, and it is wounding in any part of the body due to attribution of the instrument that is found to the hunter through use. Translation: And it is necessary to also wound according to Zahir al-Riwayah so that the emergency slaughter is realized, and that wounding is in any part of the body due to the attribution of the instrument that is found to the hunter through use. Explanation: It is necessary that the hunting animal also wounds the prey; otherwise if there is no wound and the animal dies, it will not be halal. And if it wounds and the prey dies, then it is halal, and that wound is given the status of emergency slaughter. In emergency slaughter, a wound in any part of the body is sufficient, but it is necessary that the instrument used is attributable to the hunter, and the attribution is by virtue of use. Indeed, the dog and falcon are also instruments for hunting; they must be used (released) from his side.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage states a condition (shart) for the halal nature of hunted game: the hunting animal must inflict a wound on the prey. This is according to Zahir al-Riwayah, which is the most authoritative narration. The wound is considered a form of emergency slaughter (dhakat idtirari), which does not require the precise throat-cutting of normal slaughter; any wound that causes death is sufficient. However, the wound must be attributable to the hunter's instrument through his intentional use. For dogs and falcons, this means the hunter must release them (irsal) with intent. If the prey dies without being wounded (e.g., from fear or being caught without injury), it is not halal.

Key Terms
ذکاۃ اضطراری (dhakat idtirari): Emergency slaughter; a method of slaughtering an animal that is otherwise unreachable, such as by hunting. It is valid if the animal is wounded in any part of the body.
جرح (jarh): Wound inflicted by the hunting instrument on the prey's body
ظاہر الروایہ (zahir al-riwayah): The most authoritative transmitted rulings of the Hanafi school, from the 6 core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter's trained dog chases a gazelle, and the gazelle dies from a heart attack without being bitten. The hunter finds it dead. What is the ruling?
A) Halal because the dog was trained and tasmiyah was said.
B) Halal because the dog caught it.
C) Haram because there was no wound.
D) Halal only if the hunter recites tasmiyah upon finding it.

Key Takeaway
For hunted game to be halal according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the prey must be wounded by the hunter's instrument, constituting dhakat idtirari.

The Linguistic Basis for Wounding in Hunting: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi linguistic dispute on the condition of jarh in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic derivation that supports the Hanafi requirement of wounding in hunting and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-interpretation.

Core Idea
The word 'jawarih' can mean both 'wounder' and 'earner' — Hanafis combine both for certainty; Abu Yusuf takes 'earner' alone.

This passage examines the Quranic basis for the Hanafi ruling that a hunting animal must wound its prey. The key word 'al-jawarih' is derived from 'jarh' (wounding), but Abu Yusuf interprets it differently. This lesson unpacks the linguistic reasoning and the principle of taking the meaning that yields certainty (al-akhdh bi al-yaqin).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contested Element
Determine what exactly is disputed. Is it the requirement of wounding (jarh) by the hunting animal?
The issue is whether jarh is a condition (shart) for the permissibility of the game.
2
Locate the Quranic Evidence
The verse 'wa ma 'allamtum min al-jawarih' (Surah al-Ma'idah: 4) is cited. Identify the specific phrase under analysis.
3
Analyze the Linguistic Derivation
The word 'jawarih' is derived from 'jarh' (wounding). Understand how this derivation is used to argue that wounding is essential.
Hanafi position: the primary meaning is 'wounder', but it also carries the meaning 'earner' (kasib).
4
Present Abu Yusuf's Alternative Interpretation
Abu Yusuf interprets 'al-jawarih' as 'kasib' (earner) alone, removing the implication of wounding.
He reverts to a different ta'wil (interpretation) that does not include wounding.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi majority responds that both meanings can be combined without contradiction, yielding certainty (yaqin) that wounding is intended.
This is a classic usul principle: when a word can bear multiple meanings without contradiction, taking all yields certainty.

وفى ظاهر قوله تعالى وما علمتم من الجوارح ما يشير الى اشتراط الجرح اذهو من الجرح بمعنى الجراحة في تاويل فيحمل على الجارح الكاسب بنابه ومخلبه ولا تنافى وفيه اخذ باليقين وعن ابى يوسف اله لايشترط رجوعا الى التاويل الأول وجوابه ما قلنا ترجمہ اور اللہ تعالیٰ کے فرمان و ما علمتم من الجوارح کے ظاہر میں ایسی بات ہے جو زخم لگانے کے شرط ہونے کی جانب مشیر ہے اس لئے کہ یہ ( جوارح ) جرح سے مشتق ہے جو جراحت کے معنی میں ہے ایک تاویل کے مطابق ۔ تو اس کو محمول کیا جائے گا اس جارح پر جو کمائی کرنے والا ہے اپنے دانت اور اپنے چنگل سے اور جمع کرنے میں کوئی منافات نہیں ہے اور اس میں یقین کو لینا ہے اور ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ زخم کرنا شرط نہیں ہے پہلی تاویل کی جانب رجوع کرتے ہوئے اور اس کا جواب وہ ہے جو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں ۔ تشریح مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ بظاہر فرمانِ باری و ما علمتم من الجوارح سے بھی جرح کے معنی ثابت ہوتے ہیں ۔ کیونکہ جو ارج جرح سے مشتق ہے جس کے معنی زخم لگانے کے ہیں اگر چہ اس کی ایک تفسیر وہ ہے جو ماقبل میں گذری ہے کہ جوارح کو اسب کے معنی میں ہے اور ایک تفسیر یہ ہے کہ جوارح کے معنی ہیں زخمی کرنے والے۔ اور اگر دونوں معنی مراد لئے جائیں تو اس میں کچھ منافات نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ شکاری جانور جیسے زخم لگانے والے ہیں اسی طرح کمائی کرنے والے بھی ہیں تو دونوں معنی کا اجتماع ممکن ہے اور اس میں یقین بھی ہے ورنہ ایک معنی مراد لینے کی صورت میں احتمال ہے کہ دوسرے معنی مراد ہوں اور جب دونوں جمع ہو گئے تو یقین حاصل ہو گیا، مگر ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ جوارح بمعنی کو اسب ہے لہذ ازخمی کرنا
And in the apparent meaning of Allah's statement 'wa ma 'allamtum min al-jawarih' there is an indication of the condition of wounding, because it is derived from jarh meaning wounding, according to one interpretation. So it is taken to mean the one who earns (al-jarih) with its fangs and claws, and there is no contradiction. And it includes taking by certainty. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that wounding is not required, reverting to the first interpretation, and its answer is what we have said. Explanation: The author says that apparently from Allah's statement 'wa ma 'allamtum min al-jawarih' the meaning of wounding is also established, because jawarih is derived from jarh meaning wounding. Although one interpretation is that jawarih means kasib (earning), and another interpretation is that it means wounding (wounding ones). If both meanings are intended, there is no contradiction because these hunting animals are both wounding and earning. So combining both meanings is possible, and there is certainty. Otherwise, if only one meaning is taken, there is probability that the other meaning is intended. So when both are combined, certainty is achieved. However, Abu Yusuf says that jawarih means kasib, hence wounding...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether wounding (jarh) is a condition for the permissibility of hunted game. The evidence is the Quranic verse 'wa ma 'allamtum min al-jawarih' (Surah al-Ma'idah: 4). The word 'al-jawarih' is derived from 'jarh' (wounding), suggesting that hunting animals are those that wound. The author argues that the word can bear both meanings (wounding and earning), and combining them yields certainty that wounding is required. However, Abu Yusuf holds that the primary meaning is 'earner' (kasib), not necessarily wounding, and thus wounding is not a condition. The Hanafi response (Jawab) is that the earlier reasoning (combining meanings) stands. This is a classic example of usul al-fiqh reasoning: interpretation of a word that can have multiple meanings, and the principle of taking the meaning that gives certainty (al-akhdh bi al-yaqin).

Key Terms
جوارح (jawarih): Hunting animals (dogs, falcons, etc.) that earn prey for humans. The word is derived from jarh (wounding).
جرح (jarh): Wound inflicted by a hunting animal's fangs or claws
تاویل (ta'wil): Interpretation of a word that is not its literal meaning, based on evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Yusuf's view that wounding is not required for hunting?
A) He relies on a different hadith.
B) He interprets the word 'al-jawarih' as meaning 'earner' (kasib), not 'wounder'.
C) He considers the verse abrogated.
D) He argues that any death caused by the animal is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires wounding for halal game based on combining the meanings of 'jawarih', while Abu Yusuf's view highlights legitimate linguistic flexibility within the madhhab.

The Necessity of Wounding in Hunting
Establishes jarh as condition with Quranic proof and Abu Yusuf's dissent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of jarh for hunting and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf, using the Quranic derivation.

Core Idea
A hunting animal must wound the prey; without jarh, the meat is haram.

Hunting with trained animals is a form of emergency slaughter. Unlike normal dhabh (throat cutting), the wound inflicted by the animal substitutes for the cut. The Hanafi school, based on the Quranic word 'jawarih', requires an actual wound. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed. In this lesson, we explore the evidence and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Condition
From the passage, wounding (jarh) is a condition for the prey to be halal. Note that this is the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah.
The condition applies even if the animal kills by suffocation or other means – without a wound, it is not halal.
2
Trace the Quranic Proof
The verse in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:4) uses 'jawarih'. The Hanafi scholars derive the requirement of wounding from the root 'jarh' meaning wound. They argue the word can mean both 'earners' and 'wounders', and combining both gives certainty.
Dalala is zanni because of the linguistic possibility, but combining meanings gives a stronger proof.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf held that wounding is not a condition, interpreting 'jawarih' solely as 'earners'. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require wounding. The passage answers Abu Yusuf by insisting on the derivation from jarh.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; both positions are based on the same verse.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that wounding is necessary. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, supported by Al-Hidayah and later commentaries.
This is the position for fatwa today; Abu Yusuf's view is not acted upon.

In emergency dhabh, a wound in any part of the body suffices, but it is necessary that the tool used is attributed to the hunter, and the attribution is by use; the cheetah and falcon are also tools for hunting, and their use is to be sent. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that wounding is not a condition, reverting to the first interpretation. And its answer is what we have stated. But Abu Yusuf says 'jawarih' means earners, so wounding is not a condition, but the answer to him is what we have stated.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from Hidayah commentary establishes that wounding (jarh) is a necessary condition for hunting to be valid in the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah). The ruling: if a hunting animal (dog, cheetah, falcon) kills prey without wounding, it is not halal; but if it wounds and the prey dies, it is halal. The wound can be in any part of the body, and the tool must be attributed to the hunter through use (e.g., sending the animal). The proof is from Quran 5:4, where 'jawarih' (hunting animals) is derived from 'jarh' (wounding). The author argues that the word can mean both 'earners' and 'wounders', and combining both gives certainty. Abu Yusuf holds that wounding is not a condition, interpreting 'jawarih' as 'earners' only. The author rejects this by noting the derivation supports wounding. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that wounding is necessary.

Key Terms
جوارح (jawarih): Hunting animals such as dogs, cheetahs, and falcons. The term is derived from 'jarh' (wounding), implying they cause injuries.
جرح (jarh): A wound inflicted by the hunting animal on the prey, necessary for the prey to be considered halal.
ذكاة اضطراري (dhakah idtirari): Emergency slaughter, applicable when normal throat cutting is not possible, such as in hunting. The wound inflicted by the animal substitutes for the cut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The animal must be sent with the name of Allah.
B) The animal must wound the prey.
C) The prey must be killed instantly.
D) The animal must not eat any part of the prey.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that a hunting animal wounds the prey; without jarh, the meat is haram, and this is the mufta bihi despite Abu Yusuf's contrary view.

The Ruling When a Hunting Animal Eats from Its Prey: Dog vs. Falcon
Distinguishes between dog/cheetah and falcon, with hadith evidence and madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prey from which a dog/cheetah eats versus a falcon, and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute with Malik and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A trained dog eating from prey makes it haram; a falcon eating does not, based on nature of training and hadith.

This lesson examines a key ruling in hunting law: the distinction between dogs/cheetahs and falcons when they eat from the prey. While the Hanafi school holds that a dog eating renders the prey impermissible, the falcon's eating is overlooked due to its natural behavior. The proof lies in the hadith of 'Adi, which also serves as evidence against the opposing views of Imam Malik and the old opinion of al-Shafi'i. We also explore the implications for multiple catches and intra-Hanafi differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each animal
From the matn: if a dog or cheetah eats from the prey, it is not eaten; if a falcon eats, it is eaten.
Note the immediate contrast between land predators and birds of prey.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of 'Adi bin Hatim: 'If the dog eats from it, do not eat.' This is the primary evidence.
The hadith is explicit and considered sahih by Hanafi scholars.
3
Analyse the distinction (farq)
The difference lies in 'dalalat al-ta'lim' (indication of training). A trained dog is expected not to eat, while a falcon naturally eats; eating for a dog indicates lack of training.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why the rules differ.
4
Map the inter-madhhab dispute
Hanafi school holds it is haram based on the hadith; Malik and al-Shafi'i (old opinion) consider it permissible. The hadith is presented as a direct rebuttal.
The dispute is over the interpretation of the hadith and the definition of training.
5
Examine the scenario of multiple catches and intra-Hanafi difference
If a previously trained dog catches several prey without eating, then eats from one, that prey is haram and subsequent prey are also haram until retrained. Regarding prey secured in the house, Abu Hanifa says haram, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say not haram.
The intra-Hanafi difference hinges on the status of the prey after the dog has been secured.

ولوانه صاد صيودا ولم ياكل منها ثم اكل من صيد لا يوكل هذا الصيد لانه علامة الجهل ولا يصيده بعده حتى يصير معلما على اختلاف الروايات كما بيناها في الابتداء
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے نے چنہ شکار کئے اور ان میں سے کھایا نہیں پھر ایک شکار میں سے اس نے کھالیا تو یہ شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ کھانا جہل کی علامت ہے اور نہیں کھایا جائے گا وہ شکار جو وہ بعد میں کرے یہاں تک کہ وہ معلم ہو جائے روایات کے اختلاف کے مطابق جیسا کہ ہم ان روایات کو شروع میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تشریح کتے نے چند شکار پکڑے اور ان میں سے کھایا نہیں جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ کلب معلم ہے پھر بعد میں اس نے ایک شکار پکڑا اور اس میں سے اس نے کھالیا تو اب یہ شکر کھایا نہیں جائے گا اور اسی طرح جب تک کہ وہ معلم نہ ہو جائے جب تک بعد کے شکار بھی جائز نہ ہوں گے کیونکہ جب اس نے شکار میں سے کھا لیا تو معلوم ہوا کہ یہ کتا جاہل ہے اور معلم ہونے کی وہی تفصیلات ہیں جو ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہیں ۔
پہلے پکڑے ہوئے شکاروں کا حکم ، اقوال فقہاء
واما الصيود التى اخذها من قبل فما أكل منها لا تظهر الحرمة فيه لا نعدام الحيلة وما ليس بمحرز بان كان في المفازة بان لم يظفر صاحبه بعد تثبت الحرمة فيه بالاتفاق وما هو محرز فى بيته يحرم عنده خلافا لهما
He said: If the dog or cheetah eats from it, it is not eaten; and if the falcon eats from it, it is eaten. And the difference is what we explained in the indication of training, and it is supported by what we narrated from the hadith of 'Adi (may Allah be pleased with him), and it is a proof against Malik and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion permitting what a dog ate from. Translation: Quduri said: So if the dog or cheetah eats from the prey, the prey is not eaten; and if the falcon eats from it, it is eaten. And the difference is what we explained concerning the indication of training. And it is supported by the hadith of 'Adi which we narrated, and it is a proof against Malik and al-Shafi'i in their old opinion of permitting the prey from which the dog ate. Explanation: If the dog or cheetah eats from the prey, eating it is not permissible; and if the falcon eats, eating it is permissible. The detailed explanation has preceded. And the hadith of 'Adi supports it. Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i's old opinion is that if a dog ate from the prey, eating it is permissible; the hadith of 'Adi is evidence against both, in which it is explicitly mentioned that if it eats from it, do not eat. And if the dog caught several prey and then ate from one, that prey is not eaten. And if it caught prey and did not eat from them, then ate from one prey, this prey is not eaten because it is a sign of ignorance, and it will not hunt afterwards until it becomes trained, according to different narrations as we explained at the beginning. Translation: And if the dog caught several prey and did not eat from them, then ate from one prey, this prey is not eaten because eating is a sign of ignorance, and the prey it catches afterwards will not be eaten until it becomes trained, according to the difference of narrations as we explained at the beginning. Explanation: The dog caught several prey and did not eat from them, indicating it is trained; then later it caught one prey and ate from it, so this prey is not eaten, and similarly subsequent prey are not permissible until it becomes trained again, because eating indicates the dog is ignorant. And the ruling for prey caught before: As for the prey it took before, those from which it did not eat, the prohibition does not manifest due to lack of means; and what is not secured (in a house) if in the desert and the owner has not yet recovered it, its prohibition is established by consensus; and what is secured in its house is prohibited according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from Hidayah commentary deals with the ruling if a hunting animal eats from the prey. The matn from Quduri states: if a dog or cheetah eats from the prey, it is not eaten; if a falcon eats, it is eaten. The distinction is based on the indication of training: a trained dog/cheetah is expected not to eat, while a falcon by nature eats. The proof is from the hadith of 'Adi bin Hatim (may Allah be pleased with him), where the Prophet ﷺ instructed: if the dog eats from the prey, do not eat. This hadith is evidence against Malik and al-Shafi'i's old opinion that if a dog eats, it is still permissible. Then the passage discusses a scenario where a previously trained dog, which caught several prey without eating, later eats from one prey. That specific prey becomes haram because eating is a sign of ignorance (lack of training). Furthermore, any subsequent prey caught before the dog is retrained are also haram. Finally, regarding prey caught by the dog before it ate, those are halal if the dog did not eat from them. However, if the prey was not secured (i.e., in the desert and not recovered by the owner), its prohibition is established by consensus. If the prey is secured in the owner's house, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it is prohibited (because the dog eating indicates it is not trained), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it is not prohibited. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that if a dog or cheetah eats from the prey, it becomes haram, following the hadith.

Key Terms
معلم (mu'allam): A hunting animal that is trained to hunt without eating the prey.
جاہل (jahil): An untrained hunting animal that eats from the prey, making the prey haram.
باز (baz): Falcon or hawk used for hunting. Its eating from the prey does not render the prey haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a trained dog eats from the prey, the prey is:
A) Halal
B) Haram
C) Makruh
D) Depends on the amount eaten

Key Takeaway
A trained dog or cheetah eating from prey renders it haram; a falcon eating does not, due to the different nature of training and the explicit hadith.

Dispute on Previously Caught Preserved Game
Explains three categories of previously caught game and the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over game previously caught and preserved at home, and evaluate the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn based on their arguments.

Core Idea
When a hunter eats from previously preserved game, Abu Hanifa declares the rest haram; the Sahibayn uphold the earlier ijtihad and declare it halal.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling on game previously caught by a trained animal and stored at home. The key question: if the hunter later eats from this preserved game, does that retroactively prove his earlier ignorance and render the remaining game haram? The answer hinges on whether the core of training (ḥirfa) can be forgotten and whether a later ijtihad can overturn an earlier one.

Guided Path
1
Categorise the game
Identify the three categories of previously caught game: (1) already eaten, (2) lost in the wilderness, (3) preserved at home. Note the ruling for each: first category has no prohibition, second is unanimously haram, third is disputed.
This classification frames the dispute.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that preserved game becomes haram if the hunter later eats from it. His proof: eating indicates the hunter was ignorant from the start because the essence of training (ḥirfa) is never forgotten. The earlier abstention was due to fullness, not knowledge.
He analogises to a judge changing his ijtihad before passing judgment.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that preserved game remains halal. Their proof: eating does not necessarily indicate ignorance; training may be forgotten. The earlier ijtihad had already established permissibility, and a later equal ijtihad cannot overturn it because the purpose (preservation) was achieved.
They preserve the earlier ruling unless the game was not properly secured.
4
Analyse the analogies
Compare the analogies: Abu Hanifa uses a judge changing his mind before judgment; the Sahibayn use a judge's final ruling that cannot be reversed by a later change. Consider which analogy better fits the case where the hunter changes his ijtihad before consumption.
The dispute is about whether the purpose was achieved by preservation alone or only by consumption.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if a hunter later eats from preserved game, the remaining game is haram for Abu Hanifa and halal for the Sahibayn. This affects real-world fatwas.
Later Hanafi practice often relies on the Sahibayn's view in cases of doubt.

اور وہ بھی حاصل نہیں ہوا ہے۔
Book of Hunting. Ashraf al-Bidaya Urdu commentary on al-Hidaya volume 14. Translation: And in any case, the game which he previously caught: among them, that which was eaten, then no prohibition appears due to the absence of muḥilliyya?, and that which is not preserved in the sense that it is in the wilderness, and the owner has not yet gained control over it, then in it there is unanimous prohibition. And the game which is preserved in his house is according to Abu Hanifa forbidden; there is disagreement by the two companions. Explanation: Now the issue of those games which he previously caught: what is their ruling? It is said: first they are divided into three types: 1) that which has been eaten, 2) that which has not yet been obtained, lying somewhere in the forest, 3) that which is in the house not yet eaten. For the first type, due to the absence of muḥilliyya, the ruling of prohibition does not appear. And the second type is unanimously forbidden. And the third type is disputed: Imam Abu Hanifa deems it impermissible, and the two companions deem it permissible. The proofs of the two parties are mentioned. The proof of the two companions: They say that eating does not indicate ignorance in what has passed, because training may be forgotten, and because in that which he has preserved, the ruling of permissibility has already been passed by ijtihad, so it is not nullified by an ijtihad like it, because the purpose has been achieved by the first, unlike that which is not preserved, because the purpose is not fully achieved due to its remaining as game in one sense and lack of preservation in another sense, so we declared it forbidden out of precaution. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it is a sign of his ignorance from the beginning, because the essence of training is not forgotten. So when he ate, it became clear that his leaving eating was due to fullness, not knowledge, and the change of ijtihad occurred before the achievement of the purpose because it is by eating, so it became like the change of a judge's ijtihad before passing judgment. Translation: The two companions say that eating does not indicate ignorance regarding the game that has passed, because training may be forgotten, and because in that game which the hunter has preserved, the ruling of permissibility has been passed by ijtihad, so that ruling is not nullified by a similar ijtihad, because the purpose has been achieved by the first, unlike the unpreserved game, because the purpose is not fully achieved due to its remaining as game in one sense and lack of preservation in another sense, so we declared it forbidden out of precaution. And Abu Hanifa's proof is that eating is a sign of ignorance from the beginning, because the essence of training is not forgotten. So when he ate, it became clear that his leaving eating was due to fullness, not knowledge, and the change of ijtihad before achieving the purpose is because the achievement of purpose is by eating, so it is like the change of a judge's ijtihad before passing judgment. Explanation: This disputed matter: the proof of the two companions is that sometimes training slips from the mind and is forgotten, but this is not evidence that he was ignorant in the earlier game he caught. Therefore, the game that is preserved in his house is halal. Also, one ijtihad does not invalidate another ijtihad, and in the first game, the ruling of permissibility had already been effective, so now the second ijtihad will not end that ruling of permissibility. Because the purpose is preservation, and this purpose was achieved by the first ijtihad. As for the game that has not come under control and is not preserved, it is still game and the purpose has not been achieved, so out of precaution it is declared forbidden. Imam Abu Hanifa responded: When he ate, this is his proof that he was ignorant from the beginning, and training is never forgotten (as is apparent), so his eating is proof that his earlier leaving of eating was not due to knowledge but due to fullness, and regarding the issue of ijtihad you raised, its answer is that before achieving the purpose, if ijtihad changes, the earlier ijtihad's ruling is nullified, like when a judge's ijtihad changes before passing judgment, and the purpose of hunting is eating, and that has not been achieved.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding game previously caught by a trained hunting animal. The text first divides such game into three categories: (1) already eaten, (2) lost in the wilderness, (3) preserved at home. The first category shows no prohibition because the act of eating itself does not prove the hunter's ignorance retroactively. The second category is unanimously haram because the game was never properly secured. The third category is disputed: Abu Hanifa rules it haram, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule it halal. The Sahibayn argue that eating does not indicate ignorance because training can be forgotten, and that the earlier ijtihad of preservation had already established the game as halal, so a later ijtihad cannot reverse it. They analogize to a judge's ruling that stands even if his ijtihad changes later. Abu Hanifa responds that the essence of training is never forgotten, so the hunter's eating shows he was ignorant from the start; his earlier abstention was due to being full, not knowledge. He further argues that the change of ijtihad occurred before the purpose (consumption) was achieved, similar to a judge changing his opinion before passing judgment. The commentary then explains each side's reasoning in detail. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that if a hunter later eats from preserved game, Abu Hanifa would consider the remaining game impure, while the Sahibayn would consider it permissible.

Key Terms
حرفت (ḥirfa): The trained skill of the hunting animal, e.g., a dog or falcon's knowledge of hunting.
اجتہاد (ijtihād): The juristic reasoning used to determine the permissibility of the game based on the animal's behavior.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the game preserved at home not rendered haram when the hunter later eats from it?
A) Because the hunter's eating shows he was knowledgeable
B) Because the earlier ijtihad establishing permissibility cannot be overturned by a later ijtihad
C) Because training is never forgotten
D) Because the game was already consumed

Key Takeaway
The dispute hinges on whether the essence of training (ḥirfa) can be forgotten and whether a change of ijtihad before the purpose is achieved nullifies the earlier ruling.

Ruling on Game Caught by an Escaped Falcon
States ruling that game caught by a falcon that escaped and later returned is not eaten

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game caught by an escaped falcon and explain the qiyas (analogy) to a dog that eats from the game.

Core Idea
A falcon that escapes and stays away loses its trained knowledge; its catch is haram, just like a dog that eats from the game.

In the Hanafi school, the permissibility of game caught by a trained animal depends on the animal's reliability. This passage addresses a specific case: a trained falcon that escapes from its owner and remains absent. The ruling is based on analogy (qiyas) to a dog that eats from the game, both indicating loss of training. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the criteria for lawful hunting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A falcon escapes, stays for a while, then catches game. The ruling: the game is not eaten (haram).
The phrase 'لا يوكل صيده' clearly states the game is not to be eaten.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) to a hunting dog that eats from the game. The text says 'كالكلب اذا اكل من الصيد' (like the dog when it eats from the game).
The original case is the dog eating from the game, which is already established as causing the dog to be judged ignorant.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The common effective cause (illa) is that the animal has lost its trained knowledge (ḥirfa). For the dog, eating from the game indicates it is no longer reliable; for the falcon, fleeing and staying away shows it has abandoned its training.
The illa is extracted via tanqih al-manat: both actions demonstrate a departure from the state of being 'ālim (knowledgeable).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shāfiʿī) may have different criteria for training. The Ḥanafī position relies on observable behavior as evidence.
The text acknowledges ikhtilāf by labelling the case as mukhtalaf fīh.
5
Apply the ruling
If a falcon escapes and does not return despite being called, it is deemed jāhil (ignorant). Any game it catches thereafter is haram.
The commentary emphasizes that even if the owner calls, the falcon's refusal to return seals its loss of knowledge.

شکرہ کب جاہل سمجھا جائے گا
ولو ان صقرافر من صاحبه فمكث حينا ثم صادلا يوكل صيده لانه ترک ماصار به عالما فيحكم بجهله كالكب اذا اكل من الصيد
ترجمہ اور اگر شکرہ اپنے مالک کے پاس سے بھاگا پس تھوڑی دیر ٹھبرا پھر اس نے شکار کیا تو اس کا شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس کئے کہ اس نے وہ چیز چھوڑ دی جس کی وجہ سے وہ عالم ہوتا ہے تو اس کی جہالت کا حکم دیا جائے گا جیسے کتا جبکہ وہ شکار میں سے کھالے۔ تشریح شکرہ اپنے مالک کے ہاتھ سے اڑ گیا اور ہر چند مالک نے بلا یا مگر وہ نہیں آیا تو اب اس کی جہالت کا حکم دیا جائے گالہذا اگر اس نے اب کوئی شکار مارا تو وہ حلال نہ ہو گا جس کا تفصیلی بیان گزر چکا ہے۔
When is a falcon considered ignorant? And if a falcon (saqr) flees from its owner, stays for a while, then catches game, its game is not eaten because it left that by which it became knowledgeable, so it is judged ignorant like the dog when it eats from the game. Translation: And if a falcon flees from its owner, stays for a while, then catches game, its game will not be eaten because it left the thing by which it becomes knowledgeable, so it is judged ignorant like a dog when it eats from the game. Explanation: The falcon flew from its owner's hand, and even though the owner called, it did not come, so it is now judged ignorant. Therefore, if it catches any game now, it will not be halal, as has been explained in detail before.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a trained falcon (shāhīn) that escapes from its owner and remains absent for some time before catching game. The ruling is that such game is not to be eaten because the falcon's escape and refusal to return indicate it has lost its trained knowledge (ḥirfa). The proof is an analogy (qiyās) to a hunting dog that eats from the game: just as a dog that eats from the game is judged ignorant and its catch is haram, similarly a falcon that abandons its training by fleeing becomes ignorant, and its catch is haram. The commentary explains that if the falcon flies away and does not return despite being called, it is deemed ignorant, so any game it catches thereafter is not halal.

Key Terms
صقر (saqr): A trained falcon or hunting bird.
عالم / جاهل (ʿālim / jāhil): A trained animal is 'knowledgeable' (ʿālim) meaning it reliably hunts for the owner; if it loses that, it becomes 'ignorant' (jāhil) and its catch is not permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the falcon was not called back
D) Because the game belongs to someone else

Key Takeaway
A falcon that escapes and stays away loses its trained status; its catch is haram, analogous to a dog that eats from the game.

Game is Halal When a Trained Dog Drinks Its Blood
States ruling that game is lawful if a trained dog drinks its blood but does not eat the meat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a trained dog drinks the blood of game but does not eat its meat, and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) based on the dog's training.

Core Idea
A trained dog's catch is halal if it drinks the blood but leaves the meat — this proves its extreme training.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario in hunting with trained dogs: what if the dog drinks the blood of the game but does not eat the meat? The source passage resolves this by looking at the dog's behavior as evidence of its advanced training (ghāyat ʿilmihi). This ruling is an example of how the Hanafi school uses qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) to derive rulings from the general principle that a trained dog's catch is halal as long as it does not consume the meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a trained dog catching game and drinking its blood without eating any meat. The ruling is that the game is halal (permissible) to eat.
Note that the dog must be trained (mu'allam) for this ruling.
2
Identify the proof
The proof is the dog's extreme training (ghāyat ʿilmihi), shown by its discernment between what is suitable for the owner (meat) and what is not (blood). It drank the blood (impermissible for the owner) and retained the meat (permissible).
The dog's action is evidence that it is not eating for itself but retaining for the owner.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The asl (original case) is a trained dog that does not eat any part of the game — the game is halal. The far' (new case) is when the dog drinks blood but does not eat meat. The illa (effective cause) is the dog's extreme training, which makes it reliable. Therefore, the far' receives the same ruling: halal.
The distinction between drinking blood and eating meat is crucial: eating indicates the dog acted for itself, drinking blood does not.
4
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that any consumption by the dog, including blood, invalidates the catch. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the dog's training and the nature of what is consumed.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position relies on a nuanced reading of the dog's behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتے نے شکار کا خون پی لیا تو شکار کھایا جائیگا
ولو شرب الكلب من دم الصيد ولم ياكل منه اكل لانه ممسك للصيد عليه وهذا من غاية علمه حيث شرب مالا يصلح لصاحبه و امسك عليه ما يصلح له
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے نے شکار کا خون پی لیا اور شکار میں سے کچھ نہیں کھایا تو اس کو کھایا جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ کتا شکار کو مالک کے لئے روکنے والا اور یہ کتے کی انتہائی علم کی بات ہے کہ جو چیز اس کے مالک کیلئے درست نہیں ہے اس کو پی لیا اور جو چیز اس کے لئے صالح ہے اس کو اس کے لئے روک لیا۔ تشریح کتے نے فقط شکار کا خون پی لیا اور گوشت نہیں کھایا تو وہ شکار حلال ہے کیونکہ اس سے تو کتے کی مزید مہارت معلوم ہوتی ہے اور سیہ اس کی پی ایچ ڈی کی ڈگری حاصل ہونے کی علامت ہے کہ جو چیز مالک کیلئے حلال ہے اس کو روک لیا اور جو مالک کے لئے حرام اس کو پی لیا۔
If a dog drinks the blood of the game and does not eat from it, the game is eaten because the dog is a retainer of the game for the owner, and this is from its extreme knowledge (training) because it drank what is not suitable for its owner and retained what is suitable for him. Translation: And if the dog drinks the blood of the game and does not eat any of it, then it will be eaten because the dog retains the game for the owner, and this is from its extreme knowledge: it drank what is not suitable for its owner and retained what is suitable for him. Explanation: The dog only drank the blood of the game and did not eat the meat, so that game is halal, because this shows the dog's advanced skill, and it is a sign of its perfect training that it retained what is halal for the owner and drank what is haram for the owner.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that if a trained hunting dog drinks the blood of the game but does not eat its meat, the game is lawful to eat. The proof is that the dog's action demonstrates its excellent training: it distinguishes between what is permissible for the owner (the meat) and what is impermissible (blood), so it consumes the blood and preserves the meat. This shows the dog's extreme knowledge (ghāyat ʿilmihi), meaning it is fully trained and reliable. The commentary emphasizes that this behavior is a sign of the dog's perfected skill, analogous to a highly trained animal.

Key Terms
غاية علمه (ghāyat ʿilmihi): The extreme or highest level of the dog's trained knowledge, evidenced by its discernment between halal and haram for the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the dog shows advanced training by preserving the meat for the owner
C) Because the dog is not considered to have touched the game
D) Because the game is already dead

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a trained dog's catch remains halal if the dog drinks the blood but does not eat the meat, because this behavior proves the dog's extreme training (ghāyat ʿilmihi).
