Falcon That Escapes Then Hunts Is Not Halal
Explains falcon's trained status and ruling when it escapes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a falcon that escapes and then hunts, and explain why it is considered ignorant (jahil) using the analogy to a dog that eats from the prey.

Core Idea
A falcon that escapes and hunts without returning is ignorant; its catch is haram.

When a falcon escapes and later hunts without returning to its owner, its status changes from trained (ʿālim) to ignorant (jāhil). This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that such prey is not halal, the proof from analogy, and the objection regarding conflicting ijtihads.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a falcon escaping, staying away, then hunting. The ruling is that its catch is not halal (haram).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is that the falcon abandoned its trained nature by not returning and by eating prey, showing ignorance. This is analogous to a dog that eats from the prey, which also renders the prey unlawful.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The falcon's failure to return and its consumption of prey indicate that it was never truly trained; its earlier restraint was due to disinterest, not knowledge. Thus, it is jāhil, and its catch is invalid.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (objection) and Hanafi response
Some argue that the first ijtihad (training) should persist. The Hanafi response: the objective is acquisition (iḥrāz), which was not achieved because the falcon did not return; moreover, eating proves ignorance from the start.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Imam Abu Hanifa’s position is relied upon: the prey is not halal. This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

. شکرہ کب جاہل سمجھا جائے گا
وان صقر أفر من صاحبه فمكث حينا ثم صاد لا يوكل صيده لانه ترك ما صار به عالما فيحكم بجهله كالكلب اذا اكل من الصيد
ترجمہ اور اگر شکرہ اپنے مالک کے پاس سے بھاگا پس تھوڑی دیر ٹھبرا پھر اس نے شکار کیا تو اس کا شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس کئے کہ اس نے وہ چیز چھوڑ دی جس کی وجہ سے وہ عالم ہوتا ہے تو اس کی جہالت کا حکم دیا جائے گا جیسے کتا جبکہ وہ شکار میں سے کھالے۔ تشریح شکرہ اپنے مالک کے ہاتھ سے اڑ گیا اور ہر چند مالک نے بلا یا مگر وہ نہیں آیا تو اب اس کی جہالت کا حکم دیا جائے گالہذا اگر اس نے اب کوئی شکار مارا تو وہ حلال نہ ہو گا جس کا تفصیلی بیان گزر چکا ہے۔
Also, one ijtihad does not invalidate another ijtihad; the ruling of permissibility had already been issued for the first hunting, so it is not nullified by a second ijtihad. Because the objective is acquisition (ihraz), and this was achieved by the first ijtihad. As for the hunter who has not gained control, it is still prey, and the objective has not been achieved, so out of caution it is declared unlawful. Imam Abu Hanifa replied: When he ate, that is evidence that he was ignorant from the beginning, and skill is never good. So his eating proves that his previous avoidance was not due to knowledge but to aversion. As for the matter of ijtihad, the answer is that if ijtihad changes before the objective is achieved, the first ijtihad's ruling is nullified, like a judge's ijtihad before passing judgment. The objective of hunting is eating, which has not been achieved. The falcon is considered ignorant: If a falcon escaped from its owner, stayed a while, then caught prey, its prey is not eaten because it abandoned that by which it was knowledgeable, so it is judged ignorant, like a dog when it eats from the prey. Translation: And if a falcon escaped from its owner, stayed briefly, then hunted, its prey is not eaten because it left that by which it was knowledgeable, so it is judged ignorant, like a dog when it eats from the prey. Explanation: The falcon flew from the owner's hand, and even if called, it did not come, so it is ruled ignorant; if it kills game, it is not lawful, as detailed earlier.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing an objection regarding conflicting ijtihads (independent reasoning) in hunting. The commentator clarifies that if a falcon was trained and then escaped, its first successful hunt might be considered permissible under its initial training. However, the objection suggests that a subsequent ijtihad (change in the falcon's state) should nullify the earlier permissibility. The response, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, argues that the falcon's eating of the prey after escaping demonstrates that it was actually ignorant from the start—its earlier restraint was not due to training but to disinterest. Therefore, the falcon is analogous to a dog that eats the prey, which renders the prey unlawful. The commentary then presents the core ruling from the Hidayah: if a falcon escapes and later hunts without responding to its owner, its catch is not halal. The ruling is based on the principle that the falcon lost its trained status (علم) by abandoning its owner, just as a dog that eats from the prey is considered ignorant (جاهل). The passage underscores that the objective of hunting is acquisition, and if the falcon fails to return to the owner after the hunt, it is treated as an untrained bird.

Key Terms
صقر (saqr): A falcon used for hunting; a trained bird of prey.
جاهل (jahil): Ignorant; an animal (falcon or dog) that loses its trained status and is not reliable for lawful hunting.
اجتہاد (ijtihad): Independent reasoning; in this context, the falcon's learned ability to hunt and return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the falcon did not respond to its owner's call.
B) Because the falcon ate from the prey, showing ignorance.
C) Because the owner did not witness the hunting.
D) Because the falcon is a wild bird.

Key Takeaway
A falcon that escapes and hunts without returning is considered ignorant, and its catch is not halal.

Dog Drinks Blood of Prey – Ruling on Halal
Ruling that prey remains halal when dog only drinks blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a trained dog drinks the blood of the prey but does not eat its meat, and explain why this confirms the dog's training.

Core Idea
Prey is halal if the dog avoids the meat, even after tasting blood—this proves superior training.

This lesson addresses a common hunting scenario: a trained dog catches prey, drinks its blood, but leaves the meat untouched. Is the prey still permissible? The Hanafi school rules clearly: yes, and the dog's self-restraint actually strengthens the case for its reliability. We will examine the ruling, its reasoning, and the key concept of 'ilm (training) in hunting dogs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the specific facts: a trained hunting dog catches prey, consumes only the blood, and does not eat any of the flesh.
This is distinct from cases where the dog eats part of the meat.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is clear: the prey is halal (permissible) to eat. The dog's action does not render the prey unlawful.
The ruling is based on the dog's training, not the consumption of blood.
3
Analyze the reasoning (dalil)
The dog's choice to drink the impure blood while preserving the pure meat confirms it is mumsik (a holder) for its owner. This is a sign of advanced 'ilm (training): the dog knows what is haram for the owner and leaves it aside.
The reasoning is aqli (rational): the action proves the dog's reliability.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
If the dog eats any part of the meat, the prey becomes haram because the dog has consumed the owner's portion. But blood-drinking does not break the training.
This distinction is key in Hanafi fiqh of hunting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شرب الكلب من دم الصيد ولم ياكل منه اكل لانه ممسك للصيد عليه وهذا من غاية علمه حيث شرب مالا يصلح لصاحبه و امسك عليه ما يصلح له
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے نے شکار کا خون پی لیا اور شکار میں سے کچھ نہیں کھایا تو اس کو کھایا جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ کتا شکار کو مالک کے لئے روکنے والا اور یہ کتے کی انتہائی علم کی بات ہے کہ جو چیز اس کے مالک کیلئے درست نہیں ہے اس کو پی لیا اور جو چیز اس کے لئے صالح ہے اس کو اس کے لئے روک لیا۔ تشریح کتے نے فقط شکار کا خون پی لیا اور گوشت نہیں کھایا تو وہ شکار حلال ہے کیونکہ اس سے تو کتے کی مزید مہارت معلوم ہوتی ہے اور یہ اس کی اعلیٰ تعلیم کی علامت ہے کہ جو چیز مالک کیلئے حلال ہے اس کو روک لیا اور جو مالک کے لئے حرام اس کو پی لیا۔
And if the dog drinks from the blood of the prey and does not eat from it, it is eaten (halal) because it is a holder of the prey for its owner, and this is from the utmost of its knowledge, as it drank what is not suitable for its owner and held back for him what is suitable. Translation: And if the dog drank the blood of the prey and did not eat anything from the prey, then it is eaten. Because the dog is the restrainer of the prey for the owner, and this is extreme knowledge of the dog: it drank what is not permissible for its owner and held back what is permissible for him. Explanation: The dog only drank the blood and did not eat the meat; so the prey is halal because this shows the dog's superior training, and it is a sign of its high education: it held back what is halal for the owner and drank what is haram for him.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling: if a trained dog catches prey but only drinks its blood without eating the flesh, the prey is halal. The reasoning is that the dog's action demonstrates advanced training—it distinguishes between what is permissible for its owner (the meat) and what is not (the blood). The dog's knowledge (علم) is manifested by preserving the halal part for the owner and consuming only the impure part. This reinforces the principle that the dog's reliability ('being a teacher'/معلم) is based on its self-control and its ability to refrain from eating the meat, which is the owner's portion.

Key Terms
دم الصيد (dam al-sayd): The blood of the hunted prey; considered impure and not suitable for consumption.
علم ('ilm): Knowledge or training of the hunting dog; its ability to obey commands and refrain from eating the prey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A trained dog's consumption of blood alone does not render prey haram; the meat remains halal because the dog's self-restraint confirms its 'ilm.

When the Dog Eats After the Owner's Possession: Prey Remains Halal
Ruling on the effect of dog eating after owner's possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of prey when the dog eats after the owner's possession (ihraz) and explain the qiyas used.

Core Idea
After ihraz, the dog's eating does not invalidate the prey; it is like eating from other property.

In hunting with a trained dog, the key condition is that the dog must not eat from the prey before the owner takes possession. But what happens if the dog eats after the prey is already in the owner's hands? This lesson clarifies that such eating does not affect the permissibility of the remaining prey, and the reasoning is based on a clear analogy (qiyas jaliy) to feeding the dog ordinary food.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: (1) the owner cuts a piece from the prey and feeds it to the dog, and (2) the dog snatches the prey from the owner and eats from it. Both occur after the owner has taken possession (ihraz).
2
Recognize the key condition
For a trained dog (al-kalb al-mu'allam), the condition is that it does not eat from the prey before the owner controls it. Once ihraz occurs, the prey is no longer in the 'prey state' (jihah al-saydiyyah) from the dog's perspective.
3
Analyze the qiyas for feeding a piece
The text likens the owner cutting a piece and feeding it to the dog to throwing any other food to the dog. Since the piece is now owned property (like other food), the dog's eating it does not break its training or affect the permissibility of the remaining prey.
The original 'prey' aspect is severed by the owner's act of cutting.
4
Apply the qiyas to the dog snatching
The dog snatching from the owner and eating is analogized to a dog tearing its owner's sheep. In both cases, the animal is already owned property, so the dog's eating does not violate the condition of not eating from the prey.
This shows that the prohibition is only about eating before ihraz, not after.
5
Contrast with the case before ihraz
If the dog eats from the prey before the owner takes possession, the prey is not halal because the 'aspect of being prey' (jihah al-saydiyyah) remains, and the dog's eating indicates it is not fully trained.
This distinguishes the two scenarios and reinforces the importance of ihraz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اخذا الصيد من المعلم ثم قطع منه قطعة والقاها اليه فاكلها يوكل ما بقى لانه لم يبق صيدا فصار كما اذا القى اليه طعاما غيره وكذا اذا وثب الكلب فاخذه منه واكل منه لانه ما اكل من الصيد والشرط ترك الاكل من الصيد فصار كما اذا فترى شاته بخلاف ما اذا فعل ذلك قبل ان يحرزه المالك لانه بقيت فيه جهة الصيدية
ترجمہ اور اگر شکاری نے کلب معلم کے شکار کو لے لیا پھر اس نے شکار میں سے ایک ٹکڑا کاٹا اور اس کو کتے کے سامنے ڈال یا پس اس نے اس کو کھالیا تو مابقی کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ وہ شکار باقی نہیں رہا تو ایسا ہو گیا جبکہ اس نے اس کی طرف کوئی اور کھا نا ڈالا ہو اور ایسے ہی جبکہ کتے نے چھلانگ لگائی پس شکار کو مالک سے لے لیا اور اس میں سے کھا لیا ( تو یہ حلال ہے ) اس لئے کہ اس نے شکار میں سے نہیں کھایا اور شرط شکار میں سے کھانے کو چھوڑ نا تھا پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے جبکہ کتا ما لک کی بکری کو پھاڑ ڈالے بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ کتے نے یہ کام مالک کے احراز سے پہلے کیا ہو اس لئے کہ شکار میں شکار ہونے کی جہت باقی ہے۔
And if he takes the prey from the trained dog, then cuts a piece from it and throws it to him, and he eats it, the remainder is eaten because it is no longer prey, so it becomes as if he threw other food to him. Similarly, if the dog jumps and takes it from him and eats from it, because he did not eat from the prey, and the condition is refraining from eating from the prey, so it becomes as if he tore apart his sheep. Unlike if he does that before the owner takes possession (ihraz), because the aspect of being prey remains in it. Translation: And if the hunter took the prey from the trained dog, then cut a piece from it and placed it before the dog, so he ate it, then the remainder is eaten because it is no longer prey, so it is like when he threw other food to him. Similarly, if the dog jumped and took the prey from the owner and ate from it (it is halal), because he did not eat from the prey, and the condition was refraining from eating from the prey, so it is like when the dog tears apart the owner's sheep. Unlike the case when he did that before the owner's possession, because the aspect of being prey remains.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage covers multiple scenarios after the prey is acquired by the owner (ihraz). The first scenario: the owner takes the prey from the dog, cuts a piece, and feeds it to the dog; the dog eats it. The remaining prey is halal because the dog did not eat from the prey itself but from a piece that is no longer considered prey. It is analogous to the owner feeding the dog any other food. The second scenario: the dog jumps and snatches the prey from the owner's hand and eats from it. This is also halal because the dog did not eat from the prey while it was still in the state of being hunted; rather, it ate after the owner had already taken possession. The condition for the dog's training (being 'alim) is that it does not eat from the prey before it is acquired by the owner. Once the owner takes possession, the prey is no longer in the category of 'prey' for the dog, so the dog's eating does not break its training. The ruling is analogized to a dog that tears apart its owner's sheep—it does not affect the permissibility of the sheep. However, if the dog does these actions before the owner takes possession (i.e., while the prey is still in the wild or before the owner grabs it), then the prey is not halal because the aspect of being prey remains, and the dog's eating indicates ignorance.

Key Terms
احراز (ihraz): Taking possession; the owner physically acquiring the prey, which changes its legal status.
جهة الصيديه (jihah al-saydiyyah): The aspect of being prey; the state before the owner takes possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a dog eating from the prey NOT affect the permissibility of the prey?
A) When the dog eats before the owner controls the prey.
B) When the dog eats after the owner has taken possession of the prey.
C) When the dog eats the whole prey.
D) When the dog is untrained.

Key Takeaway
The dog's eating after the owner takes possession (ihraz) does not affect the permissibility of the prey, as it is no longer in the legal category of 'prey'.

beginner
6 min
Dog Bites and Eats During Chase: Prey Not Halal
Ruling on a dog that eats part of prey during chase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on prey killed by a dog that ate from it during the chase and identify the condition that renders the dog 'jahil'.

Core Idea
If a hunting dog eats any part of the prey during pursuit, its entire catch becomes haram.

Hunting with trained dogs is permitted in Islam, but a key sign of training (ta'lim) is that the dog does not eat from the prey until its owner takes possession. This passage examines a scenario where a dog bites and eats a piece of meat from the prey during the chase, then later catches and kills the same prey without eating again. The ruling clarifies that the dog's initial consumption is sufficient to classify it as ignorant (jahil), rendering the entire prey impermissible, even though it did not eat from the final kill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The dog bites a piece of meat from the prey during the chase, swallows it, then continues, catches, and kills the prey without eating further.
The dog's action of eating is key, not just biting.
2
Determine the ruling
The prey is not halal (haram) and cannot be eaten.
The ruling is clear: 'لم يوكل' (it is not eaten).
3
Find the legal basis
The dog's act of eating during the chase proves it is 'kalb jahil' (ignorant/untrained). The catch of an ignorant dog is impermissible.
This is based on the condition that a trained dog refrains from eating from the prey.
4
Understand the condition of training
A trained dog (mu'allam) must not eat any part of the prey until the hunter takes possession. Eating at any point indicates lack of control.
The Quranic permission (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:4) for hunting dogs is contingent on training.
5
Apply to variations
If the dog eats only after killing, some scholars allow it. But during chase, eating breaks training definitively.
This scenario is clear-cut; the dog's ignorance is established by the first bite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نهس الصيد فقطع منه بضعة فاكلها ثم ادرك الصيد فقتله ولم ياكل منه لم يوكل لانه صيد كلب جاهل حيث اكل من الصيد
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے نے شکار کو نو چا ہی کاٹ لیا پس اس کو کھا لیا پھر اس نے شکار کو پکڑ لیا پس اس کو مار ڈالا اور اس میں سے نہیں کھایا تو وہ شکار کھایا نہیں جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ جاہل کتے کا شکار ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ اس نے شکار میں سے کھا لیا۔ تشریح کتا شکار کے پیچھے دوڑا اور کتے نے شکار پر منہ مارا جس سے شکار کے گوشت کا ایک ٹکڑا بدن سے الگ ہو گیا یہ کتا اس ٹکڑے کو کھانے لگا اور کھانے سے فارغ ہو کر پھر شکار کے پیچھے دوڑا اور اس کو پکڑ کر مار ڈالا اور اس میں سے کچھ نہیں کھایا تو وہ شکار کھایا نہیں جائے گا کیونکہ جب کتا شکار کو کھانے میں لگ گیا تو معلوم ہوا کہ یہ جاہل کتا ہے اور یہ معلوم ہو چکا کہ جاہل کتے کا مارا ہوا شکار حلال نہیں ہے۔
And if the dog bites the prey, cuts a piece from it and eats it, then catches the prey, kills it, and does not eat from it, it is not eaten because it is the prey of an ignorant dog, since it ate from the prey. Translation: And if the dog bit the prey, tore a piece from it and ate it, then caught the prey, killed it, and did not eat from it, that prey is not eaten because it is the prey of an ignorant dog, given that it ate from the prey. Explanation: The dog chased the prey, bit it, tore a piece of meat from its body, ate that piece, then after finishing chased the prey again, caught it and killed it without eating again. That prey is not eaten because when the dog started eating the prey, it became clear that it is an ignorant dog, and it is known that the prey killed by an ignorant dog is not halal.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a dog bites the prey during the chase, tears off and eats a piece of meat, then later catches and kills the same prey without eating further. The ruling is that the prey is not halal. The reasoning is that the dog's act of eating from the prey—even before the final capture—classifies it as an ignorant (جاهل) dog. The condition for a dog's training is that it refrains from eating from the prey entirely until the owner takes possession. Once the dog eats any part during the pursuit, it demonstrates a lack of control, and its subsequent catch is not considered the result of trained hunting. This applies even if the dog does not eat from the prey after killing it. The commentary clarifies that the dog's initial bite and consumption of a piece is sufficient to establish ignorance, rendering the entire catch impermissible.

Key Terms
نهس (nahasa): To bite or tear; the dog's action of biting the prey during pursuit.
كلب جاهل (kalb jahil): An ignorant/un-trained dog; one that eats from the prey during the hunt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dog bites a piece from a prey during the chase, eats it, then later catches and kills the prey without eating again. What is the ruling?
A) The prey is halal because the dog did not eat after killing.
B) The prey is haram because the dog ate during the chase.
C) The prey is makruh but can be eaten.
D) The prey is halal because the dog eventually killed it.

Key Takeaway
A hunting dog must not eat any part of the prey during the chase; if it does, the prey is haram regardless of later actions.
